## Page 5

ABSTRACT This report presents the substantive findings and management recommendations for the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) eligibility evaluations of two prehistoric archeological sites within the MacArthur Reclamation project area in the City of Grand Prairie, Dallas County, Texas. This investigation was conducted by Integrated Environmental Solutions LLC (IES), under contract to MacArthur Boulevard Property Account, LLC, and BSREP III 2003 MacArthur, LP. The proposed project requires the reclamation of approximately 37 acres (ac) of the West Fork Trinity River floodplain to support the property development for a contracted buyer and specific warehouse tenants. To develop the property, the project will require a Section 404 of the Clean Water Act Individual Permit from the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers. Therefore, the project is subject to the provisions of the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA; 54 U.S. Code [U.S.C] 300101 et seq.). The primary goal of these investigations was to provide NRHP eligibility evaluations of the two previously recorded archeological sites (41DL553 and 41DL554) within the proposed project area. These investigations included mechanical excavation of site delineation and exploratory trenches and the manual excavation of 1-by-1-meter (m) controlled excavation units. At 41DL553, 11 Gradall trenches were excavated to fully delineate the site and to facilitate the excavation of eight 1-by-1-m test units. Nine Gradall trenches and eight 1-by-1-m test units were excavated at 41DL554. During these investigations, 14.4 cubic meters (m3) of sediment at 41DL553 and 12.8 m3 of sediment at 41DL554 were excavated, deflocculated, and water screened. In addition, soil column samples were collected from 41DL553 and 41DL554 that were deflocculated and processed via flotation for macrobotanical analysis. Through these methods, five chipped stone tools, 479 pieces of debitage, 4.9 kilograms (kg) of burned rock, and 451 faunal bone fragments were recovered from 41DL553. Similarly, seven chipped stone tools, 345 pieces of chipped stone debitage, 5.3 kg of burned rock, and 172 faunal bone fragments were recovered from 41DL554. During these investigations, one prehistoric cultural feature was identified at 41DL553 and two prehistoric cultural features were identified at 41DL554. The fieldwork for this effort was conducted by IES from 28 September through 06 November 2020. Christopher Goodmaster served as the Principal Investigator for the project and was supported by Thomas Chapman (Project Archeologist/Lithic Analyst), Karl Kibler (Geoarcheologist), Leslie Bush, Ph.D. (Macrobotanical Analyst), Jodi Jacobson, Ph.D., and James Ramsey (Faunal Analysts). Based on the findings of this cultural resources survey, IES recommends that both 41DL553 and 41DL554 be considered not eligible for listing in the NRHP. This technical report conforms to the Council of Texas Archeologists (CTA) guidelines for cultural resources management reports. All records generated by this project will be permanently stored at the IES office in McKinney, Texas. All artifacts collected were temporarily stored at IES office, or IES subconsultant offices, and will be returned to the landowner after completion of the Section 106 of the NHPA process. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page i

## Page 13

CHAPTER 1: PROJECT DESCRIPTION This report presents the results of National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) eligibility evaluations of two prehistoric archeological sites (41DL553 and 41DL554) located within the MacArthur Reclamation project area in the City of Grand Prairie, Dallas County, Texas (Figure 1.1). This investigation was conducted by Integrated Environmental Solutions, LLC (IES), under contract to MacArthur Boulevard Property Account, LLC, and BSREP III 2003 MacArthur, LP. This report documents the fieldwork conducted and provides the NRHP recommendations for both of the archeological sites evaluated. A description of the proposed project area, environmental and historical contexts, field and analytical methods, results of the investigations, and recommendations regarding the evaluated archeological sites are provided in this document. 1.1 Regulatory Framework The proposed project requires compliance with Section 404 of the Clean Water Act (CWA) Individual Permit (IP) from the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers (USACE). Therefore, the project is subject to the provisions of the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA; 54 U.S. Code [U.S.C.] 300101 et seq.). The NHPA, specifically Section 106 (54 U.S.C. 306108), requires the State Historic Preservation Officer (SHPO), an official appointed in each state or territory, to administer and coordinate historic preservation activities, and to review and comment on all actions licensed or permitted by the federal government that will have an effect on properties listed in the NRHP, or eligible for such listing. Federal actions include, but are not limited to, construction, rehabilitation, repair projects, demolition, licenses, permits, loans, loan guarantees, grants, and federal property transfers. As the proposed project requires federal permitting by the USACE, it is subject to the provisions of the NHPA. Per 36 Code of Federal Regulations (CFR) 800, the federal agency responsible for overseeing the action must make a reasonable and good faith effort to identify, evaluate, and mitigate cultural resources. This effort was conducted in accordance with 33 CFR 325, Appendices B and C, which establish the procedures followed by the USACE to fulfill the requirements set forth in National Environmental Policy Act of 1969 (NEPA), NHPA, and other applicable historic preservation laws as they relate to the USACE regulatory program. 1.2 Reporting Conventions Standards for archeological methods require that measurements be recorded in metric units. For this reason, while general distances and engineering specifications are described in imperial units (e.g., inch [in], foot [ft], mile [mi], acre [ac]) within this report, archeological measurements and observations are reported in metric units (e.g., centimeter [cm], meter [m], kilometer [km], hectare [ha]), unless historic-period artifacts are more appropriately recorded in imperial units. 1.3 Nature of the Undertaking The project, as proposed, encompasses a 66.25-ac area and will involve the reclamation of approximately 37 ac of the West Fork Trinity River floodplain to support the property development for a contracted buyer and specific warehouse tenants. Through the floodplain reclamation, 8 to 12 ft of fill will be placed across the eastern half of the project area for the construction of a proposed warehouse and associated infrastructure. During the Phase I survey component of this project, 41DL553 and 41DL554 were the only archeological sites encountered within the Area of Potential Effects (APE) for the Section 404 of the CWA IP, which encompassed the entire 66.25-ac project area (Figure 1.2). The placement of fill associated with the proposed construction will be the sole impact to sites 41DL553 and 41DL554. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 1

## Page 17

CHAPTER 2: ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND 2.1 Modern Physical Environment and Hydrology Sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 are located within the upper Trinity River basin of North-Central Texas. Locally, the project area occupies one of the few remaining undeveloped portions of the Trinity River valley in the Dallas/Fort Worth metropolitan area and has avoided the sand and gravel quarrying activities that were common in the vicinity during the early to mid-20th century. Situated within the meander belt of the West Fork Trinity River floodplain, the project area is located approximately 0.3 mi west of the confluence of Bear Creek and the West Fork Trinity River (Figure 2.1). The West Fork Trinity River floodplain occupies an elevation range of approximately 426 ft (130 m) above modern sea level in the vicinity of the sites. As with other major streams within the upper Trinity River basin, the West Fork Trinity River represents a dynamic hydrological environment. Channel migration and avulsions have occurred at a relatively high frequency within floodplains across the upper Trinity River basin throughout the Holocene. As a result, former channel locations and numerous channel meander scars are discernable within the project area on a variety of historical maps, aerial photographs, and modern satellite and remote sensing imagery. Early 20th- century maps of the West Fork Trinity River valley depict the floodplain environment with varying degrees of precision and accuracy. The 1893 U.S. Geological Survey (USGS) topographic quadrangle depicts the West Fork Trinity River channel flowing west and north of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 (Figure 2.2). That depiction of the channel carried over to later editions of the map printed in 1902, 1909, 1927, and 1949. Oddly enough, maps and aerial photographs contemporary to the later editions of the 1893 USGS quadrangle show the channel flowing at or near its current location. It is possible that the depiction of the channel relative to the sites on the 1893 USGS map is inaccurate and was never corrected or revised in later editions of the map. It is also possible, however, that the earlier depiction of the channel is correct and, sometime in the early 20th century, the channel shifted through avulsion and the later editions of the USGS maps were never revised to reflect the avulsion, while other maps of the period did. In the latter scenario, the lower reaches of Bear Creek, above its confluence with the West Fork Trinity River, could be the 1893 West Fork Trinity channel, giving some credence to an early 20th century avulsion event that shifted the channel to the south and east of the sites. The earliest reliable map of the vicinity surrounding the project area and sites 41DL553 and 41DL554, produced by the U.S. Department of Agriculture (USDA) in 1920, depicts the sinuous character of the West Fork Trinity River, as well as an abandoned meander channel cut-off as an oxbow lake (Horseshoe Lake), and a network of dendritic tributary channels (primarily Bear Creek) draining the surrounding uplands (Figure 2.3). Frequent and catastrophic flooding events during the early 20th century prompted the implementation of extensive flood control measures, including channelization and levee construction, throughout much of the surrounding metropolitan area. A more detailed map, created by the USACE in 1925, better illustrates the West Fork Trinity River floodplain environment and includes the locations of artificial levees constructed to ameliorate flooding (Figure 2.4). Also depicted within the 1925 USACE map are locations of multiple aggregate (primarily sand and gravel) quarrying operations that would drastically expand during the mid-20th century. Despite urban and suburban development within the region, the area encompassing the project area and sites 41DL553 and 41DL544 remained relatively free of large- scale development impacts during that time. As recorded on a 1945 USDA aerial photograph, the property encompassing the sites retained vestiges of the complex floodplain geomorphology characteristic of the area, including numerous curvilinear in-filled abandoned (relic) channel meander scars that likely served as flood chutes and overflow channels (Figure 2.5). During the latter half of the 20th century, modern agricultural practices, likely through extensive plowing in an attempt to improve the natural drainage of the landform for management of the property as pasture, served to obliterate the surface expression of much of the floodplain geomorphology (Figure 2.6). Although the current floodplain surface is relatively level, modern Light Detection and Ranging (LiDAR) topographic data indicate the presence of numerous, subtle MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 5

## Page 24

swales at the former locations of the relic channel meander scars, many of which now support emergent wetlands (Figure 2.7). Analysis of the LiDAR data indicates that both sites occupy slightly elevated portions of the floodplain immediately adjacent to shallow swales that flank portions of both sites (Figure 2.8). Those geomorphic features probably represent abandoned and in-filled channel segments of the West Fork Trinity River that now act as chute or overflow channels during flood events and intermittent wetlands when not accommodating channelized flows. 2.2 Physiography and Geology The upper Trinity River basin includes three physiographic zones generally extending from east to west: the Blackland Prairie, the Eastern Cross Timbers, and the Grand Prairie (see Yates and Ferring 1986:12– 31). Sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 are located within the Northern Blackland Prairie subregion of the Texas Blackland Prairie ecoregion (Figure 2.9). This region is distinguished from surrounding regions by gently undulating plains underlain by Cretaceous-age carbonate rocks, marls, and mudstones and primarily mantled by dark, fine-textured, vertic soils that once supported extensive tallgrass prairie vegetation (Griffith et al. 2007). The Northern Blackland Prairie landscape is incised by numerous stream valleys that host Quaternary-age alluvial landforms (Brown et al. 1983; McGowen et al. 1972, 1991; Proctor et al. 1970, 1974; USGS 2020; Figure 2.10). The West Fork Trinity River floodplain at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 is up to 1.3 km wide and stands about 8 m above the channel. The floodplain is flanked by older alluvial terraces inset within the valley walls defined by the outcrop of the upper Cretaceous-age Eagle Ford Formation (McGowen et al. 1972). 2.3 Soils The floodplain surface encompassing sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 is mantled by two soil map units, Frio silty clay, 0 to 1 percent slopes, occasionally flooded, and Frio silty clay, 0 to 1 percent slopes, frequently flooded (Coffee et al. 1980; Web Soil Survey 2019; Figure 2.11). Soils of the Frio series are classified as Cumulic Haplustolls and are described as very deep and well-drained soils that typically form in calcareous loamy and clayey Holocene alluvium (National Cooperative Soil Survey 2017). Frio series soils are comprised of dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) clay or silty clay and commonly contain a series of A and Bk (calcic) horizons that extend to depths of 80 in (203 cm) or greater (Coffee et al. 1980). The late Quaternary alluvial stratigraphy of the upper Trinity River basin has been previously described by Ferring (1986, 1997) in which he identified seven allostratigraphic units, four of which typically comprise the floodplain alluvium of the larger streams within the basin. Ferring designated the four floodplain alluvial units, from oldest to most recent, the Aubrey, Sanger, Pilot Point, and Recent alluviums. The Aubrey alluvium was deposited between 14,000 and 11,000 years before present (B.P.) and rests unconformably on bedrock with an erosional upper boundary (Ferring 1997:35). Above the Aubrey alluvium is the Sanger unit, which was deposited between 11,000 and 7,500 B.P. (Ferring 1997:37). The upper boundary of the Sanger alluvium is marked by a soil, which is in contact with the overlying Pilot Point unit. Citing a suite of 32 radiocarbon ages, Ferring (1994:51–52) suggested the Pilot Point alluvium was deposited during the late Holocene between approximately 4,500 and 500 years ago. The Pilot Point alluvium is subaerially exposed on the floodplain (Ferring 1997:37). However, along modern channels, the Pilot Point alluvium is often buried by a relatively thin (≤ 5 ft [1.5 m]) mantle of more recent alluvium. Ferring (1997:38) referred to this latter unit as the Recent alluvium. The upper part of the Pilot Point unit is marked by a cumulic soil or paleosol, depending on stratigraphic and geomorphic context, that Ferring named the West Fork. It is a prominent soil-geomorphic feature within the upper Trinity River basin (see Ferring 1986, 1997). The stratigraphic and geomorphic variability of the West Fork is reflected in the attributes of the soil itself, with a thick, cumulic version formed between 4,500 B.P. and present is imprinted on the floodplain surface, and a thinner paleosol along meander belts formed between 2,000 and 1,000 B.P. as stream channels laterally shifted (Ferring 1997:37, 2000:25). MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 12

## Page 30

Hall et al. (2012) have suggested that the formation of the West Fork soil occurred over a shorter time frame than what Ferring suggested for the surficial floodplain soil, but slightly longer than the meander belt paleosol. Based on radiocarbon ages obtained on the West Fork paleosol at site 41TR170 along the Clear Fork Trinity River, Hall et al. (2012:544) suggest the soil formed between 2,300 and 1,000 years ago. More specifically, Hall et al. (2012:548) argue that the West Fork represents a cumulic wet meadow floodplain Mollisol that ceased forming due to channel entrenchment spurred on by the onset of increasingly xeric conditions around 1,000 years ago. After channel entrenchment, sometime between 800 and 100 years ago, the West Fork soil was buried by the Recent alluvium. More recent work regarding the age of the West Fork soil by Frederick and McKee (2019:78–82) compiled 74 radiocarbon ages from West Fork soil and paleosol contexts at multiple sites and locales. They calibrated the ages and calculated an aggregate sum probability distribution. In aggregate, the 74 calibrated radiocarbon ages from the West Fork soil/paleosol returned a mean age and standard deviation of 1,531±159 B.P., spanning a period from around 4,000 to 250 years ago, with the majority of the dated contexts indicating the West Fork soil formed between 2,400 and 700 years ago. Their study, prompted by work at the Mercado site (41TR203) on the West Fork Trinity River upstream of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554, illustrates that the age of the West Fork soil varies greatly across the basin and also suggests that the Pilot Point alluvium, particularly the thicker, near-channel meander belt facies, can host multiple soils depending on local depositional factors. A complete geoarcheological analysis of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 is presented and discussed in Chapter 5. 2.4 Flora and Fauna The Blackland Prairie is the southernmost extension of the North American tallgrass prairie and is within the Texan biotic province (Blair 1950). This ecoregion has undergone profound alterations from its native state by historic period farming and ranching and by modern urban, suburban, and exurban development (cf. Bezanson 2001). After centuries of disturbance and decades of deliberate replacement of tree and grass species, only isolated examples of precontact and early historic vegetation communities remain today (Telfair 1999). Dominant vegetation across the Blackland Prairie once consisted of big bluestem (Andropogon gerardii gerardii), little bluestem (Schizachyrium scoparium), Indiangrass (Sorghastrum nutans), switchgrass (Panicum virgatum), brownseed paspalum (Paspalum plicatulum), and gramagrass (Bouteloua gracilis) in the uplands, and eastern cottonwood (Populus deltoides), bur oak (Quercus macrocarpa), Shumard oak (Q. shumardii), sycamore (Platanus occidentalis), bois d’arc (Maclura pomifera), black willow (Salix nigra), hackberry (Celtis occidentalis), elm (Ulmus sp.), pecan (Carya illinoinensis), and green ash (Fraxinus pennsylvanica) in bottomland forests and riparian forest corridors. Common forbs include aster (Aster sp.), prairie bluet (Coenagrion angulatum), prairie clover (Petalostemum purpureum), and black-eyed Susan (Rudbeckia hirta). The ecosystems within the Blackland Prairie along the West Fork Trinity River support a diverse biotic community, characterized by hydrophilic vegetation and numerous aquatic vertebrates and invertebrates that include a variety of mussel (Unionidae sp.) and numerous species of freshwater fish. Present-day mammal species consist primarily of cougar (Puma concolor), coyote (Canis latrans), white-tailed deer (Odocoileus virginianus), American badger (Taxidea taxus), American beaver (Castor canadensis), gray fox (Urocyon cinereoargenteus), red fox (Vulpes vulpes), muskrat (Ondatra zibethicus), Virginia opossum (Didelphis virginiana), raccoon (Procyon lotor), striped skunk (Mephitis mephitis), eastern cottontail rabbit (Sylvilagus floridanus), and fox squirrel (Sciurus niger). Mammals that once occupied the region include plains bison (Bison bison bison), gray (C. lupus) and red wolf (C. lupus rufus), black (Ursus americanus) and grizzly bear (U. arctos horribilis), and pronghorn (Antilocapra americana). Avian species include bobwhite quail (Colinus virginianus), mourning dove (Zenaida macroura), and a variety of songbirds and migratory waterfowl. Various reptiles and amphibians are also found throughout the area. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 18

## Page 31

2.5 Climate The modern regional climate is humid subtropical, with mild winters and hot summers. Daytime temperatures of greater than 100 degrees Fahrenheit (ºF; 37.8º Celsius [C]) are typical during the summer months while temperatures rarely fall below freezing during the winter. Average annual precipitation is 102 cm (40 in), which falls primarily as rainfall and typically in short periods of intense thunderstorms with peak precipitation in May and a nadir in August (Estaville and Earl 2008). The changing climatic and environmental conditions prior to and following the prehistoric cultural occupations at 41DL553 and 41DL554 provide context to the human-environmental relationships related to subsistence strategies of the indigenous populations that lived in the region. The comparative data for this area largely derive from paleoecological studies from surrounding regions (see Albert 1981; Bryant and Holloway 1985; Camper 1991; Toomey et al. 1993; Toomey and Stafford 1995; Bousman 1998). Since biotic responses and geologic records of climate shifts may not be equally reflected in the archeological record, there are implicit limitations of paleoenvironmental reconstruction (Story et al. 1990). Changes in paleoenvironmental conditions constitute only one component that explains cultural change. Nevertheless, in the ecotones that comprise the transitional woodland-prairie environment of North-Central Texas, response to moisture fluctuations is important in understanding human-environmental relationships (Peter and McGregor 1988). The glacial climate during the terminal Pleistocene, characterized by the extinction of Pleistocene megafauna, was cooler and possibly wetter than the early Holocene. The prairie-forest ecotone of this region was farther east than present and began shifting westward after approximately 12,000 B.P. (Ferring and Yates 1998). Prior research indicates that from about 10,000 to 9,000 B.P. the post-glacial landscape of the early Holocene is characterized by a general drying and warming trend punctuated by brief climatic fluctuations and increased seasonality (Dillehay 1974; Nickels 2014). The presence and absence of bison taxa in the archeological record provide a proxy for changes in annual precipitation (Prikryl 1990), and that “overall human occupation potentials should have been better during periods with increased precipitation and more common bison as well” (Ferring and Yates 1998:26). Prior research regarding climatic shifts during the middle Holocene (7,000 to 4,000 B.P.) indicates an interglacial maximum across the northern latitudes. However, the Gulf Coast Plain region was likely unaffected by a broad-scale warming and drying trend, typically referred to as the Hypsithermal or Altithermal period, that effected other regions (Prikryl 1990; Story et al. 1990). Compared to the early and late Holocene, the middle Holocene climate may have been drier in the North-Central Texas region (Ferring and Yates 1998). The environment of the Post Oak Savanna in East Central Texas, adjacent to the Blackland Prairie, may have been established approximately 3,000 B.P., a period characterized by increasing land use by Native Americans due to an abundance in natural resources during the late Holocene (Perttula 2004:347). Prior to historic settlement, streams and drainages probably provided greater abundance of flora and fauna. Current practices such as grazing, vegetation clearing, and modern agriculture contributed to landscape changes that have resulted in isolated biotic communities (Telfair 1999; Nickels 2014). The transition from the middle Holocene to late Holocene is characterized by cooler, possibly more mesic, conditions on the Southern Plains, as well as the Pilot Point alluvium deposition associated with the late Holocene in the upper Trinity River (Ferring 1990). The consensus among researchers is that the late Holocene in North-Central Texas was generally marked by periods of wet-dry cycles, but there is less consensus among the specific time intervals of those cycles. Some research suggests a dry period beginning sometime between 2,000 B.P. and 1,000 B.P. (Peter and Harrison 2011:12). In addition, the formation of the West Fork paleosol indicates a period of slower sedimentation (Shipp et al. 2019) and, therefore, a period of stability and more favorable conditions for floodplain habitation. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 19

CHAPTER 3: CULTURAL BACKGROUND 3.1 Cultural Context 3.1.1 - Native American Cultural Chronology Sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 are located in what is generally referred to as the North-Central Texas cultural area, which encompasses northern portions of the Blackland Prairie and the adjacent Cross Timbers physiographic regions (Perttula 2004). The following section presents a brief synopsis, based on regional archeology, of the Native American cultural chronology of the North-Central Texas cultural area. Given the size of the region and the depth and breadth of cultural complexity that has developed in the area, this section must necessarily be limited in scope. As noted by Peter and McGregor (1988:27–29), many of the previous archeological excavation efforts within the upper Trinity River basin have primarily focused on reservoir development, especially along the Elm Fork Trinity River (Crook and Harris 1957, 1958, 1961; Skinner and Baird 1985; Skinner et al. 1982a, 1982b) and the East Fork Trinity River (Dawson and Sullivan 1973; Lorrain and Hoffrichter 1968; Lynott 1975; Ross 1966). Although the overall chronological framework for the upper Trinity River basin is not well developed, the available data allow a generalized prehistoric cultural chronology. Current conceptions of the prehistoric cultural chronology of North-Central Texas, particularly within the upper Trinity River basin (Table 1), are largely based on four major reports by Peter and McGregor (1988), Prikryl (1987, 1990), and Yates and Ferring (1986). Additional references are provided to supplement the information summarized within the following sections. Table 3.1: Generalized Native American Cultural Chronology for North-Central Texas* Period Date Ranges (B.C. /A.D.) Paleoindian 10,000 B.C.–7,000 B.C. Archaic 7,000 B.C.– A.D. 700 Early Archaic 7,000 B.C.–4,000 B.C. Middle Archaic 4,000 B.C.–2,000 B.C. Late Archaic 2,000 B.C.–A.D. 700 Late Prehistoric A.D. 700–1600 Late Prehistoric I A.D. 700–1200 Late Prehistoric II A.D. 1200– ca. 1542 Protohistoric ca. A.D. 1542–1800 Historic Native American ca. A.D. 1800–1860 *after Peter and McGregor (1988), Prikryl (1987, 1990), and Yates and Ferring (1986) Paleoindian Period The Paleoindian occupation of the upper Trinity River basin is known primarily through diagnostic projectile points from surface collections or stratigraphically mixed contexts (Meltzer 1987; Meltzer and Bever 1995). The Field Ranch site (41CO10), along the upper Elm Fork Trinity River, is a good example of a typical Paleoindian site context (Jensen 1968). Projectile point types found in North-Central Texas that can be associated with the early through late parts of the Paleoindian period include Clovis, Folsom, Dalton, Plainview, San Patrice, and Scottsbluff. Clovis and Plainview style projectile points, temporally and culturally diagnostic artifacts of the Paleoindian period, have been recovered along both Denton Creek and Clear Creek in the Cross Timbers. Based on a sample of projectile points from surface contexts, Prikryl (1990) has concluded that among the most common Paleoindian point types in North-Central Texas are MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 21

## Page 34

Plainview and Dalton. Interestingly, the only two investigated sites in North-Central Texas with apparently discrete Paleoindian components, the Lewisville Lake (41DN72) and Aubrey (41DN479) sites, indicate occupation early in the Paleoindian period, both producing Clovis projectile points. Ferring and Yates (1997) suggest that those projectile point types date from approximately 10,000 to 12,000 years B.P., based on cross dating with sites in other regions. The suggested age for those projectile point types may correspond with the onset of early Holocene alluviation in the local river valleys, including the Trinity and Sabine River basins. Most of the recorded Paleoindian sites cluster in the upper Trinity River basin, where the most intensive archeological investigations have taken place, although most of the Paleoindian sites in the area have generally yielded no more than one or two diagnostic projectile points. The generally low frequency of Paleoindian sites and artifacts, coupled with the tendency for Paleoindian projectile point types to be made from nonlocal lithic raw materials, have led investigators to characterize these populations as highly mobile, with low regional population densities (Lynott 1981:100–101). Knowledge about the settlement-subsistence strategies used by these earliest occupants of the North-Central Texas region is extremely limited. Early research indicated that the Paleoindian lifestyle was dependent on hunting Late Pleistocene megafauna species, including mammoth, mastodon, bison, camel, and horse. However, more recent investigations at the Aubrey site, located on the Elm Fork Trinity River in Denton County, has indicated that subsistence efforts did not focus on “big game” animals alone; rather, the entire range of prairie and forest faunal species was used (Ferring 2001). The preponderance of small game species at the Aubrey site could be interpreted as representing a more generalized pattern of foraging than the reliance on mammoth and bison apparently demonstrated at other Paleoindian sites on the Southern Plains (Hofman 1989a:31–32). Such a divergence in subsistence patterns may reflect an inherent adaptability of Paleoindian technology to changing environmental conditions encountered as early indigenous populations spread southeastward into Texas (Ferring and Yates 1997). Whether the pattern of a more generalized foraging subsistence system is characteristic of Paleoindian adaptations in the Eastern Woodlands and the focus on now extinct, big game species is more characteristic of a Plains adaptation remains to be documented. The Lewisville Lake site contained 27 hearth features with an associated Clovis point and few other lithic artifacts (Crook and Harris 1957, 1958; Story 1990:182–184). Although the original radiocarbon dates of the hearths suggested an anomalously early age for Paleoindian occupation (ca. 37,000 years B.P.; Crook and Harris 1956), later analysis by the Smithsonian Institution (Shiley et al. 1985; Stanford 1981) appears to have resolved the controversy concerning the date of the occupation there. Apparently, the presence of naturally occurring lignite in the hearths, either as a fuel or as an inadvertent inclusion, contaminated the radiocarbon samples (Shiley et al. 1985). Based on a relatively recent synthesis of the Paleoindian period in Texas by Bousman and his collegues (2004), the oldest reliable radiocarbon assays associated with the Clovis culture are from the Aubrey site (11,542 +/-111 B.P. and 11,590 +/-93 B.P. [Ferring 2001]; more recently calibrated to 13,462 and 13,516 B.P. [Bousman et al. 2004]). Consequently, the generally-accepted age of 11,500 to 11,100 years B.P. is probably a reasonable estimate for the earliest Paleoindian period occupations in North-Central Texas (Bousman et al. 2004). There are no reported excavations of stratified Paleoindian site components on the central Blackland Prairie itself. Nonetheless, a variety of early projectile point styles have been found, largely in surface contexts, and it is clear that North-Central Texas was used throughout the Paleoindian period. Presumably, this use was by hunter-gatherer groups with low population densities and high residential mobility. The deeply- buried Aubrey site yielded lithic debitage and Clovis points buried beneath 8 m (26 ft) of Holocene alluvium on the Elm Fork Trinity River floodplain (Ferring 2001). The discovery of the Aubrey site suggests that well-preserved Paleoindian sites in North-Central Texas will only be found by examining deeply-stratified Holocene alluvium in floodplain contexts. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 22

## Page 35

Archaic Period In North-Central Texas, the Archaic period is generally dated to extend between ca. 7,000 B.C. and A.D. 700, with Early, Middle, and Late subdivisions of the period tentatively identified (Prikryl 1993:199). Thus, the Early Archaic has been dated from 7,000 to 4,000 B.C., the Middle Archaic from 4,000 to 2,000 B.C., and the Late Archaic from 2,000 B.C. to A.D. 700. Relatively recent overviews of the Archaic period in this portion of Texas include Hofman (1989a), Prikryl (1990), and Story (1985, 1990). Knowledge concerning the Archaic period in the upper Trinity River drainage is limited by the lack of data from major excavations. This is particularly true for the Early and Middle Archaic periods. Mixing of archeological site components also has hampered interpretations regarding downstream Trinity River use by prehistoric peoples during the lengthy Archaic period. Because few isolable Archaic components have been excavated, a comprehensive understanding of artifact chronologies, subsistence practices, and settlement strategies is lacking. Previous investigations along the West Fork Trinity River (Peter and McGregor 1988; Yates and Ferring 1986) indicate that primary contexts for Early and Middle Archaic sites could occur deeply buried within floodplain alluvium. Diagnostic artifacts for the period are similar to those of adjacent regions, although developing a sound chronological sequence of diagnostic tool types has proven difficult because many previous investigations have focused on terrace surface manifestations where early artifacts are frequently mixed with later materials. Prikryl (1990) suggests that Early Archaic projectile points include early split- stemmed varieties (and possibly the Angostura type), while Middle Archaic points include basal-notched forms such as the Andice, Bell, and Calf Creek types along with stemmed varieties such as Bulverde, Carrollton, Dawson, and Wells. Some of the artifact mixing and the generally low numbers of known Early and Middle Archaic sites may be due to extensive erosion of mid-Holocene deposits, as has been documented for the Brazos River drainage west of the Dallas area in Young, Stephens, and Throckmorton counties (Ensor et al. 1992). The relatively intensive work at Richland-Chambers Reservoir suggests a similar conclusion for the western edge of the Post Oak Savanna at the eastern margin of the Blackland Prairie, although it has been noted that data pertaining to the Early and Middle Archaic subperiods may be scarce, in part, because sites dating to that interval lie deeply buried or were removed by extensive erosion during the mid-Holocene (Fields 1995; McGregor and Bruseth 1987). Late Archaic point types within the region reportedly include Castroville, Dallas, Edgewood, Elam, Ellis, Gary, Godley, Marshall, Palmillas, Trinity, and Yarbrough (Prikryl 1990). At one time, the Carrollton and Elam foci were used to define the Middle and Late Archaic, respectively (Crook and Harris 1952, 1954). The initial treatment of the Archaic period (Crook and Harris 1952, 1954), which defined the Carrollton and Elam foci, was based upon materials from relatively shallow terrace contexts. Reevaluation of the type-site artifacts; however, showed that the materials were so mixed that perpetuation of these foci provides little interpretive value (Hofman 1989a; Prikryl 1990). Consequently, these time-space constructs are no longer recognized acceptable to describe North-Central Texas prehistoric chronology (Peter and McGregor 1988; Prikryl 1987; Yates and Ferring 1986). Though few isolable components have been analyzed for the various divisions of the Archaic period in North-Central Texas, slowly increasing populations responding to warmer and drier environmental conditions have been postulated to explain the overall archeological record of the period (Lynott 1981; Story 1990). It is thought that those factors may have led the indigenous populations of the Cross Timbers and prairie areas of North-Central Texas to develop a diversified hunting and gathering pattern based on bottomland resources of the rivers and major streams during the Archaic period, whereas, populations on the Southern Plains maintained a focus on bison hunting (Hofman 1989a). Projectile points of the period were generally fashioned from locally-available lithic raw materials (typically quartzite), suggesting that populations were less mobile than were their Paleoindian predecessors. Less mobility also may suggest refinement of the diversified subsistence pattern to include scheduling of resource use within more restricted areas and repeated seasonal site occupation. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 23


---

## Page 36

Archeological sites dating to the Late Archaic period are relatively more common across the Blackland Prairie. The Late Archaic represents the earliest period for which much is known about indigenous prehistoric lifeways. Both the Pecan Springs site (41EL11) at Bardwell Reservoir and the Strawn Creek site (41NV6) at Navarro Mills Reservoir yielded Late Archaic projectile point types such as Gary, Dawson, and Yarbrough, although they tended to be mixed with materials from later occupations (Duffield 1963; Sorrow et al. 1966). The surface collections analyzed during the Superconducting Super Collider project also contained those types, as well as several Late Archaic types common in Central Texas—such as Bulverde, Darl, Ensor, Marcos, Marshall, Montell, and Pedernales; and a variety of probable Late Archaic projectile point forms, such as Carrollton, Edgewood, Elam, Ellis, and Kent, more characteristic of regions to the north and east (Yedlowski et al. 1998). Noting the relatively high frequency of Late Archaic projectile points, Yedlowski et al. (1998) suggested an increased regional use by hunter-gatherers as a result of increasingly moist climatic conditions, presumably associated with greater productivity in subsistence resources. They also noted that, although the projectile point evidence indicates interaction with groups living in Central Texas proper, larger proportions of projectile points suggest affinities with East Texas to a greater degree than during the Early and Middle Archaic periods. Investigations at Joe Pool Lake (Peter and McGregor 1988) and Lake Ray Roberts (Ferring and Yates 1997) indicated that the Late Archaic period is characterized by site assemblages left by small bands of foraging hunters and gatherers who occupied a locality for a limited time and then moved to another locality, supporting a resource-scheduling hypothesis. Deer and numerous small mammals were the primary food resources. Despite refinement of resource utilization, evidence of dietary stress has been found on Late Archaic human skeletal materials (Ferring and Yates 1997; Gill-King 1987). Brown and his colleagues (1987:44-22–44-26) present similar conclusions concerning an increase in occupational intensity and increased interaction in the southeastern part of the North-Central Texas region (i.e., Cedar Creek Reservoir in southeastern Kaufman County and Richland-Chambers Reservoir in southeastern Navarro County). Two of the three excavated sites at Cedar Creek Reservoir have prominent Late Archaic components represented by numerous Gary-style points and a variety of other lithic tools (Story 1965), and at least 15 sites at Richland-Chambers Reservoir have identifiable components of Late Archaic age. In addition to assemblages of projectile point styles (e.g., Dawson, Gary, Godley, Kent, and Yarbrough) that indicate ties to the regions to the north and east of the Blackland Prairie, rather than to the south and west, each of these areas has yielded information suggesting that ceramics may have been introduced into the material culture of local groups during the latter part of the Late Archaic, much as they were across most of East Texas (where this interval usually is termed the Early Ceramic or Woodland period). At Cedar Creek Reservoir, distributional data from the Lacy (41HE70) and Gossett Bottom (41KF7) sites suggest that the initial use of ceramic containers preceded introduction of the bow and arrow, although it is difficult to be certain because the sites were not well stratified, and the ceramics could not be related to known early ceramic ware types such as Williams Plain (Story 1965). At Richland-Chambers Reservoir, distinctive shell-tempered sherds were recovered from contexts dated between A.D. 200 and 700 at the Adams Ranch site (41NV177; Bruseth and Martin 1987), apparently representing the earliest ceramic industry in the upper Trinity River basin. The most complete picture of the archeology of the Late Archaic in this region comes from the Post Oak Savanna at the eastern margin of the Blackland Prairie. Along Richland and Chambers creeks, Late Archaic groups appear to have been hunter-gatherers whose subsistence pursuits focused on wild plant foods such as hickory nuts and prairie turnips, and fauna such as deer, turtles, small mammals, birds, and fish (McGregor and Bruseth 1987). Although presumably not sedentary, prehistoric indigenous groups clearly used the area in an intensive fashion for residential purposes, and it appears that populations increased while territory sizes decreased. The presence of large anthropogenic basins or depressions, often measuring approximately 16 m (52.5 ft) in diameter and 0.6 m (0.2 ft) in depth, associated with Late Archaic period sites suggests that important sociopolitical changes may have been occurring during that period. These features were first identified at a series of sites along the East Fork Trinity River and were considered an MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 24


---

## Page 37

important trait of the now obsolete Wylie focus (Harris and Suhm 1963; Stephenson 1952; Wilson 1946). These were large, circular features that appear to have been used for communal processing of vegetal resources (and later as cemeteries), perhaps in the context of band aggregation in “tension zones” as territories decreased in size (McGregor and Bruseth 1987). Radiocarbon assays from those features at the Upper Rockwall (41RW2) and Sister Grove Creek (41COL36) sites in the Lavon Lake area suggest that the pits and the Wylie focus were associated with the Late Prehistoric period (Lynott 1975; Ross 1966). Lynott (1977) widened the temporal span by incorporating a Late Archaic phase into the focus definition. Subsequent work on “Wylie pit” features at Richland-Chambers Reservoir to the south confirmed their Late Archaic age (Bruseth and Martin 1987). Nonetheless, the wide range of associated artifact types and the long temporal span represented by the pits made it clear that the Wylie focus was not a useful construct. Bruseth and Martin (1987), while discarding the focus as unusable, further supported Lynott’s (1975) original interpretation of the pits as being associated with large-scale food processing. Their interpretation equates the pits to the burned rock middens of Central Texas, with both feature types representing group aggregations and feasting activities. Late Prehistoric Period The Late Prehistoric period (ca. A.D. 700 to 1600) is marked by the initial appearance of arrow points in the archeological record of North-Central Texas. The A.D. 700 date for the beginning of this period is based on dated contexts for similar material culture in the Brazos River drainage to the west. Group aggregation and large-scale manipulation of subsistence resources, as represented by the Late Archaic Wylie pit features and the human burials they contain, may be indicative of societal changes that continued into the Late Prehistoric period. Habitation structures have been found at some Late Prehistoric sites and indicate increased sedentism, at least in certain places and at certain times. Along with cultigens such as corn, the widespread presence of arrow points and ceramic vessels indicate important technological changes in the Late Prehistoric period. There may also be evidence of differential mortuary practices (e.g., the distinction between burials placed inside and outside of Wylie pits) that could reflect a shift toward a hierarchical social structure, although that evidence in North-Central Texas is not nearly as strong as that for the Caddo area of East Texas. Both Lynott (1977) and Prikryl (1990) have proposed that the Late Prehistoric period be divided into an early and a late phase, with the early phase reflecting a continuation of the foraging subsistence system of the preceding Late Archaic period and the late phase reflecting increasing influences from extra-local groups on the Southern Plains farther to the north. Evidence of horticulture and bison procurement also appears in sites of this period (Harris and Harris 1970; Morris and Morris 1970). Prikryl’s (1990, 1993) Late Prehistoric I period (A.D. 700 to 1200) is marked by the Alba, Bonham, Catahoula, Scallorn, and Steiner arrow point types. Ceramic vessels are primarily grog-tempered and undecorated, but some evidence indicates influences from other regions in locally manufactured ceramic wares displaying designs similar to those associated with East Texas Caddo ceramic types. Evidence for corn agriculture and domestic structures has been found at sites at Joe Pool Lake in western Dallas County as well as to the north at Hubert Moss Lake near the Red River (Lorrain 1969; Martin 1994; Peter and McGregor 1988). Cultural changes were also taking place on the Southern Plains farther to the west, though that area adhered to the Archaic lifestyle even after technological innovations such as the bow and arrow were accepted (Lynott 1981). As a result of intensive excavations at the Cobb-Pool site (41DL148) at Joe Pool Lake, Peter and McGregor (1988) proposed a reformulation of the Late Prehistoric period. The Cobb-Pool site yielded house structures, roasting pits, Alba arrow points, grog-tempered ceramics, and charred corn cupules. Radiocarbon dates from several features indicate the site was occupied during the late 12th or early 13th century. Present evidence suggests that the site does not represent an intrusive Caddo occupation; consequently, a significant adaptive change appears to have occurred, in at least some areas, during a middle phase of the Late Prehistoric period. It is also likely that ceramics were not introduced to the region before that time. Whether the Cobb-Pool site merely represents a local experiment or reflects a regional adaptive MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 25


---

## Page 38

change separate from that emerging on the Plains to the north remains to be fully documented; however, analysis of a Native American burial salvaged at site 41DL373 along Spring Creek (a tributary of Rowlett Creek) in northeastern Dallas County also suggests maize consumption. Radiocarbon dating indicated that the individual lived between A.D. 1155 and 1275, while a carbon isotope ratio of -17.7 ‰ suggests that, although maize was consumed, it did not form a major part of the diet (Peter and Clow 2000). These indications of maize consumption contrast with a small grouping of disturbed human remains recovered from the Harbor Pointe site (41DL369), also in Dallas County. Located on Rowlett Creek (a tributary of the East Fork Trinity River), that site yielded remains of at least four individuals dated to approximately A.D. 1035. No pottery was recovered with these remains, although shell beads and a shell gorget were present, and a carbon isotope ratio of -21.6 ‰ suggests that the group’s diet was not high in maize (Cliff et al. 1996). Given the slightly earlier date of the Harbor Pointe burials compared to the Cobb-Pool and 41DL373 sites, it is possible that maize agriculture was introduced to the region ca. A.D. 1200; conversely, this same evidence may indicate that Late Prehistoric groups in North-Central Texas practiced divergent subsistence patterns. Sites dating to the broadly-defined Late Prehistoric period (e.g., post A.D. 700), are also common downstream within the Trinity River Basin of the Blackland Prairie. The collections studied during the Superconducting Super Collider project contain substantial numbers of both early (e.g., Alba, Bonham, Catahoula, Colbert, Scallorn, and Steiner) and late (e.g., Cliffton and Perdiz) arrow points, but Yedlowski et al. (1998) note that early arrow point varieties are more frequent, perhaps reflecting high population densities continuing from the Late Archaic period. Other than the Perdiz and Scallorn types, which have such widespread distributions, the arrow styles indicate cultural influences from east and north of the Blackland Prairie region. The limited ceramic samples, containing sherds reminiscent of types such as Weches Fingernail Impressed, Killough Pinched, Maydelle Incised, and Poynor Engraved, also support interaction with Caddo groups to the east (Yedlowski et al. 1998). The Strawn Creek site at Navarro Mills Reservoir presents a similar scenario, with most of the typed arrow points dating to the first half of the Late Prehistoric period and the more distinctive ceramics relating to the early Caddo types, such as Crockett Curvilinear Incised and Weches Fingernail Impressed (Duffield 1963). The Pecan Springs site at Bardwell Reservoir also yielded numerous early style arrow points, as well as a respectable number of Perdiz points, along with a small collection of highly fragmented ceramics (Sorrow et al. 1966). Three sites excavated at Cedar Creek Reservoir contained Late Prehistoric components, although materials of this age were common only at the Gossett Bottom and Lacy sites (Story 1965). Early arrow point styles (e.g., Alba, Catahoula, Friley, Granbury, Scallorn, and Steiner) are better represented than late ones (e.g., Cliffton and Perdiz), but not in significant amounts. Not surprisingly, ceramics are more frequent in this area than to the west and clearly are related to the Caddo tradition to the east and northeast; some of the more distinctive decorated sherds were considered reminiscent of types such as Canton Incised, Killough Pinched, and Ripley or Wilder Engraved (Story 1965). The faunal remains from the Gossett Bottom and Lacy sites, many of which probably relate to Late Prehistoric occupations, consist predominantly of deer, turtle, and small mammals, with bison occurring only in small numbers. Based on the feature evidence (or lack thereof), it appears that the Cedar Creek Reservoir portion of the Trinity River basin was occupied by mobile hunter-gatherers rather than sedentary groups during the Late Prehistoric period. Some of the best data concerning Native American use of the region during the Late Prehistoric period is from Richland-Chambers Reservoir. Sites dating to this interval are common at the reservoir, especially for the early half of the Late Prehistoric period, and it appears that there was a significant decline in population densities after about A.D. 1300 (McGregor and Bruseth 1987). The data suggest that most of the excavated sites with Late Prehistoric components were used for residential purposes (McGregor and Bruseth 1987), although there are some sites that more likely represent resource extraction camps (e.g., 41FT193 and 41NV139). The structural patterns at the Bird Point Island site (41FT201) indicate intensive use by sedentary hunter-gatherers during the early Late Prehistoric period. Conversely, investigations of other generally contemporaneous components at nearby sites (e.g., Adams Ranch, Irvine [41NV182], and MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 26


---

## Page 39

Little Cedar Creek [41NV173]) have yielded middens and numerous features suggesting intensive use but which lack house structures, possibly indicating seasonal occupation of those sites. Macrobotanical remains primarily indicate the use of wild plant foods such as hardwood nuts, a variety of seeds, and tubers/rhizomes (McGregor and Bruseth 1987). The only tropical cultigen is maize, and it occurs in very small quantities and only in contexts dating to the last half of the Late Prehistoric period. Hence, groups who lived in this area were predominantly hunters and gatherers. The arrow points that characterize the early (Alba, Scallorn, and Steiner) and late (Perdiz and Cliffton) parts of the period are the same styles found elsewhere across the area, and it appears that Gary dart points may have continued to be used through the early Late Prehistoric period (McGregor and Bruseth 1987:183). Ceramics are moderately common and clearly relate to Caddo wares, with most of the identified types (e.g., Maydelle Incised, Poynor Engraved, and Weches Fingernail Impressed) indicating contact with groups in the Neches River drainage, east of the Trinity River. During the Late Prehistoric II period (A.D. 1200 to 1600), influences from the Southern Plains became pronounced in the Cross Timbers and the adjacent prairie areas. These influences coincided with an increase in bison herd size in North-Central Texas (Lynott 1981; Prikryl 1990). Bison were important to subsistence but shrinking procurement territory sizes due to population increases continued the trend toward horticulture and settled village life (Harris and Harris 1970; Morris and Morris 1970). In terms of technology, a Plains Indian tool assemblage became increasing common in the region (Prikryl 1990). Items associated with that assemblage include calcareous-tempered ceramic vessels, some of which fit the description for the Nocona Plain type (shell-tempered), and triangular arrow points such as the Fresno, Harrell, Maud, Perdiz, and Washita styles. Tools specific to bison hide processing include snub-nosed or thumbnail scrapers and edge-beveled Harahey knives. Bison scapula hoes, which also are common in Plains Indian sites, have been recovered from sites in the Lewisville Lake and Lavon Lake areas of Denton and Collin counties (Barber 1969; Harris 1945). Concerning the Late Prehistoric components investigated at Lake Ray Roberts, Ferring and Yates (1997:305) summarize Late Prehistoric II period occupations as characterized by multiple, short-term, logistic sites that lack ceramic artifacts and architectural features, with the exception of site 41DN102, which contained the only architectural remains identified within the project area and might represent a small hamlet occupied between approximately A.D. 1300 and 1450. No evidence of horticulture was recovered, and faunal remains indicate a foraging strategy that emphasized a reliance on deer procurement and occasionally bison. Little evidence of the transport and curation of chert raw materials was encountered and there was no evidence of long-distance raw material acquisition. Late Prehistoric II ceramic traditions in the Lake Ray Roberts area were dominated by locally-produced, shell-tempered wares, which were being produced across the general Southern Plains region by that time. Furthermore, little, if any, evidence of interaction with the Caddo was encountered within the area, which supports Story’s previous synthesis of that period (Story 1990). Thus, the Lake Ray Roberts data suggest that regional traditions emerged at the end of the Late Prehistoric period largely independent of the Southern Plains or East Texas woodlands. The Plains Indian influences associated with settled village life were used to define the now outmoded Henrietta complex of North-Central Texas. The Henrietta complex, based on Krieger’s (1946) more expansive Henrietta focus, extends from the Red River south along the headwaters of the Trinity and Brazos rivers. The type site, M. D. Harrell (41YN1), is located along the Brazos River in Young County just northwest of Palo Pinto County in the drainage basin now inundated by Possum Kingdom Reservoir (Krieger 1946). The Harrell site and other associated sites contain middens, house structures, hearths, storage pits, and burials, and they most often are located on sandy knolls or terraces overlooking river valleys (Brooks 1989; Forrester 1994). Excavated sites attributed to the Henrietta complex include the Glass (41MU24) and Coyote (41MU28) sites located on the Red River in Montague County (Lorrain 1967; Woodall 1967), and it is clear that sites such as Dillard (41CO174) in Cooke County are related as well (Martin 1994). The Henrietta complex as an investigative tool is limiting to archeological analysis since its loose definition tends to obscure local differences, while more recent investigations in the upper Trinity River drainage have MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 27


---

## Page 40

tended to highlight local variations. At Ray Roberts Lake, for instance, Ferring and Yates (1997) see local trends emerging independent of extra-regional influences. They cite the lack of evidence for extensive maize horticulture and the preponderance of short-term logistical camps as the basis of their hypothesis. Similarly, Peter and McGregor (1988) demonstrated that occupation of the Mountain Creek drainage was less intensive in the Late Prehistoric II period than during prior periods and evidence of the practice of maize horticulture is limited. Available evidence suggests that the regional adaptation developing in North- Central Texas after A.D. 1200 was truly distinctive from the Southern Plains adaptations revealed in areas to the northwest (Peter et al. 1997). Protohistoric & Historic Native American Periods The cultural divergences between North-Central and Northeast/East Texas that began in the Archaic period continued into the Protohistoric and Historic periods. The sporadic nature and uncertain extent of early European contact with indigenous groups in North-Central Texas, coupled with disparities in knowledge about these groups themselves late in prehistory, make it difficult to precisely determine the temporal boundary between the Late Prehistoric, Protohistoric, and Historic periods. Various sociological factors, primarily the colonization of New Mexico by the Spanish, caused drastic changes in the cultural composition of North-Central Texas, as groups from elsewhere migrated into the area and existing groups were forced to adapt to their presence. Meanwhile, Caddo groups continued to dominate the northeastern portion of the region, although significant changes were also occurring there. For purposes of this synopsis, the Protohistoric period begins in 1542 with the first sustained contact between Native Americans and the Spanish in the region. Although contact between Spanish and Native American groups during at least the early portion of the Protohistoric period was indirect and intermittent, European exploration, trading, and settlement affected various indigenous groups in a variety of ways. Unfortunately, historical documentation and archeological evidence are very sparse for the Protohistoric period in the upper Trinity River basin. After 1598; however, Spanish influence was never absent from the Southern Plains. However, direct Native American contact with Europeans continued to be limited and only brief records of Spanish journeys into or through the North-Central Texas area exist (Hofman 1989b; John 1975). Although European trade items appear on a limited number of archeological sites in North-Central Texas (Sollberger 1953), no Protohistoric site in the region has been thoroughly investigated and characterizations of the Native American adaptations during that period are conjectural at best. Tonkawa, Wichita, Caddo, and Comanche all are likely to have traversed the area. The manner in which many Native American populations responded to contact with the Spanish and their cultural trajectory following this contact is not precisely known, and the exact locations of their sites and detailed ethnohistoric data are almost nonexistent. By the 18th century, migrant Plains Indian groups had moved into and beyond North Texas, and their documentation by traders and explorers marks the beginning of the Historic Period (Prikryl 1993). Unfortunately, because reliable historical documentation is very sparse for the upper Trinity River basin during the early historic period, it is not clear which specific aboriginal groups were residing in the present- day region at the beginning of this period. Clearly, the early historic era in North Texas was a time of population fluctuation, movement, and amalgamation (see Newcomb 1961). Documentary sources suggest that the Tonkawa, Apache, Comanche, Wichita, Kitsai, Yojaune, Caddo, Delaware, and Kickapoo traversed the region at various times during the period (Campbell 1983; Newcomb 1961; Newcomb and Campbell 1982). At the beginning of the 19th century, the physical presence of Europeans on the Southern Plains became commonplace. This was the result of increasingly peaceful relations between the Spanish in Texas and the Plains Indians to the north, and the acquisition of the Louisiana Territory by the United States in 1803. 3.2 Previous Investigations in the Vicinity of the Project Area A file search within the Texas Archeological Sites Atlas (TASA), maintained by the THC, indicates that six professional archeological surveys have been previously conducted within 1 mi (1.6 km) of the project area (Figure 3.1; Table 3.2). Artifacts encountered at the site consisted of faunal bone, mussel shell, burned rock, and burned clay. Through the survey investigations, it was determined that the site likely represents a briefly utilized hunting/foraging campsite that was quickly buried after abandonment. While the archeologists that documented the site detail that the contextual integrity of the site may be compromised, the THC’s NRHP status for the site remains undetermined. Site 41DL427 is located within a high-voltage overhead electric transmission line easement east of MacArthur Boulevard and north of Bear Creek. The site was encountered during the archeological monitoring of bore holes for the construction of the overhead transmission line towers. The site was encountered within two adjacent bore holes at a depth of approximately 100 cmbs. Artifacts documented at the site consisted solely of mussel shell and faunal bone. Archeologists documented that the site appeared to have high levels of preservation and may be eligible for inclusion in the NRHP. 3.2.1 - Previous IES Investigations Between 08 to 11 July and 17 to 18 July 2019, IES conducted a Phase I intensive archeological survey for the proposed MacArthur Reclamation project (Goodmaster et al. 2019). Due to the alluvial setting and potential for deeply-buried archeological deposits, IES implemented intensive survey methods tailored to assess for those resources. During the Phase I survey, 33 backhoe trenches were excavated within the project area. The trench placement was based on soil data, the presence of wetland features, ground surface microtopography derived from LiDAR topographic survey data, and survey impediments such as existing buildings, structures, fences, and roads. A prominent paleosol, generally expressed as a subangular blocky to massive very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay or silty clay, was encountered within 32 of the 33 excavated survey trenches. The top of the paleosol ranged in depth from 26 to 180 cmbs with an average depth of 65 cmbs. The top of the paleosol was deepest at the western end of the project area, where it was between 109 and 180 cmbs in Trenches 24 and 26 through 28. It was not observed in Trench 25, which reached at maximum depth of 320 cmbs. A notable feature of the paleosol was the apparent presence of a cumulic A horizon. Although not dated, either by radiocarbon or temporally diagnostic artifacts, the pedogenic features and the stratigraphic and geomorphic contexts of the buried soil suggest it is the West Fork paleosol. Twenty-six of the 33 trenches excavated during the July 2019 survey were negative for archeological deposits. However, a variety of prehistoric cultural materials and the remnants of several possible cultural features were encountered within the paleosol in seven of the survey trenches. Based on the locations of the positive trenches, it was concluded that two prehistoric sites (41DL553 and 41DL554) were encountered within the project area during the survey. Site 41DL553 Site 41DL553 pertains to a prehistoric habitation site, likely a logistical or foraging camp, located near the eastern margin of the project area. The site is within the proposed building footprint and grading limits of the proposed project. The site was tentatively defined during the Phase I survey as extending approximately 209 ft (63 m) north-to-south by 260 ft (80 m) east-to-west to encompass 0.86 ac. At that time, the site boundary was established by the excavation of negative trenches to the west and the presence of two large emergent herbaceous wetlands, which likely occupy surface depressions representing abandoned channel meanders of the West Fork Trinity River, located to the north and southwest of the site. The eastern portion of the site boundary was based on the property fence and the adjacent MacArthur Boulevard road berm. Based on the Phase I survey data, it was concluded that 41DL553 consisted of a prehistoric occupation within a cumulic paleosol (cf. West Fork paleosol), the top of which ranged in depth from 32 to 74 cmbs. One thermal feature, likely representing the remnants of a hearth or roasting pit, was encountered within Phase I survey Trench 29 at a depth of 150 to 160 cmbs. Cultural materials encountered at the site included chipped lithic artifacts (n=3), faunal remains (deer [n>20], fish [n=1], waterfowl [n=1], and mussel shell [n>30]), burned clay fragments (n>50), and fire-cracked rocks (n>20). Based on the Phase I survey data, the archeological deposits at site 41DL553 appeared to be concentrated in northern portion of the site and were relatively more shallow in the northeastern portion of the site (136 to 150 cmbs) and deeper in the northwestern portion of the site (150 to 200 cmbs). Site 41DL554 Site 41DL554 pertains to a prehistoric logistical or foraging camp located near the southeastern margin of the project area. The site is within the grading limits of the proposed project and extends slightly into the MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 31

proposed building footprint. The site was tentatively defined during the Phase I survey as extending approximately 190 ft (58 m) northwest-to-southeast by 558 ft (170 m) northeast-to-southwest to encompass 1.67 ac. At the time of the Phase I survey, the site boundary was established by the excavation of negative trenches to the east and west, as well as the project area boundary to the south. The northern and northwestern portions of the site boundary were also influenced by the presence of emergent herbaceous wetlands to the north and west of the site.
