CHAPTER 4: METHODS The objectives of this study were to identify the horizontal and vertical extents of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 within the project area and to collect site-specific data through a combination of mechanical and controlled manual excavation methods sufficient to evaluate the significance of each site with regard to its potential for inclusion in the NRHP. A project-specific scope of work was designed to implement those objectives and evaluate the significance and research potential of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554. The methods used to investigate and evaluate these sites are summarized in this chapter. 4.1 Resource Evaluation 4.1.1 - National Register Evaluation Criteria The assessment of significance of a cultural resource is based on federal guidelines and regulations. The criteria for evaluating resources for inclusion in the NRHP are codified under the authority of the NHPA, as amended (36 CFR 60.4 [a–d]), and the Advisory Council on Historic Preservation (ACHP) has set forth guidelines to use in determining site eligibility. Federal regulations indicate that “[t]he term ‘eligible for inclusion in the National Register’ includes both properties formally determined as such by the Secretary of the Interior and all other properties that meet National Register listing criteria” (36 CFR 800.2[e]). Based on ACHP guidelines, any cultural resource that is included in or eligible for inclusion in the NRHP is a historic property. Subsequent to the identification of relevant historical themes and related research questions for the evaluation of a resource, four criteria for NRHP eligibility are applied (36 CFR 60.4[a–d]). The quality of significance in American history, architecture, archeology, engineering, and culture is present in districts, sites, buildings, structures, and objects that possess integrity of location, design, setting, material, workmanship, feeling, and association and: Criterion A: that are associated with events that have made a significant contribution to the broad patterns of our history; or Criterion B: that are associated with the lives of persons significant in our past; or Criterion C: that embody the distinctive characteristics of a type, period, or method of construction, or that represent the work of a master, or that possess high artistic values, or that represent a significant and distinguishable entity whose components may lack individual distinction; or Criterion D: that have yielded, or may be likely to yield, information important in prehistory or history. The principal objective is to determine whether a cultural resource possesses the potential to contribute to one or more of the above-defined criteria. Adequate information regarding site function, context, and chronological placement from both archeological and, if appropriate, historical perspectives is essential for the evaluation of resources. Because research questions vary as a result of geography and temporal period, determining the site context and chronological placement of resources are particularly important objectives during the inventory and evaluation processes. Criteria A, B, and C typically reflect association with historic-era resources. Criterion D is generally associated with prehistoric, but also historic-era, archeological sites. The objectives of these investigations were to more accurately define both the horizontal and vertical extents of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554and evaluate each site for NRHP eligibility. The perceived importance or significance of an archeological resource is driven by several factors, including current research perspectives and the state of available information regarding specific research topics in a particular research area. Over time, data requirements for some research questions may be addressed to various degrees, even though complementary data are often needed from different temporal periods, geographic settings, or site types to fully understand the diversity of past human activities. If data approach redundancy, the need for corroborative information from similar sites is diminished. This MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 33


---

## Page 46

suggests that distinctions of what constitutes scientifically important information is tied to both a specific geographical area, a cultural adaptation, and accumulated knowledge regarding various research domains. A prehistoric archeological site’s significance, therefore, is based on a holistic knowledge of the prehistory of a region and the potential of the information and data derived from a site to significantly contribute to our understanding of the past. Under Criterion D, cultural resources sites that possess contextual integrity and that have the potential to contribute significant information to relevant research domains organized within a contextual framework are regarded as eligible for inclusion in the NRHP. 4.2 NRHP Evaluation Methods Establishing the research potential of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 requires an assessment of the capacity for data derived from the archeological excavation of each site to contribute important information relevant in addressing pertinent regional research topics and providing new insights to the understanding of regional prehistory. Overviews for various aspects of the prehistoric archeology and culture history in North-Central Texas and the upper Trinity River basin have been previously prepared (e.g., Peter and McGregor 1988; Prikryl 1987, 1990; and Yates and Ferring 1986). Previous researchers have proposed specific research questions regarding the prehistory of the region, and an overview of the regional prehistory is presented in Chapter 3. A brief contextual framework for the NRHP evaluation of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 is presented in this section. This framework identifies potential research topics and inadequacies in current knowledge pertaining to the prehistory of the region. It also addresses research domains that will be used to evaluate the two sites during this effort. The geographic focus of this context is the upper Trinity River basin of North-Central Texas, as described in Chapter 2. It should be noted that past human activities, and therefore archeological and historical studies, are inherently multi-scalar. The archeological sites within the project area were part of social, political, and economic networks that probably extended much farther than the West Fork Trinity River watershed, the upper Trinity River basin, the North-Central Texas archeological region, or perhaps even the South Plains ecological region. Although it may be convenient to compartmentalize this context within the project area, it is necessary to consider information from surrounding regions. The Blackland Prairie represents an ecotone—a boundary between several distinct geographic and ecological areas. As such, archeological sites in this area may provide an opportunity to explore characteristics of the prehistoric indigenous groups that inhabited or otherwise utilized the region in the past, the diffusion of ideas between these groups, and the cultural changes that occurred within these groups over time. It should be understood that prehistoric groups were not bound by lines on maps and that geomorphic regions did not equal cultures. Likewise, modern socially- or politically-defined areas had no bearing on the people that inhabited them in the past. Therefore, cultural identities cannot be hastily assigned to archeological manifestations within the upper Trinity River basin based on our cursory understanding of the archeological record preserved there. Likewise, theories of human geography and landscape perspectives in archeology suggest that settlement patterns and other socio-political phenomena are as much cognitive as they are physical, further blurring the lines between what have previously been thought of as distinct “culture areas.” Another critical aspect of a context is its temporal framework. In addition to understanding what happened in the past, it is imperative to know when events and phenomena occurred. Unfortunately, the human past, and in particular the prehistoric past, encompasses a broad time interval of at least 12,000 years for which there is no written record in this region. Archeologists have worked diligently to organize the past into a temporal framework based on various chronometric dating techniques (most commonly radiocarbon dating) and artifact chronologies from well-preserved (and sometimes not-so-well-preserved) contexts. The temporal framework used to organize the Native American cultural context presented in Chapter 3 is based on archeological research across the region, which is generally accepted as applicable to North-Central Texas and the upper Trinity River basin, in light of available information for specific time periods. In some cases, date ranges are hypothesized or otherwise imprecise and require refinement through future archeological research. Historically, archeologists have associated the passing of time with cultural change and often associate a cultural period with a time interval. The accuracy and implications of these normative MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 34


---

## Page 47

divisions are often debated, resulting in their periodic revision. The cultural units and temporal subdivisions presented in this report correspond with other contemporary research in the region. However, it should be stressed that many cultural periods recognized in the region lack extensive or definitive chronometric data. 4.2.1 - Research Domains Various themes, study units, and research questions have been proposed for the study of North-Central Texas archeology (for summaries and overviews, see Peter and McGregor 1988; Prikryl 1987, 1990; Story et al. 1990; and Yates and Ferring 1986). In general, such constructs are motivated by differing theoretical frameworks and provide varying amounts of interpretive and explanatory value. An array of research domains, applicable to several geographic regions, temporal units, and site types, has been organized that offers utility for NRHP eligibility determinations. These research domains focus on topics of chronology, cultural affiliation, site structure and function, subsistence, technology, bioarcheology, paleoenvironmental reconstruction, and the development of archeological methods or techniques. Research domains are often interrelated but serve to organize specific research questions that may be posed for archeological sites evaluated under NRHP evaluation Criterion D. These research domains are flexible in that they can generate research questions of varying degrees of specificity, contingent upon the corpus of knowledge regarding a particular site type or chronological period. Research domains are useful for assessing site significance and addressing the research potential of archeological sites (e.g., Davis 1982; Kenmotsu and Perttula 1993; Miller et al. 2009). They identify the types of data and information required to answer current research questions defined within temporal intervals and geographic areas. Addressing a research domain is contingent upon the recovery of specific artifact classes, cultural features, or other archeological data to provide relevant information pertinent to the associated prehistoric context. The potential of archeological data generated during the excavation of a site to answer research questions can then be evaluated. Table 4.1 provides a generalized framework of potential research domains relevant to understanding sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 in the context of the prehistoric cultural landscape within the upper Trinity River drainage basin of North-Central Texas (see Chapter 3). In addition, specific classes of artifacts, data, or other evidence necessary to contribute information relevant to the research domains are identified. The right column provides a preliminary assessment, based on prior Phase I survey results, of the level of expectation that additional excavations at these sites will have in terms of providing data relevant to the research domains. Chronology Some archeologists do not consider the chronological or chronometric data potential of a site a research domain per se and suggest an explicit distinction between chronometric data as a site characteristic and as a research domain (i.e., Miller et al. 2009). Regardless, the potential of a site to yield chronometric information is extremely important in the evaluation of NRHP significance. Without the ability to place an archeological site within a temporal framework, the site cannot be linked to a cultural context by which it can be evaluated. Chronometric data are also important information applicable to all the research domains presented here. Site chronology may be determined through the analysis of temporally diagnostic artifacts, chronometric dating techniques, archival records, or oral history. It should be noted, however, that the creation of cultural constructs or the identification of temporally diagnostic artifacts based on data from a few sites with a paucity of chronometric dating has contributed little to Texas archeology. Artifact assemblages associated with prehistoric complexes and phases in North-Central Texas are often so similar to those defined for adjacent geographic areas and temporal periods that their current utility is limited. If there is to be a reliance on cultural phases, complexes, and other normative constructs as a means of differentiating geographically and temporally discrete manifestations of past human behavior, much greater attention must be given to defining the artifact classification criteria (see Section 4.2.1.4) and determining appropriate chronological positions of those artifacts in time. Stone tool chronologies should be reconsidered and the context of dateable material, whether used for relative or absolute dating methods, MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 35

The potential to preserve temporally or culturally diagnostic artifacts or intact cultural features that may be chronometrically dated via techniques such as radiocarbon, thermoluminescence, optically stimulated luminescence, or archaeomagnetic dating should be considered a necessary factor in the evaluation of a site’s eligibility for inclusion on the NRHP. Settlement Systems The primary purpose of this research domain is to identify the function or functions of a particular site type during the temporal period or periods of its occupation. This domain encompasses information regarding duration and seasonality of site use, the types of activities that took place at the site through time, temporal changes in the function of the site, and spatial differentiation of activity areas across the site. Research in this domain contributes to understanding the roles of various sites and site types within the multi-scalar social, political, and economic networks of which they were a part. Related to this research domain are investigations into the decision-making criteria involved in site selection and placement processes. Investigations of participation in prehistoric trade or social networks can also be made based on contributions of this domain. The potential to preserve artifacts and cultural features relevant to determining the function or functions of the site, its duration of occupation, or spatial differentiation of activity areas should be considered a contributing factor in the evaluation of a site’s eligibility for inclusion on the NRHP. Subsistence Systems The primary focus of this research domain is the detailed determination of the nutritional base utilized by the past regional inhabitants and the adaptive strategies related to the procurement, harvesting, preparation, and storage of floral and faunal subsistence resources. Detailed analysis of faunal remains has the potential to provide information regarding hunting and butchering activities during prehistoric periods and phases. Likewise, detailed analysis of macrobotanical and microbotanical remains has the potential to provide MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 36

insights into subsistence strategies concerning the continuum of procuring plant foods and changes in those strategies over time. Of particular interest would be the recovery of substantial microbotanical samples from prehistoric floodplain and terrace sites to augment current knowledge derived from previous investigations. The potential to preserve artifacts, cultural features, and floral or faunal remains illustrating the procurement, preparation, and storage of subsistence resources should be considered a contributing factor in the evaluation of a site’s eligibility for inclusion on the NRHP. Material Culture and Technological Change This broad research domain is concerned with raw material selection and the manufacture, variability, function, maintenance, and discard of various types of artifacts and cultural features. Raw material types, their sources, and procurement patterns offer critical knowledge to this domain. Various trace-element analyses may be helpful in determining the sources of non-local material types or trade goods. In addition, microscopic use-wear analysis is becoming increasingly helpful in determining specific functions of individual prehistoric tools and tool types. Experimental approaches to artifact functional analysis have yielded insight to research questions within this domain. Another topic within this domain is the determination of differential sequences of manufacture or shifts in manufacturing techniques among artifact types over time. Research within this domain may also delineate activity-specific artifact assemblages within and between sites. In addition, culturally, temporally, or functionally diagnostic artifact types and assemblages may be defined through the research efforts of this domain. As discussed in Chapter 3, artifact assemblages associated with cultural or temporal units in North-Central Texas are currently of limited utility. Additional research and analyses are required to define culturally, temporally, or functionally diagnostic artifact styles. Therefore, the potential to preserve artifacts and cultural features illustrating selection of raw materials or the manufacture, variability, function, and maintenance of various artifact types should be considered a contributing factor in the evaluation of a site’s eligibility for inclusion on the NRHP. Cultural Affiliation The primary purpose of this research domain is to determine the cultural affiliation of components present at a given site. Due to the limited understanding of prehistoric cultures in North-Central Texas, coupled with the fact that more specific research questions are often intrinsically linked to cultural affiliation, an understanding of past cultural manifestations is of primary importance in the evaluation of a site. Cultural affiliation may be determined by the presence of culturally diagnostic artifacts or feature types that have been linked to the various archeological phases and temporal periods described in Chapter 3. However, it is important not to simply assume that the presence of specific artifact types on a site equates to a clear determination of cultural affiliation. Archeological phases are, by their very nature, normative constructs that focus attention on perceived commonalities at the expense of variation. Many artifact styles in North- Central Texas persisted for millennia and were developed or adopted, possibly at different times, by what were likely several different cultural groups. In addition, certain materials were trade goods that may have been procured or created by one “culture” but entered the archeological record through the actions of another. In other cases, the archeological manifestations of specific cultures at certain times in prehistory is still not clear. The determination of cultural affiliation is not limited to prehistoric period sites, as this topic is important in archeological research of the protohistoric and early historic periods as well. This information is also important in instances when consultation with present-day ancestors or descendant communities is necessary. The potential to preserve information that is attributable to specific cultural complexes, phases, patterns, or foci, or that aids in more clearly defining the characteristics of a particular culture, or that elucidate the transition between phases of a culture should be considered a contributing factor in the evaluation of a site’s eligibility for inclusion on the NRHP. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 37


---

## Page 50

Bioarcheology The primary focus of this research domain is the study of human biological remains within archeological contexts (see Story et al. 1990 for regional overview). This domain encompasses research germane to biometrics, dentition, disease, stress, trauma, mechanical injury, and mortuary analysis. Bioarcheological research is also concerned with determining adaptive efficiency through indirect measures such as demographic profiles, nutritional adequacy, stress levels, and the magnitude of disease and trauma loads. Bioarcheology may also be applied in an attempt to recognize genetic affinities between human skeletal remains and existing ancestral populations. The potential to preserve human skeletal remains or mortuary artifacts may be considered a contributing factor in the evaluation of a site’s eligibility for inclusion on the NRHP. Paleoenvironmental Reconstruction The primary focus of this research domain is the reconstruction of past environmental regimes. This domain is concerned with understanding the depositional processes that form archeological sites, the post- depositional processes that act upon them prior to their discovery and excavation, and reconstruction of the former geomorphic, hydraulic, floral, and faunal regimes that were present when the site was occupied. As noted by Miller and his colleagues (2009), paleoenvironmental data potential is rarely an intrinsic characteristic of an archeological site, as practically any subsurface excavation (whether on an archeological site or not) has the potential to yield data germane to paleoenvironmental reconstruction. However, much insight to past environments can be gained, largely through the application of geoarcheological techniques, but also through detailed faunal and microbotanical analysis when these materials are adequately preserved. Thus, the potential to preserve deposits that enhance the reconstruction of past environmental regimes may be considered a contributing factor in the evaluation of a site’s eligibility for inclusion on the NRHP. Archeological Techniques This research domain is concerned with the development of archeological survey, excavation, and analysis techniques. The techniques employed by archeologists will continue to change over time, as former methods are scrutinized and new methods are developed to more effectively locate, evaluate, and analyze cultural resources. Just as the water flotation method for the recovery of microscopic botanical remains was developed and subsequently evaluated and improved since the late 1960s, other archeological practices will be developed and refined to address research needs. Geospatial techniques of analysis, coupled with the recent availability of high-resolution geospatial data sets, have provided much insight to human- environment interactions. Recent advances in shallow subsurface geophysical survey allows this method to provide a rapid and noninvasive means of prospecting for buried archeological sites and investigating their potential for preserved cultural features and activity areas. It has been proposed that research questions that relate to the improvement of archeological methods or analytical techniques should be considered when assessing the significance of a site (cf. Little et al. 2000:29). Conversely, it can be argued that archeological methods and analytical techniques could be developed on any site, regardless of its significance. Significance, therefore, is contingent on data preserved at a site, not the methods or techniques used to extract such information. Thus, the potential to contribute to the development or refinement of archeological methods and analytical techniques will not, for the purposes of this project, be considered a factor in the evaluation of a site’s eligibility for inclusion on the NRHP. 4.3 Archeological Site Significance The application of NRHP criteria to archeological site evaluation is, by its very nature, somewhat subjective. It is important to ensure that sites are evaluated as completely and objectively as possible and in reference to explicit contexts and research questions to avoid both situations of de facto eligibility of some site types and the a priori dismissal of other, typically small or ephemeral sites, as not eligible. The evaluation of archeological site significance is contingent upon an understanding of a site’s temporal MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 38


---

## Page 51

position (as described previously in this chapter), the integrity of its deposits, and an assessment of the site’s information potential; that is, the ability for the data preserved at the site to contribute to important research questions organized in a contextual framework. 4.3.1 - Site Integrity In addition to meeting one or more of the criteria outlined in 36 CFR 60.4, a site must possess integrity of various aspects of its character to be considered a significant cultural resource. In the most general sense, integrity refers to the “ability of a property to convey its significance” (National Register Bulletin 15:44). The integrity determination is intrinsically related to the physical features or site aspects and their ability to contribute to the resource significance. Typically, a cultural resources site must retain integrity of several aspects, including location, design, setting, materials, workmanship, feeling, and association. For a site to maintain integrity of location, its structural remains, artifacts, and other cultural features should exist in the place where they were originally constructed or otherwise deposited. Design typically refers to elements such as the form, structure, space, plan, or style of a structure or site. It can also be applied to characteristics of technology, ornamentation, or materials reflected in the artifacts or features of a site. Setting refers to the physical environment of a cultural resource, which differs from the location aspect in that setting considers the character of a site’s surroundings and how it relates to them. Materials are the physical elements of a site, such as the building materials used to construct a structure, or remnants of the raw materials that provided the subsistence base for past human occupation of a site. Workmanship encompasses physical aspects of labor or craft industries through the application of both technological practices and aesthetic principles and can be expressed either as common traditions or innovative techniques. Feeling refers to an expression of the aesthetic character of a site and is typically applied to sites that include visible structural features. Association is the direct relationship between the characteristics that contribute to a site’s significant and the physical manifestation of that site. The manner in which these aspects are evaluated varies by site type and the criterion or criteria under which they are being evaluated. As the cultural resources within the MacArthur Reclamation project area consist entirely of prehistoric archeological sites, integrity can be described as the extent to which the three- dimensional distribution of artifacts and cultural features of the site reflect the behavioral processes of the people responsible for depositing those artifacts. A site’s integrity, although sometimes measured subjectively, is inherent to that particular site as it is a result of both site formation processes and the post- depositional processes that have occurred there. Furthermore, the integrity of the site must be critically evaluated. Assessment of site integrity should consider an array of observations including the type of landform and microtopographic features present at the site, soil characteristics, and the observation and documentation of natural and modern disturbances that have impacted the site. In many cases, typically due to natural processes of erosion and deposition or modern disturbances, the preservation of meaningful and interpretable spatial patterning of features and artifacts at archeological sites is compromised. At a minimum, an archeological site must retain a sufficient sample of associated artifacts or cultural features identified as being affiliated with a known or unique function or activity, cultural group, or temporal period. Isolable components, features, or activity areas, especially those found in dateable contexts could contribute important information. Therefore, pertinent research is contingent upon locating relatively undisturbed sites, components, or features that provide sufficient numbers of artifacts to facilitate statistically robust spatial or functional analyses. 4.3.2 - Information Potential A site that possesses integrity is regarded as eligible for inclusion in the NRHP if it has the potential to contribute significant information to research domains and questions organized within a relevant context. NRHP eligibility recommendations should not be formulaic, but rather should be based on systematic and judicious consideration. Research questions should not be overly compartmentalized within each of the research domains, but rather encompass aspects of several such domains to derive more expansive and regionally-relevant research potential. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 39


---

## Page 52

Based on Phase I survey data, the encapsulation of cultural materials within a cumulic paleosol at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 indicates the sites were occupied during a period of relative floodplain surface stability or, at the very least, during a period of low alluvial sedimentation rates. Although previously undated by radiocarbon analysis or diagnostic artifacts, the stratigraphic and geomorphic contexts of the paleosol and its attributes suggest it is the West Fork paleosol. The West Fork is a prominent soil- geomorphic feature within the upper Trinity River basin that, at a minimum, formed between 2,400 and 700 years ago. Stable carbon isotope analyses of organic matter from the West Fork soil/paleosol and overlying alluvium have exhibited shifts from more mesic C -dominated plant communities (e.g., tallgrass 4 wet meadows on floodplains) to drier floodplain environments dominated by C plants, though the exact 3 timing and rates of change seem to vary across the basin (see Frederick and McKee 2019; Hall et al. 2012). Thus, sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 have the potential to reflect basin-wide climatic and environmental trends or changes during the late Holocene and yield data on how site occupants during the latter part of the Late Archaic to the early half of the Late Prehistoric periods adapted to these changes. Because the cultural materials at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 appeared to be contained within a cumulic paleosol, stratigraphically or vertically discrete assemblages and occupation surfaces may be present for analysis, thus providing a record of change or trends over a 1,700-year or greater time interval. This time frame would include the transition from Late Archaic to early Late Prehistoric use of the West Fork Trinity River floodplain environment. Based on Phase I survey data, it was hypothesized that 41DL553 may represent a single occupation layer located between 136 to 200 cmbs. Less clear, based on the available survey data, was whether 41DL554 represents a stratified multi-component site, or a single component site that has been disturbed through pedoturbation. More detailed and controlled manual excavations and analyses were deemed necessary to delineate and identify more discrete occupation zones and/or evidence of disturbance. Site structure and integrity were therefore the primary focus of the NRHP evaluation of these sites. The degree of contextual integrity of both the cultural components and features of the sites was to be assessed during this Phase II evaluation. During the previous Phase I investigation, faunal bone and the possible remnants of thermal features were observed that could provide accurate dates for site utilization. Additional radiocarbon ages could also be obtained from soil organic matter within the paleosol and overlying alluvium, if necessary. The sequence of radiocarbon ages will be used to examine gross rates of sedimentation and depositional hiatuses through time. In conjunction with physical, chemical, and magnetic properties of bulk soil column samples and artifact distributions, an assessment of the structure and integrity of both sites was proposed. If either site contained discrete cultural components with high degrees of integrity, this investigation would determine if they may assist in refining the regional prehistoric chronology, and whether the artifacts and features present at these sites had the potential to fill some of the key data gaps pertaining to prehistoric lifeways within the North-Central Texas region. Several other factors were used to evaluate the significance of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554. The diversity of the artifact assemblage is critical for these sites to yield new data on prehistoric lifeways in the region. In addition to potentially providing chronological data, preserved bone, shell, and charred organic material may also contribute to answering questions about site function, seasonality, and subsistence. Therefore, if the artifact assemblage is diverse and abundant for one or more of the components, then it may be possible to draw conclusions about cultural affiliation and subsistence strategies, issues which represent key data gaps for this region and the Southern Plains in general. Unlike other sites in proximity (e.g., 41DL246 and 41DL426), sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 are situated at a distance from the modern West Fork Trinity River channel and may have historically been located in a less compromised setting, thus retaining higher potential to preserve intact deposits than exhibited in the aforementioned sites. Therefore, it was hypothesized that more accurate chronological data could be obtained from these sites. In summary, to be considered eligible for inclusion in the NRHP, sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 must each have 1) contextual integrity (either horizontal and/or vertical) of one or more of the cultural components and 2) datable organic material that can be linked to vertical strata within the soil column, in addition to MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 40


---

## Page 53

either 3) discrete cultural features and/or 4) a diverse artifact assemblage that can be analyzed and interpreted to yield significant data that will contribute important new information to substantially enhance the understanding of one or more of the previously discussed research domains relevant to the regional prehistoric context presented in Chapter 3. The assessment of these attributes and data potential of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 was evaluated using the excavation methods and analyses summarized in the following section. 4.4 Archeological Excavation Methods 4.4.1 - Mechanical Excavation Additional Phase I Site Delineation Trenching During the previous Phase I survey investigations, portions of the 41DL553 and 41DL554 site boundaries were largely based on the presence of emergent herbaceous wetland features and were not fully delineated. Thus, IES conducted additional delineation trenching for both sites to complete the Phase I survey component of this project. IES conducted 10 additional site delineation trenches, seven trenches at 41DL553 and three trenches at 41DL554. The additional Phase I delineation trenches were conducted within the project area using a Gradall hydraulic excavator, as it more easily permits the controlled excavation of sediments in standard intervals and the exposure of buried living surfaces and occupation zones as represented by clusters of artifacts, concentrations of faunal remains, and associated thermal or storage features. The trenches were typically 5 m (16.4 ft) in length and 1.5 m (4.9 ft) in width, with additional areas excavated to allow for trench access and egress and the benching and sloping of profiles for safety. Trenches were excavated to a depth of 20 cm below the deepest cultural deposits encountered at each site. Once each trench had been excavated to a depth of approximately 1.2 m (4 ft), an Occupational Safety and Health Administration (OSHA) competent field supervisor assessed the stability of the trench prior to recording soil data. If soil stability was low, the trench was widened through benching or sloping or limited to a safe depth for documentation. During excavation, the trench profiles and floor were monitored and periodically scraped to inspect for the presence of artifacts and potential prehistoric cultural features. Approximately 38 liters (l; the equivalent of two 5- gallon buckets) of excavated soil from every third excavator bucket were screened through 0.25-in (0.64- cm) hardware mesh or were manually troweled and inspected for cultural materials. The remaining excavated soil was visually inspected as it was placed on the spoil pile. Trench excavation ceased once culturally-sterile deposits were encountered that persisted for a depth of at least 20 cm below the deepest known cultural deposits at each site. Detailed soil stratigraphic data was recorded for each delineation trench. The additional Phase I delineation trenches were reviewed and sampled for geoarcheological analysis at the discretion of the Project Geoarcheologist, Karl Kibler of Cross Timbers Geoarcheological Services. Phase II Exploratory Trench Monitoring Phase II exploratory trench locations were selected to further assess previously encountered archeological features, higher artifact concentrations, and to expand the overall subsurface exposure of each site. Four exploratory trenches were excavated at 41DL553 and six exploratory trenches were excavated at 41DL554. All Phase II exploratory trenches were oriented to the cardinal directions to facilitate the placement and excavation of controlled test units within and immediately adjacent to the mechanically-excavated areas. Excavation of the Phase II exploratory trenches were monitored by IES field staff working in conjunction with the Project Geoarcheologist. Due to the depth of the known archeological deposits, which were previously encountered up to 200 cmbs at 41DL553 and 230 cmbs at 41DL554, trenches were widened, benched, and/or sloped, then deepened, as needed, to create a safe work zone and facilitate formal test unit excavation. The excavation process involved the monitoring of the mechanically-scraped surface and MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 41


---

## Page 54

excavated soil was closely inspected for evidence of cultural materials. All work was monitored by an OSHA-competent field supervisor. 4.4.2 - Controlled Excavation Units Controlled excavation (test) units were manually excavated to identify and evaluate the archeological content, vertical extent, and contextual integrity of each site more adequately. Eight, 1-m-x-1-m test units were manually excavated at each site. Artifacts recovered within test units were identified, tabulated, and inventoried by excavation level. Additionally, the physical properties of the soil or sediments excavated from each level were documented. Investigators documented the results of each test unit on standardized forms. Cultural and natural features were recorded in plan view sketches. The soil profile of each unit was also recorded. Initial Test Unit Excavation Two initial test units were excavated at each site in proximity to the area of highest artifact density and/or most intact prehistoric cultural feature encountered during previous site investigations. Each of the test units were manually excavated within the profile of a Phase II exploratory trench. The test units were excavated in 10-cm levels from the current ground surface to a depth of 20 cm below the deepest encountered cultural artifact and/or feature identified during the previous IES investigation. The goal of the initial test units was to confirm the relative vertical limits of the cultural horizons at each site and to ensure that no additional cultural horizons are located above the deepest deposits previously identified at 41DL553 and 41DL554. Targeted Test Unit Excavation Following excavation of the two initial test units at each site, the Gradall was utilized to strip culturally- sterile overburden from the ground surface and allow for targeted excavations of the subsequent six targeted test units per site. The mechanical removal of overburden was visually monitored, and the overburden deposits were periodically sampled during the overburden removal process. Mechanical overburden removal ceased approximately 20 cm above the depth of archeological deposits identified during previous stages of this investigation. Targeted test units were manually excavated in 10-cm levels to a depth of 20 cm below the deepest encountered cultural artifact and/or feature identified during the Phase II effort. 4.5 Analytical Methods All artifacts encountered in subsurface contexts were examined, identified, and tabulated. Each artifact was carefully examined to identify and determine, to the extent possible, its function or role and cultural or chronological affiliation within the identified contexts. Context and attribute data for all excavated materials were recorded in a database. The primary categories of material recovered were chipped lithic artifacts, burned or fire-cracked rock (FCR), faunal remains (including bivalve shell), and charred botanical samples. The following discussion describes analytical strategies and summarizes the attributes recorded during the analysis of each class of material. 4.5.1 - Prehistoric Artifact Analysis Lithic Analysis The objectives of the lithic analysis were to provide information about site function, technology and tool production, lithic source procurement (exchange/interaction), and temporal or cultural affiliation based on the presence of diagnostic chipped stone tool types. To address these issues, lithic artifacts were initially separated into five classes: (1) chipped stone tools, (2) chipped stone debitage, (3) cores, (4) ground, pecked, and battered stone artifacts, and (5) unworked stone (e.g., FCR and manuports). Lithic materials were cleaned and bagged according to provenience. Specimens within each provenience were generally sorted into five main classes comprised of cores, debitage, chipped stone tools, ground stone MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 42


---

## Page 55

tools, and unworked stone. Within each class, raw material types were sorted based on macroscopic observations. All specimens were examined macroscopically to identify any latent indications of use and/or wear. When necessary, 10x hand lenses were utilized. 4.5.1.1.1 - Cores and Reduction Strategies Cores identified during this project were cataloged following Andrefsky’s (1998) general morphological types. These types generally consisted of unidirectional and multidirectional cores, with subclasses consisting of bipolar cores, blade cores, and bifacial cores. Unidirectional cores typically feature a single flat surface or striking platform from which flakes were detached in a single trajectory away from the platform and parallel to previous flakes. Multidirectional cores feature a range of forms, as flakes were removed in various directions from multiple striking platforms. Cores are modified through five basic reduction techniques, the by-product of which are idiosyncratic flake forms. Core preparation reduction represents the early stages of typical reduction strategies. This strategy incorporates radial core forms and, in some cases, the primary reduction stage of single-platform pyramidal cores and opposed-platform cylindrical cores (Odell 2004). Flakes produced through initial core preparation often feature a single facet platform, multiple platform facets, or a cortical platform. They also contain little to no platform abrasion. The dorsal surfaces of flakes produced during initial core preparation typically feature the presence of cortex. In many cases, initial core preparation flakes feature multidirectional flake scar orientation. Blade core reduction represents strategies aimed to produce functional blades. They represent strategies for reducing both single-platform pyramidal cores and opposed-platform cylindrical cores discussed by Odell (2004). Flakes produced through blade core reduction feature flat platforms angled approximately 90 degrees to the ventral surface. These flakes feature slight curvature and unidirectional flake scar orientation. Depending on the progress of the reduction, blade core reduction flakes will feature little to no cortex (Andrefsky 1998). If cortex is present, it is typically visible on one of the few longitudinal flake scars. Bifacial thinning reduction is a strategy used to produce useable bifaces from cores. They represent strategies for later-stage reduction of both centripetal and radial cores (Andrefsky 1998; Odell 2004). Flakes produced through this method are often described as flakes of bifacial retouch (Frison 1968). These flakes feature multifaceted, ground platforms, lenticular cross sections, and multidirectional flake scar orientations. Bifacial thinning flakes also feature a diffuse bulb of percussion. There is often little to no cortex present on bifacial thinning flakes. Bipolar reduction is a strategy used to produce useable materials from significantly smaller source materials than other reduction strategies. These cores are modified through the force produced with a percussor and a supportive anvil (Andrefsky 1998; Odell 2004). Burination reduction is a strategy used to produce a sharp workable edge or corner. Burin flakes or spalls are distinctively narrow and thick (Odell 2004). They also feature triangular or trapezoidal cross sections. The most distinguishing feature of the burin spall is the dorsal surface. Typically produced from the edges of a bifacial tool, the dorsal face of a burin spall features the bifacially retouched edge of the original tool (Semenov 1964; Tomáŝková 2005). 4.5.1.1.2 - Debitage Chipped stone debitage refers to the initial by-products and waste flakes created during the lithic reduction process. Debitage recovered during excavation was catalogued using Andrefsky’s (1998) general morphological types. Flake types were distinguished by metric and technological attributes representing varying stages in a linear reduction model based on core form, platform preparation, and cortex removal. Qualitative attributes included: platform type, flake scar orientation, curvature, cortex coverage, size grade, thermal alteration, edge modification, raw material, flake type. Using those variables, each flake was typed MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 43


---

## Page 56

based on the general reduction strategy used to produce the flake. Metric attributes recorded during this analysis included the weight of the individual debitage specimens and cumulative weights. Frequency tables were used to aid in the organization of the data to provide a general sense of the proportional composition of the lithic assemblage analyzed at these sites and are presented in Chapter 5. 4.5.1.1.3 - Chipped Stone Tools Chipped-stone tools were identified and classified based on the presence of formal shaping, formal retouch and maintenance, or incidental flake scars or other wear. They were catalogued using a technology-based general morphological taxonomy that included metric and qualitative attributes. Metric attributes included maximum length, maximum width, maximum thickness, weight, and edge angle, when applicable. Qualitative attributes generally followed linear reduction models of stone tool production. These included the production stage, completeness, edge morphology, flake scar pattern, and macrowear patterning. In instances of formal projectile points, additional variables were recorded pertaining to the technological characteristic of the artifact. All formal tools were identified, when possible, to a type or style defined for the North-Central Texas region. When applicable, chipped stone tool analysis was augmented through a formal use-wear analysis. Use- wear analysis generally consists of the identification of microchipping, striations, and polishes on chipped stone tool surfaces imparted during use of the tool. The development of these phenomena is affected by several variables, including the microtopography and other properties of the lithic material, the properties of the tool use, the motion of the tool, properties of the worked material, and the presence and properties of lubricating agents or abrasives (Juel Jensen 1988; Semenov 1964; Shea 1992). Two microscopes were utilized during the use-wear analysis: an Amscope SE series stereomicroscope capable of 3x to 225x magnification and an Amscope ME series metallurgical microscope capable of 50x to 1000x magnification. Although these two microscopes are similar in function, the majority of the use- wear analysis was performed with the metallurgical microscope with light field illumination. All wear patterns were photographed using an Amscope Digital Eyepiece Camera and processed using ToupView X software. Tools examined during this analysis underwent a series of cleaning procedures. Field lab technicians cleaned the tools with soft bristle brushes and water to remove the majority of attached soil matrix. Preliminary analysis showed that further cleaning was necessary to provide a clean pallet from which use- wear could be more easily discerned. Further cleaning was performed with toothbrushes and diluted isopropyl alcohol to remove any remaining sediment and finger oils. Prior to analysis, each artifact selected for use-wear analysis was photographed. Using those photographs, the location and type of use-wear identified on each artifact was recorded. Once the cleaning was completed, the tools were analyzed under magnification. Low-powered microscopy provided an efficient means to scan each tool for general use-wear traces and their respective recording. High-powered microscopy provided a means to better understand the locations of, and differences between, polish development and edge damage. The identification of use-wear patterns used comparative data drawn from published materials (Aldenderfer et al. 1989; Aoyama 1995; Beers 2006; Keeley 1980; Odell and Odell- Vereecken 1980; Semenov 1964; Stemp 2000; Tringham et al. 1974; Yerkes 1983; Yerkes 1984; Yerkes 2009). Fire-Cracked Rock Thermally altered or FCR is a common class of archeological material throughout North America and is particularly ubiquitous in Central Texas, where dense FCR concentrations, variously labeled burned rock middens, thermal features, and rock ovens, among other terms, “have long received a large share of archeologists’ attention” due to their widespread occurrence (Collins 2004:109). Although FCR features are present at a lesser scale in North-Central Texas (for a rare exception, see Lintz et al. 2008), it is nonetheless an important material class with the potential to highlight changes in prehistoric subsistence MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 44


---

## Page 57

patterns. Recent experimental studies have produced data and models with implications beyond Central Texas, some focusing on FCR fragment morphology (e.g., Backhouse and Johnson 2007) and others on fragment size distributions as an indicator of repeated reheating (e.g., Pagoulatos 2005; Thompson and Mauldin 2008). FCR was separated based on material type (e.g., sandstone, limestone, quartzite), sorted into size classes, and the number and cumulative weight of FCR fragments within each material and size class were recorded from each archeological context. Burned Clay Masses of burned or baked clay are common at prehistoric sites in North-Central Texas and surrounding regions. Burned clay may be found as massive, fused deposits enclosing hearths or cooking pits (Osburn and Ward 2007), shaped or rounded nodules that served as boiling “stones” (Ford and Webb 1956), fallen wattle-and-daub structures (Boyd 2004), fallen plastered thatch structures (Drass 2008), intrusive or local material deliberately or accidentally trampled into a living surface, masses introduced into a living area on collected plant roots (Goldberg 2008), insect nests that can sometimes be considered as proxy indicators of human occupation (Boyd 2004), or as evidence of wild fires with no direct indication of human activity. Results of a recent experimental study suggest that much of the burned clay recovered in the region is likely due to the use of fired clay balls as heating elements in hearths or for stone boiling, a common pre-ceramic cooking technique, in an environment where readily-available stone is relatively scarce (Tinsley and Dayton 2011:111). Experimental evidence also suggested the possibility of mud-plastered thatch surfaces; however, results were inconclusive and the lack of ethnographic or archeological corroboration was noted (Tinsley and Dayton 2011:117). A basic analysis of burned clay masses was performed, including separation by the presence or absence of plant or other type of impressions and recording the cumulative weight of burned clay masses within each class from each archeological context. 4.5.2 - Faunal Analysis Faunal remains recovered during Phase II investigations from 41DL553 and 41DL554 were analyzed by James Ramsey, M.A., and Jodi Jacobson, Ph.D., at the Center for Archaeological Studies (CAS) at Texas State University. All bones were identified through the use of CAS’s comparative vertebrate osteological collection, use of loan specimens from the University of Tennessee Department of Anthropology’s Vertebrate Comparative Skeletal Collection, and assorted identification manuals (Olsen 1964, Olsen 1968, Olsen 1979, Cohen and Serjeantson 1996, Jones and Manning 1992, Romer 1997, Balkwill and Cumbaa 1992, Jacobson 2004, Ford 1990). Taxonomic classification for mammals follows Schmidly (1994), Stebbins (2003) for reptiles, and Peterson (1988) for birds. The primary goal of the faunal analysis was to identify faunal bone fragments to the most specific taxonomic level possible. Bone specimens not bearing diagnostic features but similar in size and other formal characteristics were placed within specific size categories. The categories consisted of large mammal (e.g., bison, cattle, etc.), medium-large mammal (e.g., deer, antelope, etc.), medium mammal (e.g., dog, raccoon, etc.), small mammal (e.g., rabbit, prairie dog, opossum, etc.), and very small mammal (e.g., wood rat, hispid cotton rat, vole, etc.). Size categories for birds included medium (e.g., mallard duck, caracara, red-tailed hawk) and small (e.g., dove, grackle, screech owl). Size categories for reptiles included medium (e.g., river cooter, red-eared slider) and small (e.g., box turtle, musk turtle). Assessments were also made of the following characteristics: bone element, size of fragment, fusion state, rodent gnawing, burning/butchery evidence, and number of identifiable specimens (NISP). All attempts were made to identify specimens to taxon and element. Measures of differential preservation (i.e., size, completeness, modification, taphonomy) were carried out on the faunal assemblage to assess human verses natural modifications to bone surfaces. Bone is a unique material in that it contains two major components: an inorganic component (primarily hydroxyapatite) that contributes to its rigidity and an organic component (primarily Type I collagen) that permits some degree of elasticity. After the death of an organism, the organic component gradually decomposes, ultimately MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 45


---

## Page 58

changing the biomechanical properties of the bone. Consequently, the characteristics of bone fractures in “fresh” bone (i.e., those with higher amounts of collagen) often appear very different from fractures to more “dry” bone (i.e., where the majority of collagen has decomposed; Lyman and Lyman 1994). In addition to distinguishing between fresh and dry breaks, fresh fracture types can be further divided into spiral and other green breaks (e.g., oblique fractures). For this analysis, break types were recorded as spiral, green, dry, indeterminate, or unbroken. Indeterminate break types include those with fracture characteristics that were obscured by other taphonomic processes, such as abrasion, and those with mixed fresh and dry fracture characteristics. To better assess the issue of cultural versus natural breakage, degree of completeness was assessed based on taxa size. Smaller taxa would have smaller bones and a lower size category may not indicate higher fragmentation. Whenever possible, bones were assessed for completeness using a ranking system from 0 to 6 (0 = 0 to 4 percent; 1 = 5 to 20 percent; 2 = 21 to 39 percent; 3 = 40 to 59 percent; 4 = 60 to 85 percent; 5 = 86 to 99 percent; 6 = 100 percent). In addition, data were collected regarding the identification of any human and/or natural modification to the bone such as butchering marks (i.e., cut, chop, or other modification marks such as percussion points for bone fracturing, cultural abrasion or scrape marks by tools or other cultural artifact modification, and thermal alteration). Thermal alteration that results in brown discoloration of bone is interpreted as being indicative of short-term and lower heat burning, such as exposure to a grass fire or campfire for less than 6 minutes (Lyman 1994). Blackened bone has been carbonized and would indicate prolonged exposure to a grass fire or campfire. Grey and white discolored bone has been fully calcined, which means that it has lost all organic matter and become plastically deformed. Taphonomic alterations (i.e., carnivore or rodent gnawing or evidence for consumption, root etching, and degree of weathering of the bone) were also recorded. Prior research (Klippel and Synstelien 2007) has demonstrated that brown rats only gnaw bone, which is fresh and laden with fats, while squirrels only gnaw on thicker bone cortices after fats have leached away. Simply put, rats will gnaw on recently deposited bones while squirrels are more likely to gnaw on bone left exposed for prolonged periods of time. Carnivore modification includes punctures, scoring, gnawing, and intestinal modification through digestion. The degree of weathering was recorded following Lyman’s (1994) synthesis of Behrensmeyer (1978), Andrews (1990), and Johnson (1985) research. The weathering scale consisted of rankings scale from 0 to 5, with 0 indicating that flesh was still attached to the bone, and 5 characterizing a bone with large splinters that was disintegrating. Typically, all faunal materials recovered in an archeological context would be minimally assigned a weathering rank of 2 or 3. Additionally, any characteristics on the bone identifying the age or age range (e.g., epiphyseal union, tooth eruption or wear, etc.), sex (e.g., antlers, morphometric characteristics, etc.), or seasonality of the animal were recorded, if present. A few direct indicators of seasonal site occupation are discernable from faunal remains. Seasonality is based on the season of death of various animals and can be ascertained in many ways. Age profiles can be useful for determining season if younger animals, which have a predictable season of birth, are present. The season of death can be determined by projecting back to estimated birth dates (e.g., a 6-month-old animal with standard parturition dates of May/June would indicate a death in November/December). Some animals also hibernate during cold seasons or may have seasonal migrations. Their presence within an assemblage can indicate deaths during seasons where they would be an available resource based on these known patterns. Skeletal part frequency is used to infer butchering patterns, transport decisions, food preparation, disposal habits, nutritional analysis, activity areas, site function, economic institutions, and social organization (Reitz and Wing 1999). Of particular interest are site function, transport decisions, and within site patterning and what those characteristics can indicate about the activities that took place at a site and the environment surrounding it. For example, if animals are being brought into the site from outside the local environment, the site should contain a predominance of higher utility elements of that animal. When transporting animals for food, typically only the higher and middle utility elements are transported to save MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 46


---

## Page 59

time and energy. Conversely, animals that are being procured from the immediate environment should be represented at the site by high, middle, and low utility elements. If animals are procured locally, yet being transported away, lower utility elements would be likely to occur. Specific skeletal part frequencies, standardized gross utility rankings (Jacobson 2000), and standardized marrow utility rankings (Jacobson 2000) for sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 are presented and discussed in Chapter 5. 4.5.3 - Botanical Analysis Botanical remains collected at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 during this investigation consist of opportunistically collected wood charcoal encountered during excavation and water screening of the Phase II test units and from systematically collected flotation samples from selected targeted test units and identified prehistoric cultural features. Flotation samples were processed by personnel at IES using a manual flotation system with light fraction square mesh openings of less than 0.2 millimeters (mm) and 1.1-mm square heavy fraction mesh. Charcoal and modern seeds were removed from flotation heavy fractions by personnel at IES and submitted with the light fractions to Leslie Bush, Ph.D., of Macrobotanical Analysis in Manchaca, Texas, for analysis. Flotation samples were sorted according to standard techniques (Pearsall 2015) modified for the small size of charcoal fragments present at the site. Material from heavy fractions was examined under a microscope at low magnification. Modern plant material and non-botanical items were added to the light fractions prior to sorting. Carbonized plant material from each heavy fraction was also added to its light fraction, but only after it was weighed separately from the light fraction to evaluate flotation success. Recovery of charcoal into flotation light fractions, which are caught in a mesh fine enough to collect small seeds, ranged from 0 to 25 percent. For site 41DL553, overall recovery of charred botanical specimens in the light fractions was 22 percent. For site 41DL554, overall recovery of charred botanical specimens in the light fractions was 3 percent. Flotation light fractions were weighed and placed on a stack of graduated geologic mesh along with the previously weighed heavy fraction charcoal to facilitate sorting. Material that did not fall through the largest mesh (No. 10; 2-mm square openings) was examined under a Leica S9i stereoscopic microscope at 6.1 to 55x magnification. Uncarbonized plant material and non-botanical materials (e.g., insects, soil peds) were removed, weighed, placed into a new plastic bag, and labeled. Material that fell through the 2-mm mesh was examined under the microscope, and smaller carbonized plant material was removed from those size fractions for identification and possible radiocarbon dating. In standard macrobotanical practice, wood charcoal fragments that fall through the No. 10 (2-mm) mesh are not sampled for identification. Due to the low frequency of wood charcoal fragments larger than 2 mm present within these samples, identification was attempted starting with the largest fragments in each sample and proceeding with successively smaller fragments regardless of size until fragments became too small to be snapped to observe clean transverse sections. When wood charcoal fragments could not be snapped, wood charcoal identification was sometimes possible using aspects of wood anatomy visible in longitudinal sections, notably the presence of vessels, fibers, and rays. Because of the small size of the fragments recovered from these sites, many wood charcoal fragments were identifiable only as hardwood (angiosperm clade). Botanical materials were identified to the lowest possible taxonomic level by comparison to materials in the Macrobotanical Analysis comparative collection and through the use of standard reference works (e.g., Davis 1993; Hoadley 1990; InsideWood 2004; Martin and Barkley 1961; Panshin and de Zeeuw 1980; Wheeler 2011). Plant nomenclature follows that of the PLANTS Database (USDA, NCRS 2021). After identification, specimens were labeled, recorded, and weighed on an Ohaus Scout II 200 x 0.01 g electronic balance. To retain suitability for radiometric dating, samples were placed on freshly-washed glassware when not in specimen containers, and they were handled only with latex gloves, small paintbrushes, and metal forceps. After identification, specimens were placed in a new microcentrifuge tube. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 47


---

## Page 60

4.5.4 - Geoarcheological Analysis Geoarcheological methods of investigation consisted of in-field observations and documentation of soil stratigraphic profiles that were exposed through mechanical trenching at both sites, and laboratory analyses of soil/sediment samples collected from those profiles. Soil Stratigraphic Profile Descriptions The documentation of the trench profiles consisted of photographs and the completion of a soil stratigraphic profile form. Completion of each form commenced with dividing the hand-cleaned trench wall into individual zones based on observable changes in color, soil/sediment texture, structure, and/or other meaningful differences throughout the profile. The neutral term “zone” was used to allow for pedogenic and stratigraphic variability within the profile to be recorded under a single term. Detailed soil stratigraphic descriptions were completed by the project geoarcheologist for six of the 20 trench profiles (three trench profiles at each site) and noted the thickness and depth below surface, Munsell color (moist), consistence (moist), texture (using USDA soil texture and Folk [1974] nomenclature), soil structure and ped surface features, bioturbation features, and lower boundary characteristics for each zone, following the guidelines of Schoeneberger et al. (2012). Soil/Sediment Laboratory Analysis Soil/sediment samples were collected at 10-cm intervals from columns of two of the three trench profiles described at each site. The samples were collected in 8-milliliter (ml) clear plastic cubes measuring 2 cm by 2 cm by 2 cm and were submitted to Dr. Charles Frederick for measurements of magnetic susceptibility, particle size, and calcium carbonate (calcium carbonate equivalent [CCE]). A paired set of samples was submitted to the W. M. Keck – National Science Foundation Paleoenvironmental and Environmental Stable Isotope Laboratory at the University of Kansas in Lawrence for measurements of organic carbon (%C) and nitrogen (%N) and stable isotopes of carbon (δ13C) and nitrogen (δ15N) on soil organic matter. 4.5.4.2.1 - Magnetic Susceptibility Magnetic susceptibility analysis is a rapid, nondestructive technique that is based upon the principle that magnetic minerals become more strongly magnetized in the presence of a weak external magnetic field; and is essentially a measure of the degree to which a sample may be magnetized (Dalan and Bannerjee 1998:6). The magnetic susceptibility of a soil/sediment sample is dependent upon the mineralogy, the concentration of magnetic minerals present, and the size and shape of the magnetic mineral grains (Dalan and Bannerjee 1998; Dalan 2008). This property is sometimes useful in assessing the degree of soil development (i.e., the formation of topsoil or A horizon) as well as identifying the stratigraphic position of former cultural occupation surfaces owing to enhancement of the magnetic susceptibility by increasing the amount of highly magnetic minerals through anthropogenic activities such as cooking, hearths, and campfires. The soil/sediment samples were first weighed and then measured for magnetic susceptibility on a Bartington MS2 meter using a MS2B sensor in SI units on the 0.01 sensitivity setting. From those data the mass corrected low frequency magnetic susceptibility (X ) was calculated. The units for the X values lf lf presented are 10-8m3kg-1. 4.5.4.2.2 - Particle Size Analysis The soil/sediment samples were then split for particle size analysis and assay of the calcium carbonate content. The particle size analysis was performed on a Beckman-Coulter LS 13 320 multi-wavelength laser particle size analyzer. Samples were pretreated to disperse the clays by soaking overnight in a 5 percent solution of sodium hexametaphosphate. Organic matter that may bind particles together was then removed by adding sodium hypochlorite (bleach) and boiling the samples on a hotplate for approximately 20 minutes. The samples were then introduced to the Aqueous Liquid Module of the analyzer and the MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 48


---

## Page 61

concentration was diluted, if necessary. From that analysis, the percentages of sand, silt, and clay for the <2-mm size fraction was determined, as well as various descriptive statistics of the particle size distribution such as the mean particle size, sorting (standard deviation), skewness, and kurtosis. 4.5.4.2.3 - Calcium Carbonate Equivalent (CCE) A small (1.7 gram [g]) portion of the <2-mm matrix of each soil/sediment sample was then dried overnight at 105˚C, subsequently ground in a mortar and pestle, and used to determine the CCE by means of a Chittick apparatus (Dreimanis 1962; Machette 1986). The dried and ground samples were weighed and placed into a small (250 ml) Erlenmeyer flask. Once attached to the Chittick apparatus, the liquid level in the measuring burette was set to -10 ml, then the stopcock was closed to prevent off gassing, and the leveling bulb was lowered to establish a vacuum inside the sample chamber. Subsequently, the barometric pressure and room temperature were recorded. Then 10 ml of 50 percent hydrochloric acid (HCl) was delivered to the sample flask and was periodically agitated until the reaction was complete (about 1 to 2 minutes). The leveling bulb was then raised to the elevation of the liquid level of the sample in the burette. The volume of gas evolved was then measured and the CCE was calculated. 4.5.4.2.4 - Soil Organic Matter and Stable Isotope Analysis Measurements of organic C, N, δ13C, and δ15N were obtained using a Costech 4010 elemental analyzer in conjunction with a Finnigan MAT 253 Stable Isotope Ratio Mass Spectrometer from Thermo Electron Corporation on a matching set of soil/sediment samples. Carbonates were removed from the samples through dissolution prior to analysis. 4.6 Curation All artifacts encountered during fieldwork were collected, identified, tabulated by provenience, and analyzed. Selected stylistically, temporally, and functionally diagnostic artifacts and representative samples of non-diagnostic artifacts were photographed. Project-related records, field notes, forms, photographs, and other documentation will be permanently stored at the IES office in McKinney, Texas. All collected artifacts were temporarily stored at IES office in McKinney, or IES subconsultant offices, and returned to the landowner after completion of the Section 106 of the NHPA process. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 49

CHAPTER 5: RESULTS Prehistoric archeological sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 were previously identified and documented during a Phase I intensive archeological survey conducted within the MacArthur Reclamation project area (Goodmaster et al. 2019). Both sites comprise deeply-buried prehistoric cultural deposits within the sediments of the West Fork Trinity River floodplain. Due to project-related impacts and the potential for the sites to contain important archeological information, the eligibility of both sites for inclusion in the NRHP was evaluated. During this investigation, mechanically-excavated site delineation trenches were conducted at each site to confirm the extents of the sites within the project area. In addition, a series of exploratory trenches were excavated to further identify and assess archeological features, horizontal and vertical artifact frequencies and concentrations, and to expand the overall subsurface exposure at each site to facilitate both the geoarcheological analysis of the sites and the placement of Phase II controlled excavation units. This chapter summarizes the mechanical and controlled manual excavations, as well as the analyses of each artifact class and prehistoric cultural features encountered at each site. Due to the similarities and general continuity of the West Fork Trinity River floodplain sediments across the two sites, the geoarcheological analysis section of this chapter provides a summary and synthesis of the overall geoarcheological context of the sites. 5.1 Site 41DL553 5.1.1 - Previous Investigations Site 41DL553 pertains to a prehistoric habitation site, initially identified as a logistical or foraging camp, located near the eastern margin of the project area. The site is within the proposed building footprint and grading limits of the proposed project. The site was tentatively delineated during the Phase I survey as extending approximately 209 ft (63 m) north-to-south by 260 ft (80 m) east-to-west to encompass 0.86 ac (Figure 5.1). At that time, the site boundary was established by the excavation of negative Phase I survey trenches to the west and the presence of two large emergent herbaceous wetlands, which occupied surface depressions likely representing abandoned channel meanders of the West Fork Trinity River, located to the north and southwest of the site (Figures 5.2 and 5.3). The eastern portion of the site boundary was based on the property fence and the adjacent MacArthur Boulevard road berm. Based on the Phase I survey data, it was concluded that 41DL553 consisted of a prehistoric occupation within a cumulic paleosol, likely the West Fork paleosol, the top of which ranged in depth from 32 to 74 cmbs. One thermal feature, representing the possible remnants of a hearth or roasting pit comprising a thin lens of disaggregated burned clay fragments, was encountered within Phase I survey Trench 29 at a depth of 150 to 160 cmbs. Cultural materials encountered at the site during the Phase I survey included chipped lithic artifacts (n=3), faunal remains (deer [n>20], fish [n=1], waterfowl [n=1], and mussel shell [n>30]), burned clay fragments (n>50), and fire-cracked rocks (n>20). Based on the Phase I survey data, the archeological deposits at site 41DL553 appeared to be concentrated in northern portion of the site and were relatively more shallow in the northeastern portion of the site (136 to 150 cmbs) and deeper in the northwestern portion of the site (150 to 200 cmbs). 5.1.2 - Current Investigations Additional Site Delineation Trench Results During the current investigation, IES conducted eight additional site delineation trenches at 41DL553 to confirm the site boundary within the project area (Figure 5.4; Appendix A.1). Three of the trenches (Trenches 34, 35, and 39) were positive for prehistoric cultural deposits and the boundary of site 41DL553 within the project area was expanded approximately 40 to 50 m north and northwest west of the Phase I site boundary. Two of the positive site delineation trenches (Trenches 34 and 39) were located north of the Phase I site boundary within a subtle topographic swale occupied by a seasonally inundated, emergent MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 51

## Page 68
