The frequency of prehistoric cultural artifacts identified within Trenches 34 and 39 was minimal, comprising several fragments of FCR, mussel shell fragments, a mammal bone fragment, and unmodified cobbles at a depth range of approximately 100 to 180 cmbs. The third positive site delineation trench (Trench 35) was excavated along the elevated portion of the landform, west of the initial positive Phase I survey Trenches 8 and 29. Within Trench 35, several fragments of FCR and burned clay fragments were observed at 150 to 160 cmbs, depths consistent with the depths of observed cultural materials within the Phase I survey Trench 29, which had been previously excavated approximately 30 m to the west. Phase II Exploratory Trench Results Three Phase II exploratory trenches were excavated at 41DL553 (Trenches 40, 41, and 42; see Figure 5.4; Appendix A.2). Phase II exploratory trench locations were selected to further identify and assess archeological features, horizontal and vertical artifact frequencies and concentrations, and to expand the overall subsurface exposure at the site to facilitate both the geoarcheological analysis of the site and the placement of Phase II controlled excavation units. The placement of the exploratory trenches generally coincided with the central portion of the site, which occupied a slightly elevated, generally level portion of the floodplain flanked by gentle swales or depressions occupied by emergent herbaceous wetlands to the north and south. Phase II Initial Test Unit Results Two initial 1-m-by-1-m test units (Test Unit [TU] 1 and 2) were excavated at site 41DL553 to more accurately and adequately sample vertical artifact distributions in proximity to the area of highest artifact frequencies and potential prehistoric cultural features encountered during the previous Phase I site investigation. Each of the initial test units were manually excavated within the profile of a Phase II exploratory trench, specifically Trenches 40 and 42 (see Figure 5.4). The initial test units were excavated in 10-cm levels from the modern ground surface to a depth of 20 cm below the deepest encountered cultural artifacts identified during both the previous Phase I investigation and the current Phase II investigation. The goal of the initial test units was to confirm the vertical limits of the cultural deposits at the site and to identify any additional cultural horizons located above or below the cultural deposits previously identified at 41DL553 (Table 5.1). The results of the initial test unit excavations at site 41DL553 confirmed that the archeological deposits present at the site contained higher artifact frequencies within the central portion of the site, centered along the narrow, generally level landform situated between the emergent wetlands to the north and south of the site (as initially identified in Phase I survey Trenches 8 and 29; see Figure 5.1). However, excavation of the initial test units also served to update and inform the previous understanding of the depth and distribution of artifacts within the site matrix. The systematic, manual excavation of the larger soil volume from the initial test units (in comparison to the systematic screening of excavated trench fill during the Phase I survey investigation), coupled with the increased rate of artifact recovery via deflocculation and water screening, led to the assessment that archeological deposits extended to slightly greater depths (approximately 230 cmbs) than previously expected at the site, in addition to the identification of a more shallow, relatively sparse occupation at a depth range of approximately 40 to 140 cmbs, primarily identified within TU 2. Targeted Test Units Following excavation of the two initial test units at the site, the culturally-sterile overburden was mechanically-stripped from the ground surface to allow for excavation of the subsequent six targeted test units at the site (see Figure 5.3). The mechanical removal of overburden was visually monitored, and the overburden deposits were periodically sampled during the mechanical removal process for screening through 0.25-in hardware mesh to monitor for the presence of artifacts. Features 5.1.2.5.1 - Feature 3 The presence of a potential thermal feature was initially noted during the mechanical excavation of exploratory Trench 41. The potential feature comprised a cluster of several fragments of FCR within a dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) silty clay loam soil matrix at a depth of approximately 245 cmbs. A pair of targeted test units (TUs 3 and 4) were placed adjacent to exploratory Trench 41 to target the potential feature via controlled, manual excavation. The presence of the feature was confirmed and designated as Feature 3 during manual excavation of TU 4. During excavation of TU 4, an increase in the frequency of FCR was observed within Level 22 (215 to 225 cmbs) and an amorphous area of slightly darker, very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) soil matrix was noted within Level 23 (225 to 235 cmbs). By a depth of 239 cmbs within Level 24 (235 to 245 cmbs), Feature 3 was designated as a very diffuse matrix-defined feature that contained a cluster of FCR comingled with several fragments of burned clay, charcoal, and ash (Figure 5.5). Feature 3 was then bisected along an east-to-west bisection line, and excavation of the south half of the feature continued to expose a profile of the north half of the portion of the feature within TU 4. Feature 3 was generally lenticular in cross-section and extended to a maximum depth of 252 cmbs. Both the north half and the south half of the feature were removed separately and retained for flotation processing. ## Page 71

Feature 3 is interpreted as a former hearth feature that has been largely disaggregated by vertic soil movement. The Feature 3 fill yielded 1.99 kg of FCR, 58.02 g of burned clay, two pieces of chipped lithic debitage, 0.15 g of faunal bone, and 0.15 g of charcoal. Incorporated into the FCR thermal elements of the feature were two fragmentary groundstone artifacts. Those artifacts likely served as plant processing tools prior to being recycled as heating elements in the hearth after their use-life was expended due to breakage (Figure 5.6). Subsequent artifact analysis of the two excavation levels above Feature 3 indicated that 670.5 g of FCR were recovered in TU 4 Level 22 (215 to 225 cmbs), indicating a 12.5-fold increase in FCR volume from the previous level. A comparable amount of FCR (696.2 g) was recovered in Level 23 (225 to 235 cmbs), with most of the FCR (559.3 g) from that level recovered from the amorphous area of slightly darker (10YR 3.2) soil matrix. All of the FCR within and associated with Feature 3 (i.e., within TU 4 Levels 22 through 24) was sandstone, likely derived from the nearby Woodbine Formation, which can be readily collected from sand and gravel bar deposits within and along the channel of the West Fork Trinity River. Most FCR fragments (n=305; 0.98 kg) recovered within test unit excavation Levels 22, 23, 24, and within the Feature 3 fill were less than 4 cm in maximum dimension, in part owing to the friable nature of the Woodbine sandstone, but also suggestive that these heating elements had been reused several times resulting in a higher degree of fragmentation. By comparison, much fewer fragments but a higher total weight of FCR (n=25; 1.33 kg) was comprised of larger fragments measuring between 4 and 9 cm in maximum dimension. Only three fragments (totaling 0.96 kg) are between 9 and 15 cm in maximum dimension. Incidentally, one of the larger heating elements associated with Feature 3 was the large, ovoid groundstone mano fragment (13.1 cm in maximum dimension; 671 g; see Figure 5.6A) from within the partially intact portion of the feature along with the small fragment of the possible metate (see Figure 5.6B). Figure 5.6: Groundstone artifact fragments from Feature 3 (239–252 cmbs) at 41DL553. The faunal remains from Feature 3 consisted of 11 fragments of very small mammal bones. Most of the bones were burned, but lacked any other indications of cultural modification. While it is possible that the occupants of 41DL553 may have augmented their subsistence by roasting small rodents that were MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 59

## Page 72

opportunistically captured or trapped, these bones may reflect natural inclusions in the soil matrix that were inadvertently burned during use of the hearth. The charcoal recovered through flotation processing of the Feature 3 sediment fill yielded charred acorn (Quercus sp.) nutshell fragments (n=20; 0.01 g), a burned bedstraw (Galium sp.) seed (n=1; <0.01 g), wood charcoal consisting of mulberry (Morus sp.; n=1; <0.01 g), American elm (Ulmus americana; n=2; 0.02 g), ash (Fraxinus spp.; n=7; 0.03 g), and other indeterminate hardwood charcoal (n=1; 0.01 g). As detailed below in Section 5.1.3.4, Feature 3 is the only context at site 41DL553 to produce evidence of acorn usage at the site. While acorns may have been inadvertently added to a campfire through the use of oak as a fuel wood, the groundstone implements recovered within Feature 3 suggest that acorns may have been processed as a food source by the inhabitants of 41DL553. Bedstraw, as the common name suggests, is a bur that may have been burned as a convenient means of disposing of the nuisance plant; however, bedstraw has medicinal uses and was known among historic tribes to treat a variety of ailments, especially those related to urinary tract or kidney problems (Moerman 1998). Due to a failed attempt to produce a radiocarbon date from a point-plotted charcoal fragment removed from Feature 3 at a depth of 241 cmbs during manual excavation of the feature, the entire sample of ash wood charcoal (0.03 g) recovered from flotation of the feature fill, was submitted in a second attempt to radiocarbon date the feature. That attempt produced a calibrated radiocarbon age range of 2788 to 2818 cal B.P. (conventional age of 2650 +/-30 B.P.), placing the use of Feature 3 firmly within the Late Archaic period of the upper Trinity River basin. 5.1.3 - Artifact Analysis Chipped Lithic Artifact Analysis During Phase II investigations at the site, 488 chipped lithic artifacts were recovered at 41DL553 (Table 5.2). The majority (n=479; 98 percent) of these artifacts consisted of unmodified debitage; however, hafted bifacial tools (typically projectile points and/or knives; n=3), expedient tools (n=2), bifacial cores (n=1), and hammerstones (n=3) are also represented. Table 5.2: Summary of Chipped Lithic Artifacts Recovered in Test Units at 41DL553 Debitage Tools Excavation Biface Core Tool Projectile Expedient Unit Thinning Reduction Rejuvenation Shatter Point Tools Core Hammerstone Total TU 1 14 48 50 208 – 1 – – 321 TU 2 1 9 8 34 1 – 1 2 56 TU 3 1 – – 6 1 – – – 8 TU 4 2 1 2 5 1 – – – 11 TU 5 – 1 – – – – – – 1 TU 6 – 1 1 3 – – – 1 6 TU 7 5 28 5 40 – – – – 78 TU 8 – – 7 – – – – – 7 Total 23 88 73 296 3 1 1 3 488 The 41DL553 chipped lithic assemblage was manufactured from Ogallala gravels (98 percent), chert (1 percent), and quartzite (1 percent). Ogallala gravels (sometimes referred to as Uvalde gravels based on similar deposits in South Texas) are silicified arenaceous sediments utilized in the manufacturing of chipped stone tools across the Great Plains and surrounding regions. They consist of upland Tertiary-age gravel deposits of the Miocene to early Pliocene Ogallala Formation (Hurst 2010). Ogallala gravels exhibit a range of yellow, red, and gray hues with fine-grained to coarse-grained crystalline structures. The lack of chert sources within the North-Central Texas region is well documented in previous archeological investigations (Ferring and Yates 1997; Fields et al. 1997; Goodmaster et al. 2015; McGregor et al. 1996; Prikryl 1990; Skinner and Baird 1985). Morphological categories of the chipped lithic debitage indicate the occupants of 41DL553 favored a multidirectional core reduction strategy. ## Page 73

was recovered within Phase II excavation units at 41DL553. Approximately 38 percent (n=184) were identified as unmodified flakes (e.g., consisting of a discernable platform and bulb of percussion). The remaining 62 percent (n=296) of the total pieces were identified as shatter (angular debris and distal flake fragments). Core reduction flakes comprised approximately 48 percent (n=88) of the chipped lithic debitage recovered at the site, and tool rejuvenation flakes represent 39 percent (n=73). Of note, one bifacial overshot flake was recovered that featured the distal tip of the objective biface. The remaining flakes consisted of biface thinning flakes, representing 13 percent (n=23) of the overall debitage assemblage. Approximately 30 percent (n=56) of the flake assemblage consisted of primary and secondary flakes. The majority of those flakes (n=47; 84 percent) ranged in size from 12.7 mm (0.75 in) and 19 mm (1 in). Considerable curvature was present in the majority of the flakes. Multidirectional ventral flake scarring was prevalent on secondary and tertiary flakes. Single and multifaceted platforms were equally represented at the site. Based on those factors, the occupants at the site likely utilized local lithic sources (comprised primarily of small Ogallala gravels and cobbles that were likely opportunistically collected in eroded interfluvial upland settings or within stream channel gravel bar deposits) to manufacture smaller-sized formal and informal tools from multidirectional core blanks. This is further supported by the recovery of a single bifacial core and three hammerstones at the site (Figure 5.7). The formal bifacial core, recovered from TU 2 at a depth of 130 to 140 cmbs, featured a maximum linear dimension of 67.82 mm and weighed 206.57 g (see Figure 5.7A). It exhibited flake scarring indicative of early-stage bifacial reduction. Within the same level, two hammerstones were recovered. The two hammerstones exhibited a series of pecking indentations on pronounced ridges, weighed 206.57 g and 263.04 g, respectively, and exhibited similar maximum linear dimensions as the bifacial core (see Figure 5.7B and C). A fourth hammerstone, which consisted of a rounded cobble that weighed 96.27 g, was recovered within TU 6 at a depth range of 150 to 160 cmbs (see Figure 5.7D). The relatively small nature of the core and hammerstones supports the reliance of lithic production on the locally abundant, moderately-sized Ogallala gravels typically available along interfluvial upland ridges and within stream gravels across the region. Later stage reduction activities are also prevalent in the 41DL553 lithic assemblage. The biface thinning flakes (n=23) were small to moderate in size, featuring maximum dimensions averaging less than 12.7 mm (0.75 in). They generally exhibited slight curvature with abraded platforms. Tool rejuvenation flakes (n=72) were also recovered. Those flakes were absent of cortex and featured slight curvature and abraded platforms. The significant quantity of small, thin bifacial thinning flakes and tool rejuvenation flakes indicate that informal and formal tool production was a prevalent practice occurring alongside of initial core reduction at the site. Across the site, chipped lithic debitage exhibited two general vertical trends. The easternmost portion of the site (containing TUs 1 and 7) featured a prominently higher frequency of chipped lithic debitage that exhibited a bimodal distribution with a prominent peak at 190 to 200 cmbs and a skew with depth (Figure 5.8A). A minor peak was also observed at 140 cmbs. Tertiary flakes were recovered in that level, whereas primary and secondary flakes were encountered between 160 to 200 cmbs. In the southern portion of the site (containing TUs 2 and 8), a much lower frequency of secondary and tertiary flakes (relative to TUs 1 and 7) was concentrated between 80 and 100 cmbs (Figure 5.8B). The central portion of the site (containing TUs 3 and 4) contained a single secondary flake and a very low frequency of tertiary flakes (n=5) ranging from 140 to 170 cmbs, although a single bifacial thinning flake was encountered at 245 cmbs (Figure 5.8C). The western portion of the site (TUs 5 and 6) similarly contained a very low frequency of chipped lithic debitage (n=3), consisting of a primary flake, a secondary flake, and a tertiary flake between 130 and 150 cmbs (Figure 5.8D). Based on the trends observed, it is likely that the eastern portion of the site was the focus of lithic tool production and maintenance at the site; however, lithic reduction activities occurred throughout the site to some degree. The chipped lithic debitage frequency distributions across 41DL553 also indicate that a minor, more shallow site component is present in the southernmost portion of the site, as evidenced by the distribution of chipped lithic debitage in TUs 2 and 8 (see Figure 5.8B). MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 61

Four formal and informal chipped stone tools were recovered from 41DL553, which included three projectile points and one expedient flake implement (Figure 5.9). An arrow point was recovered from TU 2 between 50 to 60 cmbs, which consists of a partial projectile point represented by a portion of the blade, the stem, and a single barb (see Figure 5.9A). The lateral edges of the blade are incurvate and slightly serrated. The rounded barb protrudes approximately 6.49 mm from the 7.28-mm-wide neck. The point features a slightly expanding, slightly convex base that measures 7.78 mm in width. The maximum thickness of the point is 4.12 mm. Well-developed polish and moderate edge rounding were observed microscopically along the lateral edges. No striations were observed. Based on the use-wear analysis, the point was moderately used on soft, greasy materials (Figure 5.10). This artifact is consistent with the previously-defined Catahoula type, which dates to the Late Prehistoric period in North-Central and North- East Texas (Turner and Hester 1999:206). A small dart point was recovered from TU 3 between 130 to 140 cmbs (see Figure 5.9B). The point is generally complete, with a minor portion of the distal tip absent due to the presence of an impact fracture. The point is extensively retouched and features straight, but slightly asymmetrical, lateral edges with shallow, ground notches. The convex base and expanding stem measures 16.85 mm wide. The point has a maximum thickness of 5.44 mm, and a neck width of 11.76 mm. Well-developed polish extending from the flake scar ridges and lateral edges was microscopically observed across most of the blade. Edge rounding was also prevalent. No striations were observed. Based on the use-wear analysis, the point was extensively used on soft, greasy materials following significant retouch (Figure 5.11). This artifact is consistent with a range of projectile point types that have been previously identified in the North-Central Texas region as Ensor, Ellis, or Trinity types (Anthony and Brown 1994; Prikryl 1990; Story et al. 1990; Shipp et al. 2009; Suhm and Jelks 1962). Crook (2020:89) indicates that the Trinity point type has been largely misidentified and temporally misattributed within the Trinity River basin and provides a revised typological description to remedy the identification and temporal association of that point type. This point is slightly smaller than the typical Trinity point specimen and there is no indication that it is within an Early MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 63

## Page 78

Archaic temporal context. The Ellis point type dates to the Middle to Late Archaic period (Turner and Hester 1999:190). A small dart point was recovered from TU 4 between 190 to 200 cmbs (see Figure 5.9C). The point was complete but relatively crudely made, with large flake scars and a maximum thickness of 10.61 mm. The point featured a maximum length of 42.32 mm and a maximum width of 23.45 mm. The body was triangular in shape with generally straight, but slightly asymmetrical, lateral edges measuring 27.89 mm long. The point exhibited squared shoulders measuring 8.45 mm wide. The contracting, rounded stem measured 11.69 mm wide and 12.67 mm long. The point featured poorly-developed polish localized on the edges and on a few of the prominent ridges between flake scars. Based on the lack of use-wear polish, a proposed worked material could not be ascertained (Figure 5.12). This artifact is consistent with the previously-defined Gary point type, which dates to the Middle to Late Archaic periods in North-Central and North-East Texas (Turner and Hester 1999:123). One flake recovered at 41DL553, from TU 1 at a depth of 120 to 130 cmbs, exhibited signs of edge modification and use (see Figure 5.9D). The modified flake implement was produced on a tertiary flake. It featured a protruding tip with unifacial microflaking and edge rounding indicative of light scraping. Figure 5.12: Use-wear analysis of projectile point recovered at 41DL553. Fire-cracked Rock During Phase II investigations at the site, 4.9 kg of FCR were recovered at 41DL553 (Table 5.3). All FCR identified at the site was composed of friable sandstone derived from the nearby Woodbine Formation. With the exception of the FCR within and associated with Feature 3, identified in TU 4 (see Section 1.1.2.5 above), all FCR encountered at the site was generally observed as small (<4 cm in maximum dimension), isolated fragments at varying frequencies within the excavated site matrix. When reviewed in aggregate by pairs of adjacent test units, a few general patterns are discernable (Figure 5.13). Faunal Analysis The total vertebrate number of identified specimens (NISP) recovered from the 41DL553 during the Phase II investigation was 451, of which 400 (88.7 percent) were identifiable to class. The dominate class of vertebrate fauna represented in the assemblages was mammal, accounting for 327 (81.8 percent) of the bones identifiable to class. In addition, reptiles (n=70; 17.5 percent) and birds (n=3; >0.01 percent) accounted for the other classes of remains present. A detailed account of taxa identified and the NISP for each taxon are listed in Table 5.4. Overall, diversity of the assemblage appears generally low with a primary focus on deer, large bovids, rabbit, and turtle; however, small amounts of canid, carnivore, rodent, and hawk or falcon along with a diversity in size range of mammal bone present. Bovid remains were not complete enough to definitively differentiate between bison and cattle; however, all bovid remains were recovered from TU 2 at a depth of 170 to 180 cmbs. Thus, it is assumed that those remains predate the introduction of cattle (Bos taurus). The large bovid remains are, therefore, assumed to be bison (Bison bison). The assemblage was highly fragmentary and only five bones (1.1 percent of the assemblage) were complete. Those five bones included unfused distal condyles of a deer metapodial (n=2), a deer or pronghorn sesamoid (n=1), a rabbit phalanx (n=1), and an unfused femoral head belonging to a small mammal (n=1). The majority of the bones in the assemblage were clustered in the smaller size catagories (216 less than 1 cm, 217 from 1 to 3 cm, and 18 from 3 to 6 cm). Despite 68.5 percent of the bones recovered from the site being represented by medium or larger mammals (n=309), no single bone fragment was greater than 6 cm in size and 96 percent of the overall assemblage was less than 3 cm in size. The high degree of bone fragmentation at the site is typically indicative of intentional bone breakage associated with bone grease rendering. Almost all of the faunal bones (n=446, 98.9 percent) exhibited a break or fracture of some kind. Break types included dry breaks (n=367, 81.4 percent), green breaks (n=46, 10.2 percent), spiral fractures (n=6, 1.3 percent), and indeterminate breaks (n=26, 5.8 percent). Spiral fractures were identified on a deer MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 68

(deer) metacarpal 2 femur 1 Odocoileus/Antilocapra sp. (deer/pronghorn) humerus 1 tibia 1 Mammalia cf. humerus 1 c.f. Leporidae (rabbit) long bone 13 Medium-large Mammal long bone 8 Medium/Medium-large Mammal long bone 11 Small-medium/Medium Mammal long bone 1 Small Mammal long bone 1 Very small Mammal long bone 2 long bone 1 Vertebrata Miscellaneous Vertebrate indeterminate 1 TOTAL 46 To better assess the issue of cultural versus natural bone breakage, the degree of completeness was assessed based on taxa size (Table 5.7; Figure 5.14). As smaller taxa have smaller bones, the higher frequencies of bones within the lower size categories may not indicate higher fragmentation rates. Whenever possible, bones were assessed for completeness from 0 to 6 (0=0 to 4 percent; 1=5 to 19 percent; 2=20 to 39 percent; 3=40 to 59 percent; 4=60 to 84 percent; 5=85 to 99 percent; 6=100 percent). Not all bones were able to be assessed for completeness as many bones were unable to be assigned to element; therefore, the original size of the bone could not be determined. Given the variation in completeness where ratios are more evenly matched for degree of completion of very small and small mammal bones and more weighted towards greater completeness in small-medium and medium bird bones, the lesser degree of completeness with the larger taxa groups is indicative that they were exposed to a higher degree of fragmentation prior to deposition. Additionally, any characteristics on the bone identifying the age of the animal (e.g., epiphyseal union, tooth eruption or wear, etc.) or sex (e.g., antlers, morphometric characteristics, etc.) were recorded if present. In terms of cultural modification associated with butchering, seven bones exhibited cutmarks (1.6 percent) and one bone exhibited a chop mark (0.2 percent). No percussion impacts were recorded within the 41DL553 faunal assemblage. One of the bones that exhibited a modification was a talon (distal phalanx of the foot) belonging to hawk or falcon (Accipitriformes or Falconiformes). The modification was classified as a “cut” but was a controlled cut/chop through the relatively thin bird bone consistent with scoring (cutting) and pressure to transect the bone during bead production (Figure 5.15). It was more controlled than a chop and the likely tool was a chipped stone knife or flake blade, so the modification was labelled as a “cut” in this case. The cut bisecting the bone was located just below the proximal articular surface suggesting that the talon was intentionally removed from the foot of the bird, possibly for use as an adornment or jewelry. A chop mark was also identified on a first phalanx of a deer (Odocoileus sp.) and bisected the phalanx cleanly in half. ## Page 83

Figure 5.15: Hawk or falcon talon fragment exhibiting cut mark from 41DL553. A total of 81 bones within the 41DL553 faunal assemblage have been thermally modified. Of the burned bone, 60 exhibited some browning (74.1 percent), seven were blackened (8.6 percent), eight were grayed (9.9 percent), and six had been calcined (7.4 percent). Brown discoloration is indicative of short-term and lower heat burning, such as exposure to a grass fire or campfire for less than 6 minutes (Lyman 1994). Blackened bone has been carbonized and indicates prolonged exposure to a grass fire or campfire. Grey and white bone has been completely calcined, which means that it has lost all organic matter and has become plastically deformed. Some of the bone with browning had localized surficial browning with microfractures and splintering. The form of brown thermal alteration is similar to observations in faunal assemblages at other sites in the region, such as at the nearby Fish Creek Slough (41DL436) site (Jacobson 2014). The browning in that instance was indicative of an indirect heat source, such as a heated rock that had been placed on the fresh bone, searing it, and causing micro-fractures while weakening the bone. The hypothesis presented by Jacobson (2014) to describe similar marks in the Fish Creek Slough faunal assemblage was that placement of heated rocks on the fresh bone surfaces facilitated easier bone breakage for marrow extraction. Five bones within the 41DL553 faunal assemblage exhibit this type of brown thermal alteration and fracture pattern. In terms of taxa, this modification was identified on deer (n=1), large bovid (n=1), and medium/medium-large mammal (n=3). There was only minor evidence for carnivore modification (n=15, 3.3 percent) and rodent gnawing (n=6, 1.3 percent) of the bone within the 41DL553 faunal assemblage. Of the rodent-gnawed bone, all teeth marks were mouse-sized. Research by Klippel and Synstelien (2007) has demonstrated that brown rats only gnaw bone that is fresh and still laden with fats, while squirrels only gnaw on thicker bone cortices after fats have leached away. Rats will gnaw on recently-deposited bones. Squirrels are more likely to gnaw on bone that has been left exposed for a prolonged time. Given the dominance of mice-gnawed bone, MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 71

## Page 84

the 41DL553 faunal data suggest gnawing activity while bone was fresh with minimal time of surface exposure prior to burial, which is not unexpected in a floodplain environment. Carnivore modification of the 41DL553 faunal assemblage is summarized in Table 5.8. Carnivore modification was identified on 15 bones (3.3 percent of the assemblage). Modification included punctures, scoring, gnawing, and intestinal modification through digestion. The relatively low amount of carnivore modification exhibited by the assemblage suggests that local scavengers were unable to access the vast majority the bones prior to their deposition further supporting the hypothesis that the material was buried relatively quickly. In general, the carnivore-modified bone consists of a higher percentage of slightly larger bone fragments for each size class than the overall bone fragments at the site (Figure 5.16). In addition, no faunal bones greater than 6 cm in size were recovered at the site, suggesting a high degree of fragmentation prior to disposal of the bone and/or consumption by carnivores. from 41DL553 is summarized in Figures 5.17 and 5.18. The majority (n=395, 87.6 percent) of the bone recovered from 41DL553 was assigned a weathering rank of 3. Overall, this pattern of weathering is consistent with discarded bone exposed for short periods of time on the ground surface before burial. It should be noted that of the 29 bones with a weathering ranking of 3 to 4, six (20.7 percent) of those were antler. Deer shed their antlers annually. Consequently, the slightly advanced weathering of antler could indicate that the antler tines were naturally dropped and experienced some degree of weathering prior to being collected and brought to the site, presumably to be used as tools or modified for cultural use. 100% 90% 80% 70% 60% P S IN 50% % 40% 30% 20% 10% 0% 2 2-3 3 3-4 4 Weathering Stage Figure 5.17: Weathering stage ranking frequency of all taxa at 41DL553. 100% 80% 2 60% P 2-3 S IN 3 % 40% 3-4 4 20% 0% Trench 20 TU 1 TU 2 TU 3 TU 4 TU 5 TU 6 TU 7 TU 8 Figure 5.5.18: Distribution of weathered bone by excavation unit at 41DL553. 5.1.3.3.1 - Mortality and Seasonality The presence of turtles (n=70, 15.5 percent of assemblage) indicates that site 41DL553 was used during warmer seasons. Turtles hibernate at temperatures below 38 to 45°F (Roots 2006). ## Page 86

in early November. Therefore, turtle presence is most likely associated with occupation between late March to early November, but in a given year may represent an earlier or later occupation. Although bird remains were present, they were not identifiable beyond order and therefore offer no information regarding seasonal migration patterns. Ageable deer bones present at within the site 41DL533 faunal assemblage are summarized in Table 5.9. Deer reproductive cycles are based on annual photoperiodic cues based on the location of their birth. Given modern and historic reproductive cycles for white-tailed deer births occurring during May to June (Jacobson 1994), the timing of parturition for deer from the assemblage is assumed to be consistent. Wear rates for the molars and premolars indicate deer between 20 and 22 months old within the faunal assemblage, suggesting a winter/spring occupation between January and April. In addition, a bone identified as a pelvis and exhibiting a high degree of porosity indicates deer between 9 and 10 months, also suggesting a late winter/spring occupation between February and early April. Though species of the teeth and pelvis were not able to be established and mule deer reproductive dates can differ slightly from white-tailed deer, in general, parturition of the two species is typically synchronic or only slightly offset in areas where the two species overlap (Whittaker and Lindzey 1999). Although deer antler was present in the assemblage, none was in velvet or still attached to cranial bones. Since deer drop their antlers annually after rut, they are frequently gathered from ground surface for tool use and may not indicate subsistence-based activity and therefore cannot be assigned to a season. Table 5.9: Ageable Deer Elements at 41DL553 Taxa (Common Name) Element Age NISP c.f. Odocoileus virginianus (white-tailed deer) unfused distal condyle of a metacarpal <20 months 2 Odocoileus sp. (deer) molar approx. 20 months 1 molar 20–22 months 8 premolar approx. 22 months 1 cf. Odocoileus sp. (deer) pelvis; small and porous 9–10 months 8 fully fused distal condyle of a first phalanx >2 years 1 Total 21 In addition to seasonality, the ageable deer remains reveal information on the age structure of the deer herd. Research by Wolverton et al. (2008) indicates that, as harvest pressure increases (either through increasing human population or decreasing prey population), the age structure of the resulting faunal assemblage should become juvenile-dominated expressing relatively steep survivorship. The deer assemblage at 41DL553 is juvenile-dominated and, based on the model by Wolverton et al., suggests that local deer herds were insufficiently large enough to produce adequate prime-aged deer to provision the local human populations. Hunters were adapting to this changing herd dynamic by hunting subprime-aged deer. Overall, deer remains suggest a late winter/spring occupation of the site from January to April. However, the presence of turtles indicates occupation during temperatures above 45°F, typical from late April to early October. An occupation occurring during spring is consistent with both deer and turtle indicators; however, it is also possible that the site was inhabited periodically at multiple times during various seasons. 5.1.3.3.1 - Skeletal Part Frequency and Utility Skeletal part frequency is used to infer butchering patterns, transport decisions, food preparation, disposal habits, nutritional analysis, activity areas, site function, economic institutions, and social organization (Reitz and Wing 1999). Of particular interest are site function, transport decisions, and intrasite patterning and the information those factors provide about the local activities and environment. For example, if animals were being brought into the site from outside the local environment, the site should contain a predominance of higher utility elements of that animal. When transporting animals for food, typically only the higher and middle utility elements are transported to save time and energy. Conversely, animals that are being procured from the immediate environment should be represented in the site by high, middle, and low utility elements. If animals were procured locally, but were being transported away, lower utility elements would be likely to occur. Patterning within a site can indicate areas of use. Although many taxa are present in the site faunal assemblage, deer (Odocoileus virginianus and Odocoileus sp.) and large MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 74

## Page 87

Bovidae (likely bison) are the primary focus of this analysis as they are larger taxa which would have been differentially affected by the distance to which they occurred within the site catchment area. Skeletal part frequencies and standardized gross utility rankings for large bovids (likely bison) were examined. Emerson’s (1990) averaged total products model was used to separate large bovid elements into high, middle, and low utility categories. Skeletal elements with a unit value greater than 70 percent (ribs, thoracic vertebrae, and lumbar vertebrae) were considered high utility. Elements with a unit value between 30 and 70 percent (cervical vertebrae, sternum, sacrum/pelvis, femur, and scapula) were considered middle utility. Elements with a unit value less than 30 percent (humerus, crania, tibia, radius/ulna, metacarpal, metatarsal, and carpals) were considered low utility, although it should be noted that humeri and tibias are on the cusp between the low and middle utility rankings. The NISP counts for elements with determinable utility values is depicted in Table 5.10. While there was minimal large bovid (likely bison) present within the 41DL553 faunal assemblage, only tibia were identifiable to element. Based on standardized gross utility values (Emerson 1993), tibia are considered low utility; however, they are considered high utility with regard to marrow content. One of the bovid long bones was seared and exhibited a green break suggesting that marrow utilization may have been the intended activity. In addition, the tibia also exhibited dry breaks making it impossible to determine the original size of the fragment, therefore, it is possible that bone grease manufacture was also occurring. Table 5.10: Element Abundance for Large Bovids at 41DL553 Standardized Gross Standardized Marrow Element NISP Utility Value Utility tibia 11.2% 84.5% 2 long bone – – 9 indeterminate – – 2 Total 13 Skeletal part frequencies, standardized gross utility rankings (Jacobson 2000), and standardized marrow utility rankings (Jacobson 2000) were examined for deer and undifferentiated deer/pronghorn to separate elements into high, middle, and low utility categories. Standardized Gross Utility Indices (SGUI) for white- tailed were developed by Jacobson (2000) and represent the comparative percent of meat, body fat, marrow fat, and bone grease represented by each bone element or group of elements (ribs, cervical vertebrae, etc.) in a whole carcass. The averages across various age and sex animals collected during various seasons was developed to represent an averaged ranking of elements regardless of age or sex of an animal. By contrast to SGUI, which considers utility for all bones based on all body products, the standardized marrow utility index (SMAUI) includes a comparative percent for marrow fat and bone grease yield for each long bone elements. The skeletal part frequency for deer and associated utility rankings are summarized in Table 5.11. For gross utility, any elements with a unit value greater than 8 percent (femur, ribs, pelvis, and thoracic vertebrae) were considered high utility. Any elements with a unit value between 4 and 8 percent (lumbar vertebrae, cervical vertebrae, scapula, and tibia) were considered middle utility. Any elements with a unit value less than 4 percent (humerus, crania, radius, mandible, phalanges, metatarsals, metacarpals, tarsals, and carpals) were considered low utility. With regard to marrow utility, tibias and femur are considered high utility, metatarsals, humeri, and radii are considered middle utility, and metacarpals are considered low utility. Based on standardized general utility values (SGUI, Jacobson 2004), high, medium, and low utility elements are all represented with a predominance of low utility elements (Figure 5.19). Elements demonstrate an inverted distribution; however, for marrow utility with a predominance of high utility elements represented (Figure 5.20). To better interpret the data, utility rankings for white-tailed deer (Jacobson 2000) were used to determine the number of deer (Odocoileus sp.) elements identifiable to high, middle, and low utility ranking (Figure 5.21). Average Utility 45% 40% 35% 30% P 25% S IN % 20% 15% 10% 5% 0% Low Utility Middle Utility High Utility Figure 5.21: Average of whole skeleton distribution for deer elements by utility ranking (from Jacobson 2000). Upon examination, deer element abundance at 41DL553 reflects a similar frequency of high, middle, and low utility elements (see Figure 5.19) similar to that of the distribution of average frequency of the elements in question relative to one whole animal (see Figure 5.21). This indicates that deer were being procured from the environment immediately surrounding the site and were being processed, consumed, and discarded at the site with minimal taphonomic element loss. This suggests that, although not permanent settlement, site 41DL553 was occupied for a relatively long amount of time and was more residential in nature than a temporary hunting camp. 5.1.3.3.1 - Environmental Indicators Due to the lack of species diversity, little information regarding ecotones or environmental data can be reconstructed from the 41DL553 faunal assemblage. The presence of turtle suggests utilization of aquatic faunal resources and is consistent with the site location within the Trinity River floodplain and along a remnant channel. Furthermore, eastern box turtles (Terrapene carolina) are most commonly found in open hardwood forest along field or wetland edges. Although primarily terrestrial, eastern box turtles also spend time partially submerged in shallow, slow-moving streams and ponds (Zuppa 2003). The presence of eastern box turtle at the site suggests that the nearby West Fork Trinity River contained shallow and slow- moving pools during the intervals in which the site was occupied. The presence of large bovid (likely bison) remains indicates nearby grasslands capable of sustaining large bovids at the time of occupation. The presence of low utility elements suggests that these large bovids were being procured from the immediate vicinity and carcasses were not being transported long distances before primary processing. Cottontail rabbits typically inhabit open scrubland environments as it provides a combination of forage as well as low shrubs that provide protection from predators (Schmidly 1994). Similarly, jackrabbits prefer hot, dry desert scrubland that is sparsely vegetated. Overall, these species suggest that open environments were near the site and that those open environments varied from prairie to drier scrubland throughout time. The presence of white-tailed deer (Odocoileus virginianus) indicates riparian and forest edge environments, likely in the immediate vicinity of the stream drainage (Schmidly 1994). Many deer specimens could only be reduced to genus level identification (Odocoileus sp.); however, all deer specimens that could be identified to species were identified as white-tailed deer utilizing methods for differentiating between mule deer and white-tailed deer (Jacobson 2004). While mule deer tend to occupy chaparral environments and MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 77

## Page 90

some of the deer presence could be associated with this species, it is assumed that all deer present were white-tailed deer associated with a more riparian habitat. 5.1.3.3.2 - Evidence of Bone Grease Processing Multiple lines of evidence indicate that bone grease processing was occurring at 41DL553. Jacobson (2021) identified archeological indicators to assess the potential for bone grease processing at sites in Texas, which included: • Bones of larger taxa represented by high proportion of small (<3 cm) fragments • Comminuted bone demonstrates a higher degree of weathering relative to other bones within the same provenience or feature, or in contrast to similar depositional context within the same component • Different patterns of breaks in bones across taxa, or differential break patterns across long bone elements corresponding to differences in available fat reserves • Presence of burned or ash-stained bone fragments • May have presence of intentional breakage (spiral breaks) of greater range of skeletal elements, not just those with a medullary cavity, as well as greater breakage of long bone epiphyseal fragments Site 41DL553 has a high percent of extensively-fragmented bone with over 96 percent of the bone fragments measuring less than 3 cm in size despite 68.5 percent of the bone recovered identified as belonging to medium or larger-sized mammals. In addition, patterning of completeness suggests a higher amount of predepositional fragmentation of medium and larger-sized mammals than of smaller fauna. Comparison of size categories between carnivore-modified bone and bone lacking carnivore modification indicates no relationship between the size of the bone fragment and the presence of carnivore modification, suggesting an alternative fracture agent was responsible for the high degree of fragmentation. There were some indicators for differential weathering within some excavation units, but the data were inconclusive. While no percussion marks were evident, they are typically difficult to identify on very small (i.e., < 3 cm) bone fragments. In spite of the high degree of fragmentation and breakage, spiral fractures were identified on deer, medium-large, and large-sized mammals. Spiral fractures occurring on taxa from those size categories is suggestive of more intentional human breakage of deer and bovid bone than other species. In addition, 18 percent of all bone from the site had been thermally altered. This evidence supports that processing of bone grease occurred at the site. Botanical Analysis Botanical remains recovered at site 41DL553 consist of opportunistically-collected wood charcoal encountered during excavation and water screening of the Phase II test units and from systematically collected flotation samples from selected targeted test units and from one identified prehistoric cultural feature. Flotation samples were processed using a manual flotation process with ≤0.2-mm light fraction square mesh and 1.1-mm heavy fraction square mesh. The total flotation sediment volume analyzed from 41DL553 was 495.9 l (131 gal). All botanical materials (charred and uncharred) were removed from the heavy flotation fractions for analysis with the light fractions. The overall light fraction recovery rate for the 41DL553 flotation samples was 22 percent. Charred botanical materials opportunistically collected during Phase II test unit excavation and recovered during 0.25-in water screening of the Phase II test unit sediments (Table 5.12) consisted of wood charcoal from white group oak (Quercus sect. Quercus), ash (Fraxinus spp.), and plum or cherry trees (Prunus spp.). In many cases, identification was possible only as hardwood (angiosperm clade). Wood charcoal from the TU 8 samples (FS 257, 262 and 275) was highly reflective and much of the area of the cross sections consisted of medium-size vessels suggestive of root wood. Flotation samples were sorted according to standard macrobotanical analysis techniques (Pearsall 2015) modified for the small size of charcoal fragments recovered at the site. Table 5.12: Botanical material from 0.25-in screen samples at 41DL553 Excavation Unit Depth Material Botanical name Common name NISP Weight (g) TU 2 100–110 cmbs Wood charcoal Hardwood Hardwood 2 0.20 Wood charcoal Prunus sp. Plum/cherry 11 0.23 TU 2 140–150 cmbs Wood charcoal Quercus sect. Quercus White group oak 6 0.06 TU 3 125–135 cmbs Wood charcoal Hardwood Hardwood 1 0.40 TU 7 210–220 cmbs Wood charcoal Fraxinus sp. Ash 2 0.24 Wood charcoal Hardwood Hardwood 20 3.54 TU 8 40–50 cmbs Wood charcoal Not examined Not examined 45 7.34 TU 8 50–60 cmbs Wood charcoal Hardwood Hardwood 16 1.90 TU 8 60–70 cmbs Wood charcoal Hardwood Hardwood 2 0.23 wood charcoal fragments less than 2 mm in maximum dimension are typically not sampled for identification. However, so few wood charcoal fragments larger than 2 mm were present in the 41DL553 samples that identification was attempted starting with the largest fragments in each sample and proceeding with successively smaller fragments regardless of size until fragments became too small to be identified. Botanical materials were identified to the lowest possible taxonomic level by comparison to materials in the macrobotanical analyst’s comparative collection and through the use of standard reference works (e.g., Davis 1993; Hoadley 1990; InsideWood 2004; Martin and Barkley 1961; Panshin and de Zeeuw 1980; Wheeler 2011). Because of the small size of the fragments recovered at site 41DL553, many wood charcoal fragments were identifiable only as hardwood (angiosperm clade). Many rootlets, uncarbonized seeds, and other plant materials were present in the 41DL553 botanical samples (Appendix B.1). Uncarbonized seeds are a common occurrence on most archeological sites, but they usually represent seeds of modern plants that have made their way into the soil either through their own dispersal mechanisms or by faunalturbation, floralturbation, or argilliturbation (Bryant 1985:51–52; Miksicek 1987:231–232). With the exception of arid areas, uncarbonized plant material recovered from archeological contexts on open-air sites can be assumed to be of modern origin unless compelling evidence suggests otherwise (Lopinot and Brussell 1982; Miksicek 1987:231). Two lines of evidence indicate that the uncarbonized botanical materials recovered at site 41DL553 are modern. First, the taxon composition is consistent with modern plants expected in the site area, and some are Eurasian natives (e.g., balloonvine [Cardiospermum halicacabum]) that have been introduced into the region historically and/or recently. Second, modern seeds decrease with depth as would be expected for material that originated at the modern surface (Figure 5.22). Therefore, all uncarbonized plant material recovered at 41DL553 is interpreted as modern. Archeological plant remains identified at site 41DL553 consisted of nutshell, bulb fragments, seeds, wood charcoal, and stem, starchy, and indeterminable fragments (Appendix B.2). Nutshell fragments, all acorn (Quercus spp.), were recovered from Feature 3. The feature also included a bedstraw (Galium sp.) seed. As the common name suggests, bedstraw is a bur. It may have been burned as a convenient means of disposing of a nuisance plant, but bedstraw also has medicinal uses. Among historic tribes, bedstraw was used medicine to treat a variety of ailments, especially those related to urinary tract or kidney problems (Moerman 1998). Bulb scales were recovered in TU 7 from Levels 13 through 21 (120 to 210 cmbs). Most could not be identified, but nine fragments from Levels 13 and 15 were wild onion (Allium spp.; Figure 5.23). Two species in this genus are common in the area today, meadow garlic (Allium canadense) and Drummond’s onion (A. drummondii). The apparent bulb cooking activities that have taken place at site 41DL553 are part of an extensive tradition of using long, slow heat to convert complex carbohydrates to simpler, more digestible ones (Thoms 2008; Wandschneider 1997). Some of the oldest bulb remains in North America are onions recovered from the Stigenwalt site in Kansas that date to around 8,800 B.P. (Adair 1990; Thoms 2008). Bulb scales were not recovered from any other contexts examined at site 41DL553. ## Page 93

Seeds recovered from the site, in addition to the bedstraw seed in Feature 3, consisted of a spurge seed and a large grass seed (both from TU 7, Level 10), and a fragment of a grass seed (TU 7, Level 14). The large grass seed from TU 7, Level 10 is likely a member of the Pooideae tribe known as the “cool season grasses” (i.e., C grasses) which includes many Eurasian crops like wheat, barley, oat, rye, and many lawn and 3 pasture grasses (Figure 5.24). The grass seed fragment from TU 7, Level 14 merits special mention because it is consistent with corn (Zea mays ssp. mays; Figure 5.25). The specimen was fragmentary and was deemed too small to produce a reliable radiocarbon date. No additional occurrences of possible corn were encountered from other contexts at 41DL553. Conversely, other varieties of large-seeded grasses, such as wildryes (Elymus spp.) and giant cutgrass (Zizaniopsis miliacea), are present in the area and the specimen from TU 7, Level 14 may be a fragmentary remain of one of those species. ## Page 94

(Fabaceae; 2 percent). All of these are trees that are common in riparian areas historically and indicate exploitation of wood available in the immediate site area. 5.1.4 - Radiocarbon Results Of particular importance in the evaluation of a prehistoric archeological site for inclusion in the NRHP is the potential for the site to contribute information important to understanding prehistory. This information potential is contingent upon the applicability of the associated data to local and regional knowledge and research questions. The availability and adequacy of information regarding chronological placement is essential to a comprehensive evaluation. During the Phase II investigation of 41DL553, datable charred botanical samples were recovered from a single prehistoric cultural feature. Following a failed attempt at producing a radiocarbon date from a fragment of charred material removed directly from the Feature 3 matrix during manual excavation, fragments of wood charcoal derived from flotation of the feature fill were submitted for radiocarbon dating. The results of radiocarbon dating indicate that Feature 3 was used between 897 and 780 BC (2788 to 2729 cal B.P. and 2846 to 2818 cal B.P.), placing it firmly within the Late Archaic period (Table 5.13). To provide further chronometric data regarding the site occupation, a series samples derived from charred botanical materials recovered through the flotation process from the TU 7 flotation column were analyzed. Specifically, charred botanical materials recovered during flotation of TU 7 Levels 6, 10, and 13 were submitted for analysis to contribute chronometric data for the geoarcheological analysis of the sites. The site is within the grading limits of the proposed project and extends slightly into the proposed building footprint. The site was tentatively defined during the Phase I survey as extending approximately 190 ft (58 m) northwest-to-southeast by 558 ft (170 m) northeast-to-southwest to encompass 1.67 ac (Figure 5.26). At the time of the Phase I survey, the site boundary was established by the excavation of negative trenches to the east and west, as well as the project area boundary to the south. The northern and northwestern portions of the site boundary were also influenced by the presence of emergent herbaceous wetlands, that occupied surface depressions likely representing abandoned channel meanders of the West Fork Trinity River, to the north and west of the site (Figures 5.27 and 5.28). The extent of the site to the northwest was not fully delineated at the time due to the presence of wetlands. ## Page 99

data, it was concluded that site 41DL554 was likely to represent at least one, possibly two, prehistoric occupations within a cumulic paleosol (cf. the West Fork paleosol), the top of which ranged in depth from 56 to 110 cmbs. One thermal feature (Feature 1), a probable mussel roasting pit, was encountered in Phase I survey Trench 20 at a depth of 135 to 192 cmbs. A second thermal feature (Feature 2), the remnants of a hearth or roasting pit, was encountered in Phase I survey Trench 30 at a depth of 170 to 180 cmbs. Cultural materials encountered at the site include chipped lithic artifacts (n=7), faunal remains (deer [n>30], bison [n=2], and mussel shell [n>60]), burned clay (n>100), and fire-cracked rocks (n>20). Based on the Phase I survey data, the archeological deposits appeared to be concentrated in the eastern half of the site as survey Trenches 32 and 33 yielded relatively fewer artifacts. 5.2.2 - Current Investigations Additional Phase I Delineation Trench Results During the current investigation, IES conducted three additional site delineation trenches (Trenches 43, 44, and 45) to confirm the 41DL554 site boundary within the project area (Figure 5.29; Appendix A.3). All of the additional site delineation trenches were negative for prehistoric cultural materials and the boundary of 41DL554 was considered to be confirmed. Phase II Exploratory Trench Results Six Phase II exploratory trenches were excavated at 41DL554 (Trenches 46 through 51; see Figure 5.29; Appendix A.4). Phase II exploratory trench locations were selected to further identify and assess horizontal and vertical artifact frequencies and concentrations, archeological features, and to expand the overall subsurface exposure at the site to facilitate both the geoarcheological analysis of the site and the placement of Phase II controlled excavation units. The placement of the exploratory trenches coincided with the central axis of the site, which occupied a slightly elevated, generally level natural levee deposit flanked by gentle swales or depressions occupied by emergent herbaceous wetlands to the north. During Phase II exploratory trenching, a much different understanding of the artifact and feature distribution at site 41DL554 emerged. Despite the identification of two possible prehistoric cultural features (Features 1 and 2) during the previous Phase I survey located in the central and eastern portion of the site (Phase I survey Trenches 20 and 30; see Figure 5.26), a relative paucity of artifacts were observed during the excavation and systematic screening of fill from Phase II exploratory Trenches 46 and 48, which were excavated adjacent to the locations of the Phase I survey Trenches 30 and 20, respectively, as well as within exploratory Trench 47. In contrast, two additional cultural features (Features 3 and 4) were encountered during the excavation of Phase II exploratory Trenches 49 and 51, located in the northern and western portions of the site (areas which had previously yielded few artifacts during the Phase I survey). Phase II Initial Test Unit Results Two initial 1-m-by-1-m test units (Test Units [TU] 1 and 2) were excavated at site 41DL554 to more accurately and adequately sample vertical artifact distributions in proximity to the area of highest artifact frequencies and potential prehistoric cultural features encountered during the previous Phase I site investigation and the Phase II exploratory trenches. Each of the initial test units were manually excavated within the profile of a Phase II exploratory trench, specifically Trenches 48 and 50 (see Figure 5.29). The initial test units were excavated in 10-cm levels from the current ground surface to a depth of 20 cm below the deepest encountered cultural artifacts identified during both the previous Phase I investigation and the current Phase II investigation. The goal of the initial test units was to confirm the vertical limits of the cultural deposits at the site and to identify any additional cultural horizons located above or below the cultural deposits previously identified at 41DL554 (Table 5.14). MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 87

Despite TU 1 targeting a location adjacent to a previously-identified cultural feature (Feature 1; Phase I Trench 20 [135 to 192 cmbs]) and TU 2 excavated in an area that yielded bison bone and FCR (Phase II exploratory Trench 50 [130 to 175 cmbs]), the results of both TU 1 and TU 2 illustrate a very sparse, low-frequency artifact assemblage extending from 80 to 240 cmbs. Targeted Test Units Following excavation of the two initial test units at the site, a Gradall was utilized to strip the culturally- sterile overburden from the ground surface and allow for targeted excavations of the subsequent six targeted test units at the site (see Figure 5.29). The mechanical removal of overburden was visually monitored, and the overburden deposits were periodically sampled during the overburden removal process for screening through 0.25-in hardware mesh to monitor for the presence of artifacts. Mechanical overburden removal ceased approximately 20 cm above the depth of archeological deposits identified in the vicinity of each targeted test unit during previous stages of this investigation. Targeted test units were then manually excavated in 10-cm levels to a depth of 20 cm below the deepest encountered cultural artifact and/or feature identified. All excavated fill from the targeted test units was deflocculated and water-screened through 0.25-in hardware mesh. During this investigation, 12.8 m3 of sediment was excavated at site 41DL554. In addition to the targeted test units, both of the cultural features (Features 3 and 4) exposed within the exploratory trenches were examined, documented, and completely excavated. Due to the vertic nature of the site sediments, preservation of the features pending analysis, report preparation, and agency review was not possible. ## Page 101

Features 5.2.2.5.1 - Feature 3 Feature 3 was initially encountered during the excavation of Phase II exploratory Trench 49 at a depth of approximately 145 cmbs within the northern portion of the trench. Due to the presence of the feature, Trench 49 was extended to the south to facilitate excavation of the trench to a greater depth while temporarily preserving the feature. Feature 3 initially presented as a cluster of FCR fragments in a generally circular pattern approximately 30 cm in diameter within a dark gray (10YR 4/1) silty clay matrix (Figure 5.30). Additional isolated fragments of FCR (n=3) were observed 156 cm south of Feature 3 and one additional FCR fragment was located 65 cm southeast of the feature. A single piece of chipped lithic debitage was located approximately 5 cm north of the FCR cluster. No feature fill was discernable within the surrounding soil matrix. Due to the lack of matrix-defined feature fill, Feature 3 was bisected in a north-to-south orientation across the approximate center of the feature. Feature 3 was then arbitrarily buffered by approximately 10 cm on the western side of the feature and excavated to facilitate an examination of the feature profile (see Figure 5.30). In profile, Feature 3 exhibited no feature fill and was only discernable by the presence of FCR within the dark gray (10YR 4/1) silty clay soil matrix. The entire contents (20.8 l) of Feature 3 were collected for flotation processing. Flotation of the Feature 3 fill yielded 4.3 kg of FCR, 1.17 g of burned clay, 17 pieces of chipped lithic debitage, 0.07 g of faunal bone. All FCR within Feature 3 was composed of friable sandstone, likely derived from the nearby Woodbine Formation. Incorporated into the FCR thermal elements of the feature was one fragment of FCR that displayed pecking or pounding modification, suggesting that it may have been used as a hammerstone prior to discard and repurposing as a heating element. Of the chipped lithic debitage recovered within the feature, all was derived from local Ogallala gravel sources. Within the chipped lithic assemblage recovered from the feature, a total of six flakes were identified, comprising five small (0.25-to-0.5-in) tertiary bifacial thinning flakes and one medium-sized (0.5-0.75-in) secondary core reduction flake in addition to 11 small (0.25-to-0.5-in) fragments of angular shatter. Faunal bone recovered from this feature included 14 bones, eight of which were identified as microtine vertebrates while the remaining six were identified as vertebrates of indeterminate size. All of the bones from this feature were highly fragmentary and each measured only several millimeters in length. No element classifications were able to be made. Seven of the bones were blackened and two were calcined, suggesting that at least some of these bones may not have been incidental occurrences but may have been intentionally heated in a fire. Due to the lack of feature matrix definition, it is unclear whether the presence of the debitage and faunal remains are related to the function of the feature, or another nearby activity, or were simply inclusions within the soil from previous or concurrent occupations of the site area. No charred botanical remains were recovered within Feature 3, neither during manual excavation nor during flotation of the feature contents. Feature 3 is interpreted as a cluster of discarded heating elements from a stone boiling pit. Based on the lack of any thermally-discolored soil matrix, charred botanical remains, or substantive faunal remains within the feature or the immediate vicinity, it is unlikely that Feature 3 represents the primary locus of the cooking activity associated with it. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 89

## Page 103

5.2.2.5.1 - Feature 4 Feature 4 was initially encountered during the excavation of Phase II exploratory Trench 51 at a depth of approximately 165 cmbs within the southern portion of the trench. Due to the presence of the feature, Trench 51 was extended to the north to facilitate excavation of the trench to a greater depth while temporarily preserving the feature. Feature 4 was identified as a generally circular zone of oxidized (red [2.5YR 4/6]) and reduced (very dark gray [10YR 3/1]) burned soil, approximately 40 cm in diameter, within the surrounding brown (10YR 4/3) silty clay soil matrix (Figure 5.31). The feature was bisected in an approximate northwest-to-southeast orientation across the center of the feature to bisect both the oxidized and reduced soil zones within it. The northeastern half of the feature was then excavated to facilitate an examination of the feature profile (see Figure 5.31). In profile, Feature 4 exhibited a generally lenticular cross section and extended to a maximum depth of 176 cmbs, with the oxidized soil matrix extending along the northwestern margin of the feature. A mass of charred botanical material or organic-rich soil was collected at a depth of 172 cmbs within the central portion of the feature and was submitted for radiocarbon dating. The entire contents of Feature 4 (7.6 l) were collected for flotation processing. Flotation of the Feature 4 fill yielded 6.10 g of burned clay, 0.15 g of FCR, one piece of chipped lithic debitage, and 0.04 g of indeterminate hardwood charcoal fragments. The single piece of debitage was identified as a bifacial thinning flake. Following a failed attempt at producing a radiocarbon date from the charred material removed directly from the Feature 4 matrix during manual excavation, fragments of hardwood charcoal derived from flotation of the feature fill were submitted for radiocarbon dating. The results of radiocarbon dating indicate that Feature 4 was used between 50 BC and 116 AD (1999 to 1866 cal B.P. and 1854 and 1834 cal B.P.), placing it firmly within the Late Archaic period. 5.2.3 - Artifact Analysis Chipped Lithic Artifacts During Phase II investigations at the site, 351 chipped lithic artifacts were recovered at 41DL554 (Table 5.15). The majority (n=345; 98 percent) of those artifacts consist of unmodified debitage and shatter. However, hafted bifacial tools (typically projectile points and/or knives; n=3), non-hafted bifacial tools (n=1), unifacial tools (n=1), and expedient tools (n=2) are also represented. ## Page 105

Morphological categories of the debitage indicate the occupants of 41DL554 favored a multidirectional core reduction strategy. Although no cores are represented within this assemblage, the debitage displayed characteristics of small to moderate-sized core selection, likely due to reliance on locally-abundant Ogallala gravels. Approximately 47 percent (n=60) of the flake assemblage consisted of primary and secondary flakes. None of the flakes exceed 25 mm (1 in) in maximum size, and a significant majority featured considerable curvature. Larger, straighter flakes typically indicative of large cores were absent. Flakes featured multidirectional ventral flake scars, and an equal proportion of single and multifaceted platforms. Based on those factors, the occupants at the site likely utilized local lithic sources to manufacture smaller- sized tools from multidirectional blanks. Later stage reduction activities were also prevalent in the 41DL554 lithic assemblage. The biface thinning flakes (n=28) were small, featuring a maximum size not exceeding 19 mm (0.75 in). They generally consisted of a slight curvature with abraded platforms. Tool rejuvenation flakes (n=14) were also recovered. These flakes were absent of cortex and featured slight curvature and abraded platforms. The significant quantity of small bifacial thinning flakes and tool rejuvenation flakes indicate that formal and informal tool production was a prevalent practice occurring alongside initial core reduction at the site. Across the site, chipped lithic debitage exhibited two general vertical trends. The southwestern portion of the site (containing TUs 3, 4, 5, and 6) featured a bimodal distribution of flakes with peaks at approximately 90 to 100 cmbs and 145 to 160 cmbs (Figure 5.32A). Within the upper peak, tertiary flakes were most prominent, whereas secondary and primary flakes were clustered within the lower peak. The northern portion of the site (containing TUs 7 and 8) consisted of unimodal distribution with a peak at 170 cmbs and a skew with depth (Figure 5.32B). Tertiary flakes are represented throughout the unit, but generally reflect the unimodal distribution of primary and secondary flakes, which are clustered between 140 and 220 cmbs. Based on these trends, it is likely that earlier stages of lithic tool production was focused within the western portions of the site and tool refinement occurred in the northern portion. A 60 B 60 Primary Secondary Tertiary 80 80 100 100 )s b 120 )s b 120 m m c ( h 140 c ( h 140 tp e 160 tp e 160 D D 180 180 200 200 220 220 0 5 10 15 0 5 10 15 Unmodified Flakes (n) Unmodified Flakes (n) Figure 5.32: Vertical distribution of unmodified flakes within TUs 3, 4, 5, and 6 (A) and TU 7 and 8 (B) at 41DL554. Six formal and informal tools were recovered from 41DL554. Those tools include a projectile point fragment, two biface stems on flake blanks, a biface fragment, and two expedient flake implements (Figure 5.33). All six tools were recovered at varying depths, ranging from 100 to 190 cmbs. Three of the tools (50 percent) were recovered from TU 7 from a depth range of 90 to 130 cmbs. The point fragment represents the only temporally diagnostic chipped lithic artifact recovered from 41DL554 and is consistent with the Ellis type, which is common throughout the Middle to Transitional Archaic in North-Central and East Texas (Turner and Hester 1999:113; see Figure 5.33A). The point was encountered within TU 8 between 130 and 140 cmbs. Unfortunately, a lateral snap extended from the notch to the opposing lateral edge resulting in only the stem and one barb left intact. The point featured a neck width of 16.19 mm and a base width of 21.16 mm. Weakly-developed polish was observed along the lateral edges and raised ridges. No striations or significant edge rounding were observed. Based on usewear analysis, the point was slightly to moderately used on soft, greasy materials before breakage (Figure 5.34). ## Page 107

Two additional biface stem fragments were recovered (see Figures 5.33B and C). The two fragmentary bifaces were constructed using flake blanks and featured poorly-defined shoulders. Both fragments featured snap fractures close to the stem. A distal segment of a late-stage biface was also encountered (see Figure 5.33D). Based on the portion recovered, this fragment likely originated from a preform. Two of the recovered flakes exhibited signs of expedient modification and use. One modified flake implement was produced on a tertiary flake (see Figure 5.33E). It featured a concave lateral edge that was improvised as a cutting surface. The final modified flake implement featured a rounded, distal tip with unifacial microflaking indicative of light scraping (see Figure 5.33F). Fire-cracked Rock During Phase II investigations at the site, 5.3 kg of FCR were recovered at 41DL554 (Table 5.16). Nearly all of the FCR identified at the site was composed of friable sandstone derived from the nearby Woodbine Formation; however, four specimens (80.6 g) were identified as limestone. With the exception of the FCR associated with Feature 3, identified in Trench 49 (see Section 5.2.2.5 above), nearly all FCR encountered at the site was generally observed as small (<4 cm in maximum dimension), isolated fragments at varying frequencies within the excavated site matrix. When reviewed in aggregate by groups of adjacent test units, a few general patterns are discernable (Figure 5.35). Within TUs 3, 4, 5, and 6, excavated in the western portion of the site, the relatively minor of FCR recovered within those units (totaling 569.95 g, of which 61 percent was recovered within TU 6) displays a general peak in the distribution from 130 to 180 cmbs (see Figure 5.13A). The FCR recovered within TUs 7 and 8, excavated in the northern portion of the site, display a more skewed distribution, with a peak at 70 to 80 cmbs and generally decreasing with depth. Faunal Analysis The total vertebrate NISP recovered from 41DL554 during the Phase II investigation was 172, of which 136 (79.1 percent) were identifiable to class. The dominate class of vertebrate fauna represented in the assemblages was mammal accounting for 105 (77.2 percent) of the bones identifiable to class. Only six bones (3.5 percent of the assemblage) were unbroken and only one bone was almost complete (0.6 percent). The seven complete and nearly complete bones included tarsals (n=6) and a lateral malleolus (n=1). Those bones are small, dense, and would be expected to retain completeness even after relatively extensive weathering. The majority of the bones in the assemblage were smaller in size (81 less than 1 cm, 69 from 1 to 3 cm, 16 from 3 to 6 cm, 4 from 6 to 9 cm, and 1 from 9 to 12 cm) and almost all of the bones (n=167, 97.7 percent) exhibited a break of some kind. Break types included dry breaks (n=76, 44.4 percent), green breaks (n=36, 21.1 percent), spiral fractures (n=2, 1.2 percent), and indeterminate breaks (n=53, 31.0 percent). Spiral fractures were identified on indeterminate long bones belonging to medium/medium-large mammals. The distribution of green breaks with regards to taxa and element is summarized in Table 5.18. 5.2.3.3.2 - Bone Modification Data collected in addition to identification of element and species included any human modification to the bone (i.e., cuts, chops, other modification such as percussion points for bone fracturing, cultural abrasion or scrapes for tool or other cultural artifact modification, or thermal alteration) and any taphonomic alterations (i.e., carnivore or rodent gnawing or evidence for consumption, root etching, and degree of weathering of the bone). Additionally, any characteristics on the bone identifying an animal’s age (e.g., epiphyseal union, tooth eruption or wear, etc.) or sex (e.g., antlers, morphometric characteristics, etc.) were recorded if present. No bones displayed other evidence for butchering such as cutmarks or chop marks. Three Eastern box turtle (Terrapene carolina) peripheral sections of the carapace exhibited grooving on both the dorsal and ventral surfaces, indicative of tool modification or cultural use, were identified within TU 5 at a depth of 155 to 165 cmbs. The grooves on the dorsal surfaces matched the locations of the grooves on the ventral surfaces (Figures 5.36 and 5.37). The spacing and morphology of the grooves was less consistent with patterning that would result from rodent gnawing or incisor scoring by carnivores; rather, the locations and regular spacing of the grooves were consistent with potential preparation (stone tool cuts or scraping) or use-wear for a cultural function, such as a turtle shell rattle. Based on the locations on the shell, the grooves were consistent with locations of drilling for handheld turtle shell rattles (Gillreath-Brown 2011) where leather straps were looped around the edges of a shell, which were hafted onto a deer metapodial handle. Given the visible sutures and unfused nature of the shell, additional support or wrapping would have been necessary to keep the shell whole if utilized for cultural purposes beyond subsistence. The faunal remains from the surrounding test units (TUs 3, 4, and 6) were reviewed to determine if any additional faunal bone potentially related to the manufacture of turtle shell rattles was present in nearby contexts. In addition to the previously mentioned turtle shell fragments, 19 fragments were identified belonging to indeterminate box turtle (Terrapene sp.) and one fragment belonging to indeterminate pond turtle (Emydidae). A metatarsal and phalanx belonging to a medium-large artiodactyl (deer or pronghorn) were also identified in the surrounding test units. This is noteworthy, as deer hooves have also been documented during the historic period as being used to make rattles and were frequently used as handles Figure 5.36: Turtle shell peripheral plate fragments exhibiting grooves from 41DL544 (TU 5, Level 10 [155–165 cmbs)]; dorsal view (left) and ventral view (right).
