## Page 110

Figure 5.37: Detailed view of turtle shell fragments exhibiting dorsal grooves from 41DL544 (TU 5, Level 10 [155– 165 cmbs]). for turtle shell rattles (Gillreath-Brown and Peres 2017; Smithsonian 2021; Turpin 1996). In addition, deer metapodials have been documented in the Trans-Pecos region as being used as an individual musical tool (Turpin 1996). A total of 60 bones within the 41DL554 faunal assemblage have been thermally modified. Of the burned bone, 52 had some browning (86.6 percent), 5 were blackened (8.3 percent), and 3 had been calcined (5 percent). Brown discoloration is indicative of short-term and lower heat burning, such as exposure to a grass fire or campfire for less than 6 minutes (Lyman 1994). Blackened bone has been carbonized and would indicate prolonged exposure to a grass fire or campfire. Grey and white bone has been fully calcined, which means that it has lost all organic matter and become plastically deformed. The form of brown thermal alteration is similar to observations in faunal assemblages at other sites in the region, such as at the nearby Fish Creek Slough (41DL436) site (Jacobson 2014). The browning in that instance was indicative of an indirect heat source, such as a heated rock that had been placed on green bone, searing it and causing micro- fractures while weakening the bone (Figure 5.38). The hypothesis presented by Jacobson (2014) to describe the marks in the Fish Creek Slough faunal assemblage was that placement of heated rocks on the fresh bone surfaces facilitated easier bone breakage for marrow extraction. Only minor evidence of carnivore modification (n=3, 1.8 percent) and rodent gnawing (n=13, 7.6 percent) was observed on the 41DL554 faunal assemblage. Of the rodent-gnawed bone, the majority (n=12) had been gnawed by mice or rats, with one gnaw mark of intermittent size between rat and squirrel which could be indicative of a large rat, pocket gopher, or ground squirrel. Research (Klippel and Synstelien 2007) has shown that brown rats only gnaw bone which is fresh and still laden with fats, while squirrels only gnaw on thicker bone cortices after fats have leached away. Rats will gnaw on bones recently deposited while squirrels would be more likely to gnaw on bone left exposed for a prolonged time. Given the dominance of mice/rat-gnawed bone, the 41DL554 data suggest rodent gnawing activity while bone was fresh with minimal time of surface exposure prior to burial, not unexpected in a floodplain setting. The degree of human and animal modifications to the bones could be under-documented, however, as they may have been MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 98

Figure 5.38: Bison bone with seared browning and microfractures from 41DL554. obscured by external factors such as root etching and alluvial abrasion. Site 41DL554 is located in the floodplain of the West Fork Trinity River and has certainly experienced periods of flooding. Clear indications of alluvial abrasion were identified on two bones and one additional bone exhibited abrasion that was of indeterminate association, indicating that at least a portion of the site has experienced alluvial erosion or scouring action. The more definitive examples of alluvial abrasion were associated with two different test units (TU 3, Level 7 and TU 6, Level 3). As each occurred within a different controlled stratigraphic level, this suggests that the site has been subjected to intermittent periods of flooding through time. Carnivore modification was identified on five bones including an astragalus, femur, phalanx, metapodial, and indeterminate long bone. In terms of taxa, carnivore modification was identified on white-tailed deer, deer, deer or pronghorn, and an indeterminate medium-large mammal. Carnivore modification included punctures, scoring, gnawing, and intestinal modification through digestion. The relatively low amount of carnivore modification exhibited by the assemblage suggests that local scavengers were unable to access the majority the bones prior to their deposition, further supporting the hypothesis that the archeological materials associated with the site were buried relatively quickly after initial discard. If the carnivore modifications were created by domesticated dogs instead of wild scavengers, short-term occupations of the site and rapid burial could also explain the relatively low levels of carnivore modification observed within the 41DL554 faunal assemblage. 5.2.3.3.3 - Weathering The bones within the 41DL554 faunal assemblage were examined for signs of weathering. The degree of weathering was recorded following Lyman’s (1994) synthesis of Behrensmeyer (1978), Andrews (1990), and Johnson (1985). The weathering scale consisted of rankings from 0 to 5. Typically, all archeological MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 99

## Page 112

material would be minimally assigned a rank of 2 or 3. The majority (n=131, 76.6 percent) of the bone recovered from 41DL554 was assigned a weathering rank of 3. Thirty-eight bones (22.2 percent) were assigned to a weathering rank of 4 and only one bone (0.6 percent) was assigned a rank of 5. Additionally, only one bone (0.6 percent) was assigned a rank of 2. Overall, this pattern of weathering is consistent with a short-term use site along a waterway where discarded bone would be buried relatively quickly by alluvial action. The slightly larger abundance of weathering stage 4 bone is higher than would be expected at a site with rapid deposition. Bone that is boiled often appears more weathered than unboiled bone that was exposed for an equal amount of time. Boiling bone is common in bone grease production therefore bone grease production may explain the apparent overabundance of weathering stage 4 bone. Of the 38 bones assigned a ranking of 4, two were identified as bison/cattle (5.3 percent), two as deer (5.3 percent), and 15 as medium/medium-large mammals (39.5 percent). The remaining 19 bones included small and small/medium sized mammals (n=18, 47.4 percent) and miscellaneous vertebrates (n=1, 2.6 percent). Bison, deer, and medium and medium-large mammals are all animals which were commonly utilized for bone grease production. The distribution of weathered bone throughout the site is summarized in Figure 5.39. Most of the bone assigned a weathering rank 4 was recovered from TU 3 (n=13, 34.2 percent), TU 4 (n=9, 23.7 percent), and TU 8 (n=10, 26.3 percent). Though less abundant than the stage 4 bone, TUs 3 and 4 both contain a significant amount of stage 3 bone suggesting that the stage 4 bone in these units may have been produced through boiling rather than solely weathering. TU 8 was almost entirely composed of stage 4 bone. Those bones were identified as small, small/small-medium, and small-medium/medium sized mammal, which are smaller than the animals from which bone grease was typically processed. This suggests that the bone recovered from TU 8 may have been exposed for a longer amount of time prior to burial than the surrounding units to produce its advanced weathering stage. 100% 90% 80% 70% 60% 2 P S IN 50% 3 % 40% 4 30% 5 20% 10% 0% Trench Trench Trench TU 1 TU 2 TU 3 TU 4 TU 5 TU 6 TU 7 TU 8 30 49 50 Figure 5.39: Distribution of weathered bone by excavation unit at 41DL554. 5.2.3.3.4 - Mortality and Seasonality Few direct indicators of seasonal occupation were possible with the 41DL554 fauna assemblage. The presence of turtles (n=74, 6.3 percent of assemblage) indicates the site was used during warmer seasons, as turtles hibernate at temperatures below 38 to 45°F (Roots 2006), which would place their utilization most likely from late March to early November (NOAA 2021, US Climate Data 2021). Since no birds were identified in the assemblage, no information on seasonal migration patterns is available. ## Page 113

5.2.3.3.5 - Skeletal Part Frequency and Utility Skeletal part frequency is used to infer butchering patterns, transport decisions, food preparation, disposal habits, nutritional analysis, activity areas, site function, economic institutions, and social organization (Reitz and Wing 1999). Of particular interest are site function, transport decisions, and within site patterning and what this can tell us about the local activities and environment. For example, if animals are being brought into the site from outside the local environment, the site should contain a predominance of higher utility elements of that animal. When transporting animals for food typically only the higher and middle utility elements are transported to save time and energy. Conversely, animals that are being procured from the immediate environment should be represented in the site by high, middle, and low utility elements. If animals are procured locally, yet being transported away, lower utility elements would be likely to occur. Patterning within a site can alert to areas of use. Although many taxa are present in the site, deer (Odocoileus virginianus and Odocoileus sp.) and bison (Bison bison and large Bovidae) are the primary focus as they are larger taxa which would have been differentially affected by the distance to which they occurred within the site catchment area. While there was minimal bison presence, identifiable bison bones include a metacarpal (n=1), tarsals (n=2), tibia (n=1), and a long bone (n=1). Based on standardized gross utility values (Emerson 1993), metacarpals, tarsals, and tibias are all considered low utility. Identifiable deer bones include carpals (n=1), tarsals (n=7), femur (n=1), crania (n=1), humerus (n=1), metatarsal (n=1), metapodial (n=1), phalanx (n=1), and lateral malleolus (n=1). With regard to SGUI (Jacobson 2004), all deer elements would be considered low utility with the exception of the femur, which is high utility. Also, although considered low utility, the humerus is on the border between middle and low utility. The prevalence of low utility elements for both bovids and deer would typically suggest they were being procured from the immediate environment around the site. The lack of higher utility elements, namely vertebrae and ribs, also indicates that higher utility elements were transported or deposited elsewhere. Typically, this would support that the site may have been utilized as a short-term camp site and is consistent with hunting or initial butchering of animals. The presence of modified turtle bone, however, is suggestive of other potential explanations for lower utility deer bone presence. Deer metacarpals and metatarsals were used as hafts for turtle shell rattles and the toe bones (phalanges) and smaller carpals were occasionally used in rattles (Gillreath-Brown 2011; Gillreath- Brown and Peres 2017). Furthermore, hooves which would have retained the third and possibly a segment of the second phalanx were utilized as a form of rattle by many groups on the Plains (Smithsonian 2021; Turpin 1996; Voegellin 1942). Given the potential evidence for a rattle, the skeletal part frequency could also indicate a production area for rattles, and/or area where a broken or older rattle was discarded. Turpin (1996:269–270) has stated, when referring to musical instruments in the archeological record, that “if unstrung, deer hoof or snail shell rattles, as well as any unpainted scapulae, could pass as kitchen waste.” 5.2.3.3.6 - Environmental Indicators Due to the lack of species diversity, little information on ecotones or environmental data can be reconstructed from the 41DL554 faunal assemblage. The presence of turtle suggests utilization of aquatic faunal resources and is consistent with the site location within the Trinity River floodplain and along a remnant channel. Furthermore, eastern box turtles (Terrapene carolina) are most commonly found in open hardwood forest along field or wetland edges. Although mostly terrestrial, eastern box turtles also spend time partially submerged in shallow slow-moving streams and ponds (Zuppa 2003). The presence of eastern box turtle at the site suggests that the nearby West Fork Trinity River contained shallow and slow- moving portions during the period in which the site was occupied. The presence of bison remains indicates nearby grasslands capable of sustaining bison at the time of occupation (Schmidly 1994). The presence of low utility elements suggests that bison were being procured from the immediate vicinity and carcasses were not being transported long distances before primary processing. However, identifiable bison remains were only identified in Phase II exploratory Trench 50. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 101


---

## Page 114

For controlled excavation units, the only mammal remains identifiable to species were white-tailed deer (Odocoileus virginianus). White-tailed deer prefer riparian and forest edge environments (Schmidly 1994). Many deer specimens could only be reduced to genus level identification (Odocoileus sp.); however, all deer specimens that could be identified to species were identified as white-tailed deer utilizing methods for differentiating between mule deer and white-tailed deer (Jacobson 2004). While mule deer tend to occupy chaparral environments and some of the deer presence could be associated with this species, it is assumed that all deer present were associated with a more riparian habitat. Botanical Analysis Botanical remains recovered at site 41DL554 consist of opportunistically collected wood charcoal encountered during excavation and water screening of the Phase II test units and from systematically collected flotation samples from selected targeted test units and from two identified prehistoric cultural features. Flotation samples were processed using a manual flotation process with ≤0.2-mm light fraction square mesh and 1.1-mm heavy fraction square mesh. The total flotation sediment volume analyzed from 41DL554 was 328.4 l (86.8 gal). All botanical materials (charred and uncharred) were removed from the heavy flotation fractions for analysis with the light fractions. The overall light fraction recovery rate for the 41DL554 flotation samples was 3 percent. Flotation samples were sorted according to standard macrobotanical analysis techniques (Pearsall 2015) modified for the small size of charcoal fragments recovered at the site. In standard macrobotanical analysis, wood charcoal fragments less than 2 mm in maximum dimension are typically not sampled for identification. However, so few wood charcoal fragments larger than 2 mm were present in the 41DL553 samples that identification was attempted starting with the largest fragments in each sample and proceeding with successively smaller fragments, regardless of size, until fragments became too small to be identified. Botanical materials were identified to the lowest possible taxonomic level by comparison to materials in the Macrobotanical Analysis comparative collection and through the use of standard reference works (e.g., Davis 1993; Hoadley 1990; InsideWood 2004; Martin and Barkley 1961; Panshin and de Zeeuw 1980; Wheeler 2011). Because of the small size of the fragments recovered at site 41DL554, many wood charcoal fragments were identifiable only as hardwood (angiosperm clade). Materials opportunistically collected during Phase II excavation at site 41DL554 consisted of a single sample of unidentifiable carbonized material from Feature 4 that may not be botanical in nature. Many rootlets, uncarbonized seeds, and other plant materials were present in the 41DL554 botanical samples (Appendix B.3). Uncarbonized seeds are a common occurrence on most archeological sites, but they usually represent seeds of modern plants that have made their way into the soil either through their own dispersal mechanisms or by faunalturbation, floralturbation, or argilliturbation (Bryant 1985:51–52; Miksicek 1987:231–232). With the exception of arid areas, uncarbonized plant material recovered from archeological contexts on open-air sites can be assumed to be of modern origin unless compelling evidence suggests otherwise (Lopinot and Brussell 1982; Miksicek 1987:231). Two lines of evidence indicate that the uncarbonized material at site 41DL553 is modern. First, the taxon composition is consistent with modern plants expected in the site area, and some are Eurasian natives (e.g., balloonvine [Cardiospermum halicacabum]) that have been introduced into the region historically and/or recently. Second, modern seeds decrease with depth as would be expected for material that originated at the modern surface (Figure 5.40). Therefore, uncarbonized plant material recovered at 41DL554 is interpreted as modern. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 102

## Page 115

Modern Taxa (n) Figure 5.40: Modern seed taxa versus depth below surface (r = -0.91) at 41DL554. Archeological plant remains at site 41DL554 consisted of bulb scale fragments, wood charcoal, and indeterminate botanical fragments (Appendix B.4). Bulb scale fragments (n=4) were recovered in TU 7, Levels 12 and 13 (110 to 130 cmbs); however, none could be identified to genus. Oaks made up the vast majority of identifiable wood charcoal at the site, with 130 of the 136 identifiable fragments being some kind of oak. An additional four charcoal fragments were identifiable as elm and two were mulberry. It appears that site inhabitants were deliberately selecting for oak wood, which burns hot and makes excellent coals (Marcoulier and Anderson n.d.). Oaks do occur on floodplains along the Trinity River, but they are usually not the dominant trees. Site inhabitants may have extended their search for fuelwood along the floodplain or into the uplands to secure sufficient quantities of this desirable wood. 5.2.4 - Radiocarbon Results During the Phase II investigation of 41DL554, datable charred botanical material samples were recovered from one prehistoric cultural feature. No charred botanical remains were recovered within Feature 3, neither during manual excavation nor during flotation of the feature contents. A mass of charred material or organic-rich soil from a depth of 172 cmbs within the central portion of Feature 4 was submitted for radiocarbon dating but failed to produce a date. Fragments of hardwood charcoal, derived from flotation of the Feature 4 fill, were submitted for radiocarbon dating. The results of radiocarbon dating indicate that Feature 4 was used between 50 BC and 116 AD (1999 to 1866 cal B.P. and 1854 and 1834 cal B.P.), placing it firmly within the Late Archaic period (Table 5.19). MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 103

## Page 116

Table 5.19: Radiocarbon Data from Site 41DL554 Beta ID 13C/12C Conventional Number Provenience Material Ratio Age Two-sigma Calibration Range(s) 585313 Feature 4 (172 cmbs) charcoal no data no data no data hardwood 50 cal BC–84 cal AD (1999–1866 cal B.P.) 589201 Feature 4 (166–176 cmbs) -26.5 ‰ 2000 +/- 30 B.P. charcoal 96–116 cal AD (1854–1834 cal B.P.) 5.3 Geoarcheological Investigations at Sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 5.3.1 - Characterization of the Floodplain Alluvium at 41DL553 and 41DL554 The alluvium below the modern West Fork Trinity River floodplain surface at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 is probably around 8 m thick. This estimate is based on the height of the floodplain surface above the channel bottom and assumes the West Fork Trinity channel locally is running on bedrock or has not incised it too deeply. The uppermost (<3 m) part of this alluvium was observed in 20 trenches excavated within and around the two sites. The characterization of the floodplain alluvium presented below is synthesized from the detailed examination of the soil-stratigraphy of six of the 20 trenches (Trenches 34, 40, and 42 at 41DL553, and Trenches 47, 49, and 50 at 41DL554) excavated during the Phase II field investigations, laboratory analyses of four sediment/soil column samples from Trenches 40, 42, 47, and 49, and five radiocarbon ages obtained on wood charcoal from controlled excavation (Figures 5.41 to 5.44; Appendix C). In-field observations revealed that the alluvial stratigraphy is consistent across the landform between the two sites and consists of the Recent alluvium and West Fork paleosol/Pilot Point alluvium (Figure 5.45). Recent Alluvium The Recent alluvium at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 has an observed maximum thickness of 130 cm (Trench 46) and generally thins with distance from the West Fork Trinity River channel, although thick (>1-m) profiles do occasionally occur far from the channel when those deposits are filling relic channels (e.g., Trenches 37 and 38). This lateral thinning of the unit away from the channel is reflected in the facies that comprise the unit, and their texture and soil imprints. The Recent alluvium exhibits pronounced textural variation both laterally and vertically. Mean particle sizes for samples range from 4.6 to 7.8 phi with sample standard deviations of 2.0 to 2.8 phi. These statistics indicate that the sediments are varied in texture, very poorly sorted, and generally positively or finely skewed. Such characteristics are indicative of relatively young sediments that have experienced minimal pedogenic modification. The textural variability of the Recent alluvium is linked to floodplain position relative to the channel. More channel-proximal locales (Trenches 47 and 50) consist of channel margin facies (i.e., natural levee and possibly crevasse splay deposits) comprised of discrete packages of mud-draped, laminated, very fine sands or muds with inset thin-to-medium beds of laminated very fine sand. Those deposits are capped by thin (<30 cm) loamy sediments. Farther from the channel, the Recent alluvium grades into silty clays, silty clay loams, and clay loams. Particle size data reveal the lateral fining of the unit as channel margin facies transition to flood basin facies. Sand percentages for the unit as a whole decrease from an average of 27.7 percent in Trench 47 to an average of 8.9 percent in Trench 49. The average mean particle size for the unit also decreases from 5.6 to 6.9 phi across the same two trenches. Though distant from the channel and classified as silty clay loams and clay loams, the Recent alluvium in Trenches 40 and 42 display relatively high sand content (12.6 to 22.3 percent). Trenches 40 and 42 are located within or along the margin of a swale, which probably serves as an overflow channel or chute for channelized flows during flood events, and thus has slightly more sand than the surrounding flood basin facies. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 104

## Page 119

Figure 5.45: Recent alluvium and West Fork paleosol / Pilot Point alluvium in Trench 40 profile at 41DL553. The thinning and textural fining of the Recent alluvium with distance from the channel are reflected in the soil catena that is imprinted on the unit. More distal portions of the floodplain, where sedimentation rates are low, exhibit thin A or AC soil profiles. They consist of dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) to very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) subangular blocky-structured silty clay loams and clay loams. Where the unit is slightly thicker, such as in Trench 49, it expresses an A-Bw soil profile. There, the A horizon consists of dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) subangular blocky-structured silty clay and the underlying Bw horizon is a grayish brown (10YR 5/2) subangular blocky-structured silty clay. On the channel proximal portions of the floodplain (Trenches 47 and 50) multiple, discrete packages of sediments are not masked by pedogenesis due to presumed higher rates of sedimentation. Trench 47 exhibits an A-AC-C soil profile, while Trench 50 exhibits an A-C1-C2 soil profile. The A horizons mimic those formed on the surface of the more distal parts of floodplain consisting of dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) subangular blocky- structured loams and silty clays. The underlying sediments are largely unmodified sands and muds classified as AC and C horizons. Pilot Point Alluvium/ West Fork Paleosol The observed thickness of the Pilot Point alluvium at 41DL553 and 41DL554 ranges from 125 to 251 cm (note that the underlying Sanger alluvium was not encountered through trenching so the true thickness of the Pilot Point at 41DL553 and 41DL554 is not known). In contrast to the Recent alluvium, there is little textural variation laterally or vertically in the Pilot Point alluvium. In the bottom 50 cm of the Trench 40 profile, sand percentages range from 25.2 to 33.6 percent and by far represent the highest sand percentages measured for the unit across the four sediment/soil sample columns. There is also a slight increase in sand content between 120 and 160 cmbs (elevations 129.23 to 128.83 m) in the Trench 42 profile. The high MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 107

## Page 120

sand content at the bottom of the Trench 40 probably represents the top of a channel fill sand suggesting that the swale is indeed an abandoned, nearly-filled channel of the West Fork Trinity as opposed to a more recently-formed flood chute. The increase in sand percentages in the middle of the Trench 42 profile are probably the product of the abandon channel acting as an overflow channel during a flood event. Aside from those two examples, the Pilot Point alluvium predominately has a sand content of less than 10 percent with much of the unit being classified as a silty clay. Excluding the higher sand content samples mentioned above, mean particle sizes for samples range from 6.7 to 8.1 phi. The same samples produce unit means of 7.3 to 7.5 phi across the four sediment/soil sample columns. These particle size data suggest that the observed Pilot Point alluvium at 41DL553 and 41DL554 is comprised of flood basin facies, except for the possible minor channel fill facies observed at the bottom of Trench 40. The most salient feature of the Pilot Point alluvium unit is the West Fork paleosol that has formed on it. Ferring (1997, 2000) and Hall et al. (2012) define the West Fork as a cumulic Mollisol, but at 41DL553 and 41DL554 it is clearly expressed as a Vertisol. Vertisols are clay soils that exhibit remarkable changes in volume due to shrinking and swelling induced by fluctuations in soil moisture (Coulombe et al. 2000; Kovda and Wilding 2004; Soil Survey Staff 1999). The process of shrinking and swelling has been attributed to the interlayer expansion and collapse of the crystallographic structure of smectite clays when wetted and dried. Over time, the volumetric changes and movement create soil features and structures that are unique to Vertisols. Despite being buried, a gilgai micro-topography with subsurface bowls below the micro-lows are discernable across laterally broad exposures of the paleosol (Figure 5.46), though these features were not observed in all trenches. The paleosol exhibits A-Bkss or A-Bss-Bkss profiles. The A Figure 5.46: Buried gilgai micro-topography and other vertisolic features of the West Fork paleosol in Trench 50 at 41DL554. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 108


![Image 1 - Page 120](assets/00 Goodmaster et al 2021 - MacArthur Reclamation 41DL553 and 41DL554 Phase II/00 Goodmaster et al 2021 - MacArthur Reclamation 41DL553 and 41DL554 Phase II_p120_img1.png)

## Page 121

horizons are generally overthickened and consist of very dark gray (10YR 3/1 and 2.5Y 3/1) and black (10YR 2/1 and 2.5Y 2.5/1) clays and silty clays. The B horizons typically consist of very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2), dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2), and very dark gray (10YR 3/1 and 2.5Y 3/1) silty clays that can be divided into multiple subhorizons largely based on color variations that increase in value and chroma with depth. Vertic features such as wedge-shaped aggregates or peds with silt coats, grooved slickensides, and glossy pressure faces on the ped surfaces are present in the A horizon but are more common in the B horizon, which warrants a designation of Bss, or Bkss where pedogenic carbonates are also present. Pedogenic carbonates are generally expressed as nodules and soft masses 1 to 5 mm in diameter. Major shear failure surfaces or large slickensides delineate the margins of subsurface bowls below the micro-lows (Williams et al. 1996:126). The vertic features in the soil profiles of Trenches 40 and 42 do not extend to the depths that they do in the soil profiles of Trenches 47, 49, and 50, which may be related to textural and facies changes with depth in the former. There, the deeper parts of the B horizons, while lacking vertic features, display common accumulations of pedogenic carbonate and thus warrant a Bk horizon designation. The Bk horizons are generally dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) to brown (10YR 5/3) loams and silty clays. They exhibit a subangular blocky structure or a weak prismatic structure that parts to a subangular blocky structure with secondary carbonates accumulations expressed as filaments and soft masses. Measurements and Trends of Organic Carbon, Calcium Carbonate, and Magnetic Susceptibility Organic enrichment, as reflected in organic carbon percentages from the sediment/soil sample columns, generally exhibit decreasing values with depth from the ground surface through the Recent alluvium (see Figures 5.41 to 5.44). Organic carbon percentages peak again at the top of the West Fork paleosol and decrease in value with depth in the Pilot Point alluvium. This mimics the overall trend of melanization decreasing with depth in the paleosol as observed in the field. One notable exception to this trend in the West Fork paleosol is a spike in organic carbon at 160 cmbs (elevation 128.83 m) in the Trench 42 sample column at 41DL553 (see Figure 5.42). This peak in organic carbon closely matches a peak in low-field magnetic susceptibility at 170 cmbs (elevation 128.73 m). It also matches a sharp decrease in δ13C values at 160 cmbs (elevation 128.83 m). All in turn coincide with the presence of scattered pieces of wood (C 3 plant) charcoal observed between 150 and 170 cmbs (elevations 128.93 to 128.73 m) within the profile. The charcoal fragments suggest that the increases in organic carbon and magnetic susceptibility values, along with an abrupt decrease in δ13C values are probably due to burning. To note, wood ash tends to have large magnetic susceptibility values (see McClean and Kean 1993). Whether the burning is natural or anthropogenic is not clear, but overall, it suggests the organic carbon and magnetic susceptibility spikes, and sharp decrease in δ13C values are not pedogenic in origin and possibly not a reflection of natural vegetation changes either. Generally, with a few exceptions, the highest percentages of calcium carbonate content occur in the Recent alluvium. For the unit as a whole, carbonate content averages 24.1 percent (ranging from 16.9 to 30.9 percent), reflecting the calcareous nature of the parent material and the largely unmodified nature of the sediments (see Figures 5.41 to 5.44). The carbonate content within the modified Pilot Point alluvium averages 20.5 percent (ranging from 9.2 to 43.0 percent), and generally increases with depth, reflecting an accumulation of carbonate at depth that is pedogenic in origin. Decreases in carbonate content appear to coincide with increases in sand percentages, suggesting that the sand population is comprised mostly of mineral constituents other than carbonate clasts. This also suggests that detrital silt-size or smaller carbonate grains are the source of the pedogenic carbonate expressed in the West Fork soil profiles. Overall, the organic carbon and calcium carbonate profile trends are in accord with a soil stratigraphy consisting of a recent surficial geologic unit overlying a pedogenically-modified geologic unit. In contrast, while the low-field magnetic susceptibility data highlights the soils at the top of the Recent and Pilot Point alluviums, they also exhibit some unexpected trends and erratic shifts well below the A horizon of the West Fork paleosol where one typically expects to see a gradual trend of decreasing X values with depth in lf modified, discrete depositional units (see Figures 5.41 to 5.44). The Trench 47 magnetic susceptibility MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 109


---

## Page 122

profile may be the least peculiar of the four sample columns. It consists of a narrow range of low X values lf (ranging from 9.3 to 13.5, x̅ = 11, σ [sigma] = 1.3) that subtly decrease with depth and then subtly increase towards the bottom of the profile. The Trench 49 profile consists of X values that are slightly higher and lf greater in range (ranging from 9.9 to 21.5, x̅ = 12.8, σ = 3.0). The trend line for these values exhibits a gradual increase with depth. In comparison, the X values are much higher for the Trench 40 and 42 lf magnetic susceptibility profiles than in those of Trenches 47 and 49, with sample means of 24.0 and 24.3, respectively. In addition, the magnetic susceptibility profiles for Trenches 40 and 42 are widely erratic, exhibiting multiple back-and-forth shifts over an approximately 2-m vertical portion of the stratigraphic profile. Both profiles display magnetic susceptibility peaks in values that are greater or nearly greater than the X values measured at or near the top of the paleosol. Only one of those magnetic susceptibility peaks lf is associated with a peak in organic carbon (in Trench 42), but as noted above, that peak is unlikely registering a weathering surface. Two additional peaks, at 110 cmbs (elevation 129.22 m) and 190 cmbs (elevation 128.42 m) in the Trench 40 profile, are associated with a small FCR fragment and bone fragments, and a zone of elevated macro- and micro-debitage counts in the vertical profile, respectively, so it is possible that those two peaks have anthropogenic origins, namely stemming from the controlled use of fire at the site. The remaining peaks seemingly defy explanation as they do not coincide with any obvious stratigraphic/pedogenic, chemical, or archeological evidence. Despite this, a few possible explanations for these peaks are discussed below, though none are entirely convincing. Because the West Fork paleosol at 41DL553 and 41DL554 is a Vertisol, it was thought that pedoturbation may be responsible for the peculiar magnetic susceptibility profiles in Trenches 40 and 42. This possible explanation however is less than satisfying given that the same back-and-forth shifts in magnetic susceptibility are not present in the sediment/soil sample columns of Trenches 47 and 49, which host the same Vertisol. Regardless, literature on magnetic susceptibility studies on Vertisols was briefly reviewed to see if pedoturbation may be factor. This review revealed that Vertisols generally display magnetic susceptibility profiles of decreasing values with depth (Jordanova and Jordanova 2016; Lepre 2019; Lindquist et al. 2011) within discrete depositional units subject to a single episode of pedogenesis like the Pilot Point alluvium/West Fork paleosol. Perhaps a more productive way to interpret at the back-and-forth shifts in the magnetic susceptibility profiles of Trenches 40 and 42 is to view them as a product of a cyclical or periodic process. With that in mind, one of the more obvious differences between the magnetic susceptibility profiles of Trenches 40 and 42 and Trenches 47 and 49, besides the X values and trends themselves, is the location of the trenches. lf Trenches 40 and 42 are located along the margin of the swale that traverses the floodplain. As a topographic low on the floodplain surface, the swale services as a sediment trap and not just for overbank sediments, but also local sediments transported by surface flow after rainfall events if vegetation barriers are limited. Obviously, sedimentation through these two mechanisms would occur with greater frequency than through overbank flooding alone and may be frequent and periodic enough not to be obscured by pedogenesis in a magnetic susceptibility profile. This would explain the difference between the profiles in Trenches 47 and 49 and those of Trenches 40 and 42, as the West Fork paleosol in the former is solely subject to aggradation through overbank flooding and at a rate that was overridden by pedogenesis, while the latter are subject to aggradation from overbank flooding and inputs of more local sediment through surface flow. Perhaps an even more satisfying explanation comes from the addition of range fires to the issue, which might also explain the much higher X values within the Trench 40 and 42 profiles. The swale is lf undoubtedly subject to cycles of soil saturation through ponding and soil desiccation due to drought and seasonal changes in moisture. This cycle promotes the growth of a voluminous amount of vegetation which subsequently becomes a large amount of fuel for intense wildfires during drought conditions. Fire is an important element of the tallgrass Blackland Prairie ecology (Griffith et al. 2007:61) and has been shown to produce surfaces layers enriched with magnetite and enhanced magnetic susceptibility (Oldfield and Crowther 2007; Oldfield et al. 1981). Fires sweeping across the floodplain would burn with greater intensity in the swale due to the greater fuel load, an intensity that should register in a magnetic MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 110


---

## Page 123

susceptibility profile with some degree of periodicity. As a trap for detritus, the swale could also concentrate wood ash and iron oxides left over on the surface from fires and introduced by surface flow that has enhanced transport capabilities due to the surrounding burned and barren landscape. In either scenario the products of burning become buried, the vegetation of the swale and the surrounding landscape recovers, and the cycle starts again. Geochronology and Paleoenvironments Five radiocarbon ages obtained on charred botanical materials excavated from the Pilot Point alluvium/West Fork paleosol range from 2650±30 to 130±30 B.P. The charred seed sample producing the age of 130±30 B.P. (Beta 589203; see Table 5.13), however, is considered intrusive and not an accurate reflection of the age of its context given the possible historic or modern origin of the seed, coupled with the charcoal samples 30 to 50 cm above and 20 to 40 cm below yielded radiocarbon ages of 810±30 and 1850±30 B.P., respectively. Excluding that intrusive sample, the range of radiocarbon ages (2650±30 to 810±30 B.P.) when calibrated centers neatly on the date range (2400 to 700 B.P.) and the mean of 1531±159 years cal B.P. obtained by Frederick and McKee (2019:78–82) for a suite of radiocarbon ages from West Fork soil and paleosol contexts. The most recent radiocarbon age (810±30 B.P.) is from a sample obtained from the A horizon of the West Fork paleosol ca. 20 to 30 cm below the top of the soil, while the other radiocarbon ages are on samples collected from below the A horizon. The calibrated median probability for these radiocarbon ages arranged by depth yielded sedimentation rates of 11.9 to 16.1 cm per century from around 2,750 to 1,950/1,750 years ago. From ca. 1,950/1,750 to 700 years ago, sedimentation rates decreased to 4.6 to 6.7 cm per century. Sometime after 700 B.P., the West Fork Trinity River incised its floodplain. The decreased rate of sedimentation and channel entrenchment is obviously reflected in the formation of the West Fork soil on top of the Pilot Point alluvium. These rates of sedimentation and change over time are comparable to those obtained by Frederick and McKee (2019:94) at the Mercado site (41TR203). There, sedimentation rates of 15.8 and 13.4 cm per century occurred between approximately 2700 and 2600 B.P., and ca. 2040 and 1880 B.P., respectively. By approximately 1880 to 1450 B.P. and ca. 1200 to 620 B.P., sedimentation rates dropped to 3.2 and 2.1 cm per century, respectively. No radiocarbon ages were obtained on the Recent alluvium at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554, though there is evidence to suggest this unit, at least the distal margins, is very recent in age. First, the channel margin deposits observed within the Recent alluvium in Trenches 47 and 50 are largely unmodified with sedimentary structures preserved throughout. Secondly, no prehistoric artifacts were encountered within it during trenching or controlled excavation of the four initial test units. In fact, the only cultural materials encountered or observed within the Recent alluvium were 20th-century artifacts (Table 5.20). ## Page 124

a few intrusive 20th-century artifacts had worked their way into the West Fork paleosol and the upper portion of the underlying Pilot Point alluvium. One of the more interesting items is a sickle bar mower tooth/guard that was recovered from the top of the paleosol in Trench 52 at site 41DL553 (Figure 5.47). Although corroded, it was identified as part of a McCormick-Deering No. 7 or No. 9 mower that most likely dates to the second quarter of the 20th century. Its context at the top of the paleosol suggests that much of the Recent alluvium (at least the distal margins) was deposited during the 20th century, which in turn suggests that much of the West Fork paleosol was in fact exposed across the floodplain as a surface soil until sometime in the early 20th century. This might explain why the buried gilgai micro-topography that was observed in Trench 50 at site 41DL554 was not observed in trenches farther from the channel, as those features can be obliterated through land clearing and cultivation (Williams et al. 1996:126), which is known to have occurred based on mid-20th century aerial photographs of the area (see Figures 2.5 and 2.6). Thus, at 41DL553 and 41DL554, the West Fork was probably expressed as a surface soil and a paleosol depending on its location relative to the channel until it was completely buried during the 20th century by the deposition of the Recent alluvium. This also explains how a few 20th-century artifacts were recovered from the upper portion of the West Fork. Their original archeological context was surficial, but through soil cracking and/or cultivation were worked into the upper part of the West Fork soil. Figure 5.47: Intrusive 20th-century artifact from the West Fork paleosol at 41DL553; (A) late 20th-century mower blade guard (Trench 52; 50-60 cmbs) and (B) bullet (TU 1, 74 cmbs). Given the possible recent burial of the West Fork paleosol at 41DL553 and 41DL554 and the radiocarbon ages obtained on materials from within it, the West Fork paleosol can be viewed as an archive of paleoenvironmental information covering the last ca. 2,350 to 2,750 years. Some of this information was obtained through a stable carbon isotope analysis of soil organic matter. That analysis yielded δ13C values ranging from -19.1 to -22.1‰ across the four sediment/soil sample columns (see Figures 5.41 to 5.44). This range of values represents plant communities with 35 to 56 percent C plant compositions. The mean 4 value for all four sample columns is -20.6‰ with the variance around this mean being 0.7‰. At the Mercado site (41TR203), δ13C values from the West Fork paleosol revealed a slight increase in organic carbon from C vegetation starting at around 2700 B.P. and peaking at a C plant contribution of 4 4 nearly 50 percent at around 1,900 years ago, after which the trend shifts to a decreasing contribution in C 4 MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 112


![Image 1 - Page 124](assets/00 Goodmaster et al 2021 - MacArthur Reclamation 41DL553 and 41DL554 Phase II/00 Goodmaster et al 2021 - MacArthur Reclamation 41DL553 and 41DL554 Phase II_p124_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 124*


---

## Page 125

vegetation with minimum values of 20 to 25 percent at the top of the soil (Frederick and McKee 2019:116). A nearly identical trend in carbon isotope signature is present at site 41TR170 (Hall et al. 2012), though the δ13C values from 41TR170 are on average 2.8‰ greater than those from the Mercado site and the shift to a smaller percentage of C plants at 41TR170 occurred about 900 years later and at a more rapid rate. 4 Regardless of those differences, the shift in δ13C values at the Mercado site (41TR203) and 41TR170 are interpreted as a change from more mesic C -dominated plant communities (e.g., tallgrass wet meadows on 4 floodplains) to drier floodplain environments dominated by trees (C plants; Frederick and McKee 2019; 3 Hall et al. 2012). While the timing of shifts and the duration of trends in the carbon isotope signatures at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 are best estimates due to the limited number of radiocarbon ages, all four sample columns starting at around 2,750 to 2,350 years ago depict a gradual decrease in δ13C values (an increase in C vegetation) 3 over several centuries. In Trenches 40 and 42, the increases in C vegetation shifts to increases in C plants 3 4 at around 1,900 to 1,600 and 2,200 to 2,000 years ago, respectively. This trend continues to about 700 years ago when δ13C values start to decrease again. In Trench 47, the decrease in δ13C values is slight and continues to near the top of the West Fork paleosol, where values sharply increase by 2‰. In Trench 49, the increase in C vegetation shifts to increases in C plants possibly around 2,300 to 2,100 years ago, after 3 4 which the δ13C values remain relatively stable through the upper part of the paleosol representing a plant community that is evenly comprised of C and C plants. Throughout the Recent alluvium in all sample 3 4 columns, there is an abrupt shift to greater δ13C values (average change of slightly more than 1.0‰) from those at the top of the West Fork paleosol. This is followed by a shift back to greater percentages of C 3 plants (woody or arboreal species moving into abandoned or fallow fields?) at the top of the unit. The stable carbon isotope signature trends at 41DL553 and 41DL554 are opposite of those at the Mercado site (41TR203) and 41TR170, though interestingly enough, the shifts in vegetation communities at the three sites occur around the same time, approximately 2,000 years ago, plus or minus a century or so. Frederick and McKee (2019:119–120) note differences among the stable carbon isotope records of the Mercado site (41TR203) and a few other sites in the upper Trinity basin and suggest that these differences could be the result of local factors, such as pedogenesis, geomorphic/topographic position within the basin, and inputs from nearby tributary streams, overriding the regional signature at some locales. Such local factors may be at play at 41DL553 and 41DL554, particular since the West Fork soil is expressed as a Vertisol. These clayey soils may differ from other soil orders in such characteristics as percolation rates and the ability to retain water and soil moisture, which in turn would influence the plant communities they support. Geoarchaeological Summary and Conclusions Sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 are situated on the floodplain of the West Fork Trinity River. Geoarcheological investigations at the sites revealed that the floodplain alluvium consists of two allostratigraphic units, the Recent and Pilot Point alluviums. The top of the Pilot Point alluvium exhibits a dark, clay-rich Vertisol, the West Fork paleosol, which encapsulates the prehistoric archeological assemblages of both sites. The occupation of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 coincided with the aggradation of the Pilot Point alluvium and the formation of the West Fork paleosol. More specifically, the occupants of 41DL554 resided on a portion of the floodplain that vertically-accreted largely through the overbank deposition of texturally fine particles that in aggregate are classified as silty clays and clays and weathered to form the West Fork soil. The inhabitants of 41DL553 occupied a portion of the floodplain that not only was comprised of fine-grained flood basin facies, but in certain parts of the site were also comprised of sandier channel fill facies. Occupation of 41DL553 clearly post-dated the accretion of this channel fill and the abandonment of the channel course as cultural materials are limited to the overlying flood basin facies. Despite that, the abandoned channel remained a minor topographic depression on the floodplain that periodically transported channelized flows during flood events and received local sediments from surface flow. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 113


---

## Page 126

Relatively artifact-dense zones at both sites occur below the A horizon of the West Fork paleosol, indicating that the most intense periods of occupations occurred while the Pilot Point alluvium was aggrading and the initial stages of pedogenesis were occurring. Sedimentation rates were around 11.9 to 16.1 cm per century between ca. 2,750 to 1,950/1,750 years ago. Contemporaneous with the vertical growth of the floodplain surface, the local vegetation became increasingly dominated by C plants until around 2,000 years ago, 3 when the vegetation underwent a change resulting in an increasing portion of the community being comprised of C plants. It is also around that time, or shortly thereafter, that aggradation of the floodplain 4 surface slowed as sedimentation rates decreased to 4.6 to 6.7 cm per century. These slow rates of sedimentation continued until around 700 years ago, after which the channel of the West Fork Trinity River incised its floodplain. This decrease in sedimentation and channel entrenchment are reflected in the formation of the most prominent feature of the soil stratigraphy at both sites, the West Fork paleosol. During that time of relative surface stability, use of the sites continued though the occupations were seemingly sporadic and less intense based on the number of artifacts left behind. The West Fork continued to form and was the soil mantling much of the floodplain surface until its complete burial by Recent alluvium within the last 100 years or so. From a geoarcheological perspective, a couple of factors are involved in determining whether the archeological components at 41DL553 and 41DL554 have contextual integrity and interpretable assemblages of artifacts and materials to render an accurate picture of life in the past. Site occupations that are contemporaneous with fine-grained sedimentation generally result in archeological sites with good contextual integrity. Artifacts and features left behind get buried and sealed off from disturbances and subsequent site occupants whose tools and materials might co-mingle with those of the former inhabitants, rendering the site assemblage less interpretable. Fine-grain sedimentation was clearly on going when both sites were occupied, in fact the more intense periods of occupation based on the number and diversity of artifacts recovered occurred while sedimentation rates were high, thus preventing, to a certain degree, the co-mingling of cultural materials of different ages as the sites were repeatedly occupied over time. Once cultural materials enter the archeological record they can be subject to disturbances (e.g., bioturbation, pedoturbation, erosion, etc.) that could affect their context and thus the quality of information they can yield. The cultural materials at 41DL553 and 41DL554 were encapsulated and recovered from the West Fork paleosol, which is expressed as a Vertisol. Vertisols are subject to shrinking and swelling based on changing soil moisture conditions, which results in soil movement and presumably the movement of objects within them. Vertisols are known to move objects, some up to 40 cm in size, throughout their matrix resulting in vertical displacements of up to 100 cm or greater (Moeyersons et al. 2006). Objects can either move down through the soil profile when desiccation cracks open or move up to the surface through peristaltic-like movements when desiccation cracks close (Miller et al. 2010; Moeyersons et al. 2006; Nyssen et al. 2002). Radiocarbon dating has shown that soil movement and mixing can occur along the micro-slope margins of the micro-highs of gilgai topography supporting the inversion model of Vertisol formation (Kodva et al. 2001). There is, however, a growing body of research that suggests there is little to no mixing by pedoturbation in Vertisols (Driese et al. 2013; Lepre 2019) suggesting that the inversion model does not adequately explain all Vertisol formation. Sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 provide evidence for both the movement of cultural materials within the soil matrix of the West Fork paleosol and evidence that artifacts have moved very little or not at all. While examining the profile of Trench 41 at 41DL553, a vertically-oriented flake was observed in a closed crack at 187 cmbs (162 cm below the top of the West Fork paleosol). In contrast, a comparison of the vertical distributions of macro-debitage (debitage recovered in 0.25-in screens) and micro-debitage (4-mm and 2- mm fractions of debitage recovered during flotation) show little difference in the frequency distributions of the size fractions, suggesting that the movement of artifacts up and down the profile within the West Fork paleosol is not pronounced, or that the vertic properties of the soil have affected the movement of all artifact sizes equally within the soil profile. Overall, it may be that the movement of artifacts is more prevalent in some areas of the West Fork paleosol, such as along the margins of subsurface bowls below the micro-lows MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 114


---

## Page 127

and the micro-slopes on the margins of the micro-highs of gilgai topography, and relatively less outside of those vertisolic features. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 115

CHAPTER 6: SUMMARY, RECOMMENDATIONS, AND CONCLUSIONS 6.1 Summary of Phase II Investigations IES archeologists conducted Phase II NRHP eligibility testing for two prehistoric sites (41DL553 and 41DL554) located within the floodplain of the West Fork Trinity River west of MacArthur Boulevard in the City of Grand Prairie, Dallas County, Texas. The fieldwork for this effort was conducted by IES from 28 September through 06 November 2020. IES executed the Phase II testing in accordance with Section 106 of the NHPA. During investigations at 41DL553, 11 Gradall trenches were excavated to fully delineate the site and to facilitate the manual excavation of eight 1-by-1-m test units. Nine Gradall trenches and eight 1-by-1-m test units were excavated at 41DL554. During these investigations, 14.4 m3 of sediment at 41DL553 and 12.8 m3 at 41DL554 were manually excavated, deflocculated, and water screened. In addition, soil column samples were collected from 41DL553 and 41DL554 that were deflocculated and processed via flotation for macrobotanical analysis. Through these methods, five chipped stone tools, 479 pieces of debitage, 4.9 kg of burned rock, and 451 faunal bone fragments were recovered from 41DL553. Similarly, seven chipped stone tools, 345 pieces of chipped stone debitage, 5.3 kg of burned rock, and 172 faunal bone fragments were recovered from 41DL554. Based on this Phase II evaluation of sites 41DL553 and 41DL554, a better understanding of the horizontal and vertical extents of the sites, their temporal associations, the characteristics of their cultural artifact and feature contents, site formation processes, integrity, and data potential has been investigated. While there are broad similarities between sites 41DL553 and 41DL554, the variations between the sites likely indicate slight differences in site functions and different lengths of occupation. Site 41DL553 likely served as a small seasonal base camp. Site 41DL554 was likely a special purpose task site ancillary to another site, such as 41DL553, or a very short-term field camp for a traveling task group. During these investigations, one prehistoric cultural feature was identified at 41DL553, and two prehistoric cultural features were identified at 41DL554. Incidentally, of the few cultural features identified during the investigation of these sites, only one (41DL553 Feature 3) was encountered partially within a controlled excavation unit. Cultural features at these sites are small, sparse, and widely scattered, both horizontally and vertically, owning to the ephemeral nature and sporadic occupation of these sites over an approximately 1,800-year period, spanning portions of the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods. As with many prehistoric sites within the floodplains of the Upper Trinity River basin, temporally and stylistically diagnostic artifacts at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 are sparse. The patterns of chipped lithic debitage at the sites are highly variable throughout the site deposits, both horizontally and vertically, due to the short-term and intermittent nature of the site occupations over time. At both sites, the reliance on locally-available Ogallala gravels as the primary raw material for chipped stone tools is evident. This fits well with the current understanding of the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods within the Upper Trinity River basin as local population numbers were relatively high and groups often inhabited restricted territories (McGregor and Bruseth 1987). Despite a relatively minor Late Prehistoric period occupation at 41DL553, and the recovery of an arrow point at the site, no evidence of ceramic vessel use was identified at either site. Although both sites contain a predominance of deer, large bovid (likely bison), and turtle, differences exist between the two sites in both the abundance and variety of other faunal taxa present at the sites. While 41DL554 contained almost entirely deer, bovid, and turtle, 41DL553 contained birds, rabbits, and rodents in addition to deer, bovid, and turtle. In addition, 41DL553 contained almost three times as much faunal material as 41DL554 (n=452 and n=171, respectively). Those differences likely result from the distinct functions of the two sites. The larger abundance and diversity of taxa from 41DL553 suggest it was perhaps a longer-term or more frequently utilized site. Smaller taxa were procured from the immediate environment to supplement larger prey species and overall abundance is in keeping with longer period of use, but seasonality data suggests only late winter to spring temporal occupation, not year-round habitation. The MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 117


---

## Page 130

lack of species diversity and limited faunal material recovered from 41DL554 indicates it was a limited use site with only brief occupation. Seasonality data suggest 41DL554 was used during a similar season as 41DL553 and occupation of the two sites could be connected. There was a relatively significant difference between the two sites with respect to bone modification. Most notably, 41DL554 only contained evidence of percussion impacts while the assemblage from 41DL553 exhibited cut marks and chop marks in addition to percussion impacts. The cut marks from 41DL554 were all identified on undifferentiated deer/pronghorn bones and are located near major tendons and muscle attachments, suggestive of secondary processing and defleshing of the carcasses. Both sites contain evidence for possible decorative, musical, and/or ritual use of bone beyond subsistence or tool manufacture. 41DL553 contained an incised raptor talon suggesting intentional removal, likely for use as a bead or other form of adornment. 41DL554 contained turtle shell fragments which exhibit peripheral scoring consistent with turtle shell rattles. Weathering of faunal bone is relatively equitable across both sites and indicates relatively rapid deposition consistent with the location of the sites within the floodplain of the West Fork Trinity River. Both sites display a high amount of faunal bone fragmentation and very small bone fragment sizes. Site 41DL553 exhibits a slightly higher degree of fragmentation with no bones larger than 6 cm, while there was a smaller, more limited assemblage at 41DL554 with a few bones between 6 to 9 cm (n=4) and 9 to 12 cm (n=1) in size. Although carnivore modification is present at both sites, given the relationship of the size of the fragments with carnivore modification contrasted with the size of all bone fragments at the site, carnivores were not the primary cause for the overall highly fragmentary nature of both faunal assemblages. At both sites there is evidence that some of this fragmentation is associated with bone grease manufacture or other intensive human modification of the bone. Other variables, such as taxa, types of breaks, presence of percussion impacts, differential weathering ratings within provenience, and thermal alteration are consistent with evidence noted elsewhere (Jacobson 2021) for bone grease production within archeological contexts. Bone grease has use beyond subsistence, however, including as a base for paint, to soften hides and leather, within medicine, and other uses (Boyd and Derring 2013; Castaneda and Koenig 2021; Peres 2018). The fragmentary nature of the faunal remains and lower diversity than other nearby sites such as Fish Creek Slough (41DL436) indicates a low potential to provide new information on species use or subsistence strategies. The high degree of fragmentation has largely impaired the ability to identify and quantify most of the remains beyond class and broad skeletal element. Although some of the breakage was certainly cultural, all taxa identified in the assemblage display a high amount of fragmentation suggesting that some of the breakage exhibited by the assemblage was due to non-cultural taphonomic processes. This is evidenced by the increased fragmentation and weathering of bone within the upper meter of the matrix, and evidence for carnivore breakage and even carnivore consumption of some of the bone. Potential for processing techniques, such as bone grease production and localized heating of the bone to facilitate breakage, were identified at site 41DL553 and support similar observations on faunal assemblages from other archeological sites within the region. However, further investigation of the site is unlikely to yield significant insights into these subsistence and food-processing strategies other than the fact that they were occurring at the site. Analysis of the faunal assemblage at these sites did provide evidence for potential social, ritual, or ceremonial use of turtle shell and raptor talon adornments. While information regarding those lifeways is minimal from pre-contact archeological contexts in Texas, the evidence from 41DL553 and 41DL554 is extremely limited. Extensive flotation of site matrix and feature fill at sites 41DL553 and 41DL554 illustrate additional similarities and differences at these sites. Although limited, site 41DL553 yielded evidence of acorn and onion consumption, and hinted at the possibility of the limited presence of corn at the site. The site also illustrated use of a variety of floodplain tree species. Site 41DL554 also yielded limited evidence of onion consumption, the only additional botanical evidence identified at the site related to the use of oak as the preferred fuel wood. Analysis of the recovered macrobotanical materials also illustrated the presence of multiple modern seed taxa throughout the site matrix to depths of 230 cmbs, including a charred grass seed MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 118


---

## Page 131

(recovered from a depth of 90–100 cmbs in TU 7 at 41DL553) that returned a modern to recent historic radiocarbon date. The efficacy of the soil flotation process was also evaluated during this investigation. Despite extensive deflocculation of the clay-rich floodplain soils, the recovery of charcoal in flotation light fractions ranged from 0 to 25 percent. For the site 41DL553 flotation samples, the overall light fraction recovery rate was 22 percent. For site 41DL554, the overall light fraction recovery rate was 3 percent. The challenges of obtaining meaningful data from these sites extended to radiocarbon dating the charred botanical remains recovered during manual excavation and through the flotation process. Multiple charcoal samples submitted for radiocarbon dating failed to produce a sufficient carbon sample for dating during the pretreatment procedure due to very high concentrations of secondary soil carbonate within the charcoal matrices. Overall, the recovery totals for certain artifact classes at both 41DL553 and 41DL554 is higher compared to other site testing efforts at sites like Mercado (41TR203) and Fish Creek Slough (41DL436) within the upper Trinity River valley. However, this is a result of variations in methodological approaches, specifically the intensive soil deflocculation and water screening that was implemented during this investigation, which resulted in a much higher artifact recovery rate than conventional dry screening methods used at other North-Central Texas sites. Similarly, it can be inferred that the higher artifact totals do not represent a more significant archeological assemblage than more commonly observed but are more related to a clear data sampling bias between site testing methods that have been utilized in the region. Overall, 41DL553 and 41DL554 lack stratigraphic integrity, clear temporally-associated stratigraphy, and diversity in artifact assemblage composition. Moreover, the low frequency, small sizes, and minimal complexity of features encountered, with compromised depositional integrity, significantly limits data potential and the ability to formulate and answer research-oriented questions about the site and to offer new insights about prehistoric lifeways in the region. In addition, the sites appear to offer an information potential that is redundant with the previous investigations at these sites and with information derived from other, more intensively investigated sites in the region. 6.2 NRHP Recommendations In light of the information and data gathered during the Phase II evaluation of these sites, the research domains identified previously may be revised and updated based on the results of this investigation (Table 5.20). As previously summarized, the data potential for information regarding site chronology and integrity, a research domain necessary for site significance, is low to moderate based on the low recovery rates of temporally diagnostic artifacts, the moderate efficacy of radiocarbon dating, and the variable indications of contextual integrity. The highly variable internal organization of the sites, owning largely to their ephemeral and intermittent use over long time periods, diminishes the utility of the sites in understanding settlement systems. The functions of these sites within the regional settlement system has largely been established through this investigation, leaving little research potential to necessitate additional investigations. A moderate amount of subsistence information was generated during the faunal and botanical analyses, which articulated well with similar recent research at other sites within the region. Regarding material culture and technological change, these sites offer a low potential to provide new insights about prehistoric architecture and the cultural features encountered during this investigation were small, sparse, and often reflected diminished integrity. The sites have provided no indication of the presence of human remains, or that human burials are likely to exist at the sites based on their apparent short-term use. Although advantageous from a regulatory perspective, the potential of the sites to provide paleodemographic or mortuary information is therefore low. No exotic, extra-local, or trade goods have been identified at either site. All of the chipped lithic raw materials recovered from each site are likely to have been derived from local sources. Therefore, these sites are unlikely to provide data or information regarding trade and exchange among prehistoric Native American groups within and around the region. Each site has contributed information regarding the past environment and environmental change over time, based on the isotopic data generated during the geoarcheological analysis. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 119

Paleoenvironmental Reconstruction Macrobotanical remains Probable Moderate Isotopic data Probable High As detailed in Chapter 4, the research design for this project specified the criteria by which the sites would be evaluated for NRHP eligibility. To be considered eligible for inclusion in the NRHP, 41DL553 and 41DL554 must each have 1) contextual integrity (either horizontal and/or vertical) of one or more cultural components and 2) datable organic material that can be linked to vertical strata within the soil column, in addition to either 3) discrete cultural features and/or 4) a diverse artifact assemblage that can be analyzed and interpreted to yield significant data that will contribute important new information to substantially enhance the understanding of one or more of the research domains relevant to the regional prehistoric context. When evaluating NRHP eligibility for each site, a site that was determined to be lacking in one or more of these areas were judged to have little or no potential to contribute to broader research questions and would therefore no yield information important in prehistory. Based on the data and analysis presented in this report, IES recommends that 41DL553 and 41DL554 are not eligible for listing on the NRHP under Criterion D as outlined in 36 CFR 60.4. ## Page 133

6.3 Conclusions IES recommends that 41DL553 and 41DL554 are not eligible for listing on the NRHP. As such, it is the recommendation that no historic properties will be affected by this undertaking per 36 CFR 800.4(d)(1) and that the undertaking be allowed to continue without the need for further archeological investigations. However, if any unanticipated discoveries are encountered during construction, specifically if human remains are encountered, construction activities should stop immediately in the vicinity of the inadvertent discovery. Both law enforcement and the USACE must be notified per Chapter 711 of the Texas Health and Safety Code (including Section 711.011). As per the Native American Graves Protection and Repatriation Act (NAGPRA) and the Texas Natural Resource Code of 1977, Title 9, Chapter 191, the USACE and appropriate tribal organizations must be consulted prior to continuing construction efforts. MacArthur Reclamation Project (SWF-2019-00097) IES Project No. 04.338.002 Phase II Archeological Evaluation Report Page 121
